THE RED PAGE.
ON*E S opinion of Le Gallienne’s ne
1 Omar’ is considerably bettered 1
perusal of the whole. Doubtlee
Fitzgerald’s version remains supreme
there is a masculine strength in it whic
Le Gallienne cannot reach ; but it is no
therefore necessary to say, with Lone
‘ Times,’that ‘there is only one thing fo
Mr. Le Gallienne to do. Let him tur
his.attention to Malory’s “Morte'd’ Arthur
and re-write “The Idylls of the King.’’
One can gather flowers from every bush
and many of Le Gallienne’s quatrains are
welcome addition to the Omarian stock
And he pays very agreeable court to th
Girl who helped to make the Wilderness
Paradise enow—Fitzgerald rather neglectet
her. borne touches and embraces—
Mine is a passion that can never change,
It is so sorrowful and sweet and strange
That even from the very nightingale
I must conceal it—’tis so very strange.
For lo 1 I love a woman this strange way s
To he as dead without her, yet to stay,
A stubborn exile from felicity,
Far from her side until the Judgment Day.
O Love, why say so oft— ‘ the world ! the world !
Have we not put it by—the world ! the world !
That cruel thief of all our dearest joys,
Hath it not all but murdered us—the world !
Who brought thee last night lovely to my side f
Who drew thy warm veil cunningly aside ?
Who snatched thee back again so soon, so soon f
Who set this hell-fire burning in my side ?
Dike a dead man within thine arms I lay,
Entranced beyond the bounds of night and day—
-0 cruel breath of the dissevering dawn
That bids me fly who would for ever stay J
O Heart, my heart, the world is weary-wise,
My / only resting-place is your dear eyes,
O wrap me warm in their illusive love,—
For well I know that they are also lies.
Dong have I sought, but seldom found a lover ;
To love aright is to be nought but lover;
He who would love, yet eat and rest him too t
Is still an animal, and not a lover.
For love is a great sleepless, foodless fire ;
Lave never moves his eyes from his desire /
Were love to sleep — awaking, love were gone;
And what gross sustenance should love require t
1 Where are the fair old faces gone a-hiding ?
Where is the far-off place of their abiding T ’
I asked the wise, and thus the wise to me:
‘Drink, they are gone—and there is never a tid-
ing.’
A ight with a sudden splendour opens unde
Her purple robe, and bares her silver side ;
The moon, her bosom, fills the world with light, —
Only thy breast is lovelier, my bride.
With twilight dew each rose’s face is wet,
Morning was grey upon them when we met,
Stitt, must I drink, and still must drink with
thee, —
’Tis many laughing hours to bedtime yet.
How wonderfully has the day gone by !
If.only when the stars come we could die,
And morning find us gathered to our dreams, —
Two happy solemn faces and the sky !
A learned writer in ‘ The Academy ’ places
in order of merit Fitzgerald’s version, then
Whinfleld’s, then Le Gallienne’s ; but each
has its best points. A new translation, too,
is, promised by John Payne, to imitate
more closely the original and very various
Persian rhythm. A verse or two published
in ‘The Corn hill ’ maga., some years ago,
will give an idea how that rhythm meanders :
Lord ! to my heart trepanned, be merciful !
Lord ! to my breast grief-spanned, be merciful!
—Pity, oh Lord, this tavern-haunting foot !
Tc this goblet snatching hand, be merciful !
Or—
Be gay ! for the world will onward plod in time
to come ; *
The soul still cry for its fleshy pod, in time to
come ;
This skull thou seest so sprightly will be lying
Under the foot of the potter trod, in time to come !
By pondering such literal versions one gets
an idea of the true might of Fitzgerald’s
magic.
Arthur Symons’s ‘ Studies in Two Litera-
tures are the work of a young writer, who is
not yet quite sure of his prose style or his
critical judgment. The varying style is for
tue moat part imitated from Pater ai
otevenson : the judgment, where one ca
toilow, does not always seem the true thin
well said. And the papers tend to scrapp: -
ness. The note on James Thomson f
example, gives little help ; and in insisten
on the husk of phrase the essentials of th
man and his message are slurred. Bu;
Stevenson is correctly seen : “in comparin
him with the great names of literature w
cannot but feel all the difference, and all th
meaning of the difference, between a grea ;
intellect and a bright intelligence. . .
Stevenson remains charming : his per
sonality, individual and exquisite as it was
had not the strength and depth of great
ness.
* • • •
Some of Symons’s ‘ Studies in the Eliza
bethan Drama ’ —that on Massinger, for ex-
ample-have been made with pains, to profit
and, as S. is first and foremost a poet, hi
rhetoric often rings agreeably as well a
truly. E.g. —
Not the highest art only, bub all art, if it is s
much as to come into existence, must be emo-
tional ; for it is only emotion which puts life int
the death-like slumber of words, of stones, of th
figures on a clef. But emotion may take anr
shape, may inform the least likely of substances
Is not all music a kind of divine mathematics, anc
is not mathematics itself a rapture to the true
adept ? To Patmore abstract things were an
emotion, became indeed the highest emotion o
which he was capable; and that joy, which h
notes as the mark of fine art, that peace, which t
him was the sign of great art, themselves th
most final of the emotions, interpenetrated fo
him the whole substance of thought, aspiration
even argument. Never were arguments at once
■o metaphysical and so mystical, so precise
analytic, and passionate as those ‘high argu
ments ’ which fill these pages [of ‘ Religio Poetse v
with so thrilling a life.
• * • •
This is kin with the local idea that the
essence of Art is its dynamic, compelling
quality ; that this quality represents actual
life which the artist throws from himself
into his work ; and that if he wants his work
to carry far he has to make effort to give
impulse—just as a speaker who wants his
voice to carry far has, as it were, to take it
with his whole strength and fling it at the
audience. And again, comparing Maupas-
sant, Symons says—
A tale by Mr. Kipling is merely an anecdote;
an anecdote of the most vivid kind, but nothing
more. What is lacking? Just that which seems
to count for so little, and whieh really counts for
so much : the moral idea. With Maupassant, the
moral idea is always thore, at the root of what
may seem a mere anecdote [Not always; some of
the Norman sketches are purely objective and
detached]; it is there, permeating the whole sub-
stance of the story, giving it its vitality, and its
place in the organism of nature. Every story is
thus rounded, and becomes complete in itself by
becoming the part of a great whole.
Prefer, instead of “ the moral idea ” (which
Is mixed with baser associations), the uni-
versal application (asked for in Symons’s last
sentence). That —the all-human reference,
the accurate judgment, placement, of every
detail in due proportion and relation to the
widest conceivable synthesis—is the secret
of imperishable Art, which sees never a
flower without the tree, never a tree without
the landscape, never a landscape of the eye
without the illimitable landscape of the
creative imagination.

Writes George Pearce regarding Tambaroora in
N. Queensland :
Drinks being nearly everywhere 6d. each, a sweep of
* Is. in and the winner shouts ’ means the winner pays for
drinks all round and pockets the other half of the sweep.
This is a Tambaroora, and mast frequently occurs when
half-a-dozen or more acquaintances meet in the only
club-room of the North—the pub. parlor. It is the
least objectionable form of the ‘ shouting ’ custom. Say
a dozen persons meet thus, and one stands drinks all
round : it often means that all stay and drink twelve
‘shouts ’—result, more whisky i.n and more cash out than
some can carry and afford. The tambaroora obviates
this, and gives you a drink for Is., with a chance of 6s. in
pocket.
By the way, another curious result of ‘shouting * is the
contempt which a drinker-alone (or ‘ Jimmy Woodser
incurs. This, with sensitive people, involves asking the
bar-tender or publican to share your thirst, though you
never saw him before and never will again. One Oa
rington is known all through the North for the peci
Jiaritj of always insisting on paying for his own gla
wherever or whatever the company. I mention this at
showing how any deviation from * shouting ’ is at on
remarked.
And, on the same subject, “ Dipso ”
The essence ®f tambaroora is gambling and econom
combined. Say two mates go into a pub. 'We
they are prepared to liquor up. But, lo I they meet tw<
more coming in at the other door, and while g rectify,
are exchanged, two more enter, and again two more, ti
there are rather too many for one single purse to shou
for. The etiquette of conviviality, however, insists tha
all-these foregathering “ blithers’' must drink togethe
Apparently a Tambaroora (N.S.W. gold-rush) genius in
vented the “ shilling in and winner shouts ” —blessing
ever on his head ! Here is at once a difficulty solved and
a fair chance for a gamble. Each man was going to spent
at least a bob, so he willingly ris sit in the sweep. E
loses nothing “in his mind,’’ and if he wins the swee]
he gains 6d. per man! But tambaroora is not “
Queensland game.” It’s Australian.
The referenoe to ‘ tambaroora muster ’ should surel
be to a ‘tarpaulin muster,’ and that was when
drouthy crowd was ‘stony,’ or rather *n.s.f.,’ excep
perhaps one or two. Then the despairing cry of th(
breaking-up sma’ hours was, ‘ A tarpaulin muster, boys !
It would have been a mortal sin against the godlik
Thirst for the small Orcesus of the crowd to have declined.
Each man threw in his ‘all ’; from the Dives with eigh
teen-pence to the Lazarus with nixey. Then the crowd
consumed, as per results of collection ; and then, ah
Sorrow 1 ganged awa' drouthier than ever.
All casuists against shouting notwithstanding, a Jimm
Woodser ‘on principle ’ is a Bad Man—a soulless skunk
But, if you go into the bar alone, and find no mate there
it doesn’t follow that you need shout for the beer-puller
unless you know him as a decent fellow. Again, i
•morning’ is an exception to the anti-Jimmy Woodse
rule ; that is ‘medsin ’ —a pick-me-up.

-®- n Summer Isles,’ by Burton Dibbs (son oi
Sydney Commercial Bank manager T. B, Dibbs)
readably pictures in four stories the Samoa of to-
day, with its beach-girls, tourists, and mis-
sionaries. The writer has some dramatic instinct,
and a good deal of humor; but his book wants
literary quality—the material runs to waste in all
directions. The best yarn, “ The Psalm-Singer,”
is full of breezy, candid passages like this—
Far away in the New Hebrides lay the little cutter at
anchor, her black hull and tall mast reflected in the
mirror of the sea.
Under an awning reclined Rigley in a hammock;
Rutherford and Parsons lounged on the hatch.
‘ I wish I had this cargo in Samoa, * said Jack ; ‘ it would
be worth something there, but here it is simply an essen-
tial article of commerce which every French packet in the
group carries, and that with impunity. The idea of our
handing over the whole trade of the place to a handful of
Frenchmen simply beoause a lot of psalm-singing hypo-
crites make a fuss about our selling firearms and spirits
to the natives! Either stop the game altogether, as in
Samoa, or allow both nations to play it. Start fair, I say,
and then pluck and enterprise come out on top.*
A languid 1 Hear, hear! ’ came from Rigley in the
hammock.
‘ Now then, Billy, oome out of that,’ said Rutherford ;
' it's your watch, and you know you can't depend on
those cursed Kanakas to keep a look out.’
Billy growled something about stuff and nonsense, and
began leisurely to fill his pipe. Rutherford rose and
scanned the horizon. '
A thin column of smoke ascended almost perpendicu-
larly. A burst of profanity came from Jack. Billy was
out of the hammock in a moment, Eric was on his feet,
the sleepy Kanakas Jumped up, and the little deck was a
scene of bustle and excitement. The hatches were
ripped open, the boat was lowered, and long case after
case was handed over the side. Eric and Rutherford
worked like Trojans ; even Billy displayed a wonderful
amount of aotivity, and the first boat-load of arms was
soon on its way to the shore.
‘Good old Newcastle coal! ’ said Billy, as he wiped the
perspiration from his face ; ' you can see it coming.’
The column of smoke grew denser: the masts, the
yards, the yellow funnel, and finally the hull of the ap-
proaching steamer appeared, whilst boat-load after boat-
load of Sniders was conveyed to the shore.
‘Let us straighten up things a hit,’said Rutherford,
when the boat came back after her last load. ‘Have a
bucket of water or two over you, you fellows ; put on a
clean suit of pyjamas, and look cool.’
The man-of-war steamed slowly up to near where the
cutter was lying. She stopped, the engines went astern,
and the big unwieldy ship lay still. Her wash set the
empty cutter rolling.
The sun shone on the black hull of the man-of-war, the
paint blistered on her side, and her crowded crew gasped
for breath. Like most British men-of-war sent to the
tropics, she had evidently been designed for the Arctic
regions.
A boat was lowered, and a tallow-faced, unhealthy lot
of seamen manned the oars.
‘ Aw,’ said the officer in charge of the boat, ‘aw—what
is the—aw—name of this cutter?’
1 1 really, said Rigley, who had gone to receive him
and was sorely tempted to mimic him, ‘can’t pronounce
the name’—it was a long unpronounceable Fijian one—
‘ but if you will come on board I’ll write it out for you.’
The officer clambered on board.
‘ Where is the—aw— master ? ’ he asked.
‘The captain,’ corrected Rigley, pointing to Eric, 'it
there.’
‘Aw,’ said the officer, ‘have you any—aw—arms or
ammunition on board ?’
‘None, except our Winchesters,' said Eric.
‘I noticed—aw—that your boat was very—aw —active
as we were approaching' said the officer.
‘Yes, answered Eric, without a blush, ‘getting fuel
and water oh board : we are just going to make a start.’
‘Yes aw —yes,’ said the offioer, and, afler refusing
Erie s invitation to go below and have a look round, and
Billy s to have a oocktail, he got over the side into hi
boat.
And he was once a missionary ! ’ mused Ruth erf ore
as he pondered over Eric’s unblushing lie; ‘well, he's i
man now'.’
By Laurence Hmmnan—
You, the dear l rouble of my days,
W hen life shall let me cease,
Turn once ■ side from tinder ways
An 1 loo* upon my peace.
Let your feet rest upon my roof,
An l for the love we bore,
Forgive tire heart so far aloof ;
You cannot trouble more.
For if the dead man had his will,
Doubt not that be would rise
And waste his soul in passion still
With looking in your eyes.
So come when you have lost your power,
And pardon my release,
And set your feet to rest an hour,
A seal upon my peace.
Some little items :
H. G. Wells solves the problem of his ‘The War of the
Worlds ’in a perfectly characteristic w'ay. He has to kill
his Martians somehow, yet their heat-rays and poison-
fumes make them practically irresistible by humanity
So he recollected his science —and slaughtered them with
earthly microbes, which, like good watch-dogs, only bite
st.angers. The whole story is another very clever and
interesting tour deforce ; but, like Kipling’s ‘Captains
Courageous, it isn’t literature. Wells’s imagination pro-
duces effects as prodigious as Kipling’s observation and
description, but both are too objective to last. . . .
The fellow Book ’ seems to have died suddenly and
missed the usual newspaper obsequies: at all events, Syd-
ney booksellers don’t seem to have seen a number for nine
months. It was conventionalised, and isn’t much missed.
. . Dr. Walter Roth’s aboriginal book has won him a post
as medical assistant to Commissioner Parry-Okeden, in
charge of N.Q. aborigines. Thus the prophet is not
always, etc. . . . S3'dney School of Arts Reform Asso-
ciation should begin by trying to sell the white-elephant
building, wiping out the debt, and leaving the icome
free to improve library and reading-room. At present,
with some thousands of yearly income, all the institution
oan touch for really profitable purposes is £5OO or £6oo—
rest goes in up-keep and interest. Such a sale
would be desolating to a number of old fossil members
who practically live in the reading-room—come first thing
in the morning and go away last thing at night—but
these will presently learn to go to the Museum, their
proper place. As the S. of Arts is at present carried on,
the Govt, endowment has no justification whatever. ...
Viator: ‘Would like to say a word for “ Scribner’s Maga-
zine, most readable of the higher-class Americans, and
often uncommonly well illustrated. The editor seems to
have sense enough to buy live matter, instead of—with
Harper s and ‘ The Century ’ —for-the-naost-part re*
surreotingelegantcorpses belonging to people with Names.’
. . . Owing partly to W. E. Henley’s failing health, and
partly to his desire to leave, if possible, more than a
magazine-editor’s mark in literature, ‘ The New Review ’
has ceased publication. It was always readable, and paid
more attention to style than the other magazines. . .
Joseph Conrad’s • Nigger of the Narcissus ’ is a Loti-iike
vision of the sea, and contains some astonishing descrip-
tive work. But it remains too detached and impres-
sionistic-needs an epic idea to fill out its epic grandeur
of form. . . . Stead asked Labouchere what hymn had
helped him most! Labouchere! . . . Helen: ‘My
ohildren are in the habit of using “ betten’t ” in plaoe of
“had not better.” For example, “Betten’t Igo soon,
mother?” “You betten’t touch that, baby.” Short,
you see, and easily understood; and I have seriously
thought of adopting it myself—what do you think ?’—
You betten’t!

ANTHONY BARRY, Castle Inn,
ITindley-street, Adelaide, writes :
“ 1 will be glad if you will publish
the following for the benefit of suf-
ferers. For the last five years I
have been in the West, having
worked from Lake Darley to the

Nearly the whole of the time I have
been a sufferer from Debility,
and loss of energy, always moping
alone, afraid to join in any
conversation, noises in the ears, and other symp-

to myself and mates. Tried many advertised remedies
without obtaining any relief. On my way to New Guinea,
and hearing of so many from the West on their way to
other colonies breaking their journey at Adelaide to
obtain your treatment, I decided to do the same. lam
glad to say I now feel fit to continue my journey without
fear, and am sure I have only to take your remedies a
little longer to obtain a thorough cure. lam well known
in all parts of the West, and write this that my mates and
others may know where they can obtain a certain cure.”

An interesting difficulty has occurred
between the Broken Hill hospital and the Govern-
ment. The hospital derives most of its support
from weekly contributions made by mine work-
men, who, in virtue of these contributions (they
amount to in the £of wages), are guaranteed
admittance, provided beds are available. Govern-
ment now sticks at subsidising these payments,
on the ground that they are not subscriptions
“ within the meaning of the Act.”

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE REDEMPTION OF DIVES.
Hown in Hell lay Dives burning,
Given over to perdition,
Oft to Heaven his sad eyes turning,
Filled, too late, with vain contrition.
Abram on his throne was sitting,
All around him Heaven lay shining,
Dives’ hapless case unwitting ;
Lazarus on his breast reclining.
Carne a voice of sad complaining—
Father! let him dip his finger,
Lazarus—and not disdaining,
Slake the thirst in which I linger ! "
Son,” said Abram, “ thou enjoyed’sb
In thy life-time all thy pleasure ;
Every appetite thou cloyed’st;
Lazarus now shall have his measure.”
Father ! if I here must languish,”
Came again the pleading mournful,
Leave me lonely in mine anguish :
Save my brothers ! ” Abram, scornful: —
M They have Moses ! ” cried the father,
Let them hear the prophets’ teaching;
Vain it were to think that rather
They would hear a dead man’s preaching ! ”
One there was, who, not unheeding,
On the Great White Throne in glory,
Lent an ear to Dives’ pleading,
With compassion heard his story.
“ Dives damned ! ” He cried, “ and Dives,
In his torment, thinks of others ;
Hopeless, hopes where hope alive is;
Cries for mercy on his brothers ! ”
“ In a soul where love is living,
I live still, and T condemn not;
To the heartless, unforgiving
Prayers like Dives’, I contemn not ! ”
Then, swift wings sped down from Heaven,
While he lay with grief oppressed,
And, because he loved, forgiven,
Dives stood amongst the blessed.
A. NUGENT ROBERTSON.
THE MAN WHO HAS MEANS.
(As sung by himself.)
I’ve a trotter and trap trim and natty,
j I’ve a villa of brickified red,
I’ve a tile of dimensions terrific '
(To allow for the swell of my head)•
I’ve an affable smile for the ladies,
And a wink for the work-a-day queans,
And everyone knows by the cock of my nose
That I must be a Man who has Means.
They talk about Shakespeare and Byron,
They jabber of Learning and Law,
They stick up their silly old statues
To waggers of Parliament jaw ;
Why, most of these famous old buffers
Have hardly a fistful of beans,
And I haven’t a doubt I could buy ’em all out—
Por you s°e I’m a Man who has Means.
And then there’s the Party Artistic,
The fellows who fiddle and draw,
Those Poles who go banging pianos—
All thunder and thumping and paw.
Art? Well, if I want it I’ll hire ft,
I’ll pat for their Yellows and Greens ;
But let ’em sing low or I’ll soon let ’em know
How Great is a Man who has Means !
The Women ! They know me—God bless ’em !
They prize a man just at his worth.
I could just have my pick of the beauties,
So proud of their splendor and birth ;
But I’ll wag a free leg till I’m forty
Then wed a sweet thing in her teens,
For winning in marriage, like driving a carriage,
Is only a question of Means !
For Life (as I take it) is nothing
But a music-hall ringing with chimes,
With the whole human race a-performing
For him who has Dollars and Dimes ;
And Heaven—who wants any Heaven ?
While Earth with her gold intervenes
With her skittles and beer and her circus down
here
Which she runs for the Man who has Means !
Brisbane.
P. LUFTIG.
THE ROSE OF MEMORY.
O, brave grey head and pleasant face,
Gay heart and wholesome mind, —
What makes you turn upon the road
To cast a look behind ?
He said, “Inear the night apace,
The day draws to a close,
But, where my twentieth milestone lay,
I found a tender rose.
“ She scented all the air around,
She drooped a shy, sweet head ;
I gave a young love to my rose
Because my rose was red.”
“ But you are old —time-worn and old,
And age hath power to pain ” ;
He said, “ When darkness closes in,
I seek my rose again.
“ They merrily tread and merrily tread,
Nor feel the bitter wind
Who backward look from sunset hours
To see a rose behind.
“ But woe for them, and tears for them,
Who in a time of snows
Strain dim eyes down a barren past,
Nor see, behind, a rose. ”
RODERIO QUINN.

THE GRAVE OF GOVERNOR PHII.fF
The Revi,:F. B. Boyce received by the last m*i|
th» following letter from the Vicar of Bathamp.
■ ton,. Bath, the Rev. L. J. Fish, relative to tlj*
discovery of Governor Phillip’s grave. He says-
—"l read of you as being one of the pioneers t»
a proper appreciation of Phillip and his wonder,
tul work, and so I venture to write to you con-
ceming the discovery In our church of his grave.
The fact I made public In a letter to 'The Times'
of November 3. although the colony had official
Intimation of the fact first by wire, and then by
letters. I am now much concerned at the very
unworthy memorial of him which the present
grave presents to Australians and others who
have already begun to visit this church to see
where he lies.

He. had never concealed from himself the
thanks he owned to the labor party for the part
they had played. There would be good feeling-
.-between the two.parties as long as the Govern.-
Went honestly tried to carry out its pledges
when they failed to do that it was time for
everybody to fall out with them.
The freetrade party and the labor
party were at one in their belief in broad and
liberal principles, and it was the game of the
latter to get the Government to go faster than
they would otherwise go. The only difference
between the parties was that the labor party was
more prepared than the Other, to makp ran id
changes and rapid developments.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing .with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion — i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 22, 1898.
The Reformed N.S.W. Council.
A few days ago, in the course of that famous
political address at Mudgee, in which all his
figures were wrong, Premier Reid expounded
the details of his scheme for the reform of
New South Wales Legislative Council. Some
weeks ago he furnished the public with an
outline of his plan, and the plan (reviewed
in a Bulletin leading article of January 8
under the title of “A Reform Not Worth
Having ”) was chiefly remarkable because it
left the Council very nearly as bad as it is at
present. And now that the particulars are
filled in it appears, on Premier Reid’s own
showing, that his plan of renovation is many
degrees weaker and more worthless than it
originally appeared, and leaves the Council
in a worse state than ever. In fact it is so
absurdly and incredibly weak that, but for
the fact that all the Sydney papers give the
same account, it would seem almost beyond
belief that any man could design such a
thing. The following is the great democratic
reform for which New South Wales has been
waiting so long, as outlined by the great
reformer himself: —
S.M. Herald report: “ He was in favor of an Upper
House as a check upon legislation, but it must not have
power to deal with money bills, and at any rate a fifth of
the members must retire each fifth year.” Daily Tele-
graph report: “He would remove a fifth of the members
every fifth year, and replace them.”
* * * *
As a scheme of reform, this is the very
saddest wreck The Bulletin ever heard of.
If one-fifth of the councillors are to be re-
tired every fifth year, it necessarily implies
that the new class of temporary nominees,
who are to replace the old life nominees,
must be appointed for twenty-five years.
When the reformed Council gets into fair
working order it will take twenty-five years
before there is a complete change of per-
sonnel, and the member appointed this year
won’t fall due to be fired out till 1923. The
object is to keep new blood circulating in the
Stagnant Chamber—to waken it up by the
constant introduction of new men and new
ideas ; and it would do this so effectively that
if all the present councillors had been ap-
pointed under it the only result would be
the prompt fii'ing out of George Henry Cox.
He is absolutely the only individual in the
Legislative Council who, under Reid’s re-
formed system, would have finished his term
and be replaceable by a bright young Demo-
crat of the new school. The next oldest
fossil would not be removable for two years
yet, and another ancient would be due for
removal in 1902, and three more in 1905.
There are now 64 councillors, so, roughly
speaking, the great scheme of Democratic
renovation provides for the removal of 13
every five years, and the substitution, if
necessary, of younger and more up-to-date
politicians. Under the present arrangement
there are now in the Council 13 members
appointed in the last five years, and 17 ap-
pointed in the previous five years, so,
judged by the figures, the present system is
more reformed than the reformed principle
which Premier Reid would establish in its
stead. The great new Democratic idea is to
empower the Government to send up 26 new
nominees in ten years. The old, stupid
Tory idea created over 30 new nominees in
the last ten years, therefore, in its results,
the old system is wildly revolutionary com-
pared with the new one.
• • • •
Premier Reid’s new system involves
making the number of councillors fixed and
permanent. If it were applied now, the
Government could appoint no more progres-
sive Democrats to the Council until 1900
(the last batch of appointments being in
1895), except in the case of death vacan-
cies ; whereas, under the present order of
things, it can appoint a new batch any day.
If the people decided on an absolute change
of policy, and created a new Government and
a new Assembly with that end in view, and
the Legislative Council went solidly for the
old system, it could block the popular will,
under the proposed “reformed” constitu-
tion, for 15 years. Unless there was a very
great mortality among the old men, it would
not be till the third batch retired, at the end
of the third period of five years, that the
new Government could command a majority;
whereas, under the present system, it can
get a majority in the Council almost any
time, if it has a sufficiently strong popular-
mandate behind it. All that is necessary is
to get the formal sanction of the Imperial
figurehead to the necessary new appoint-
ments, and that can almost invariably
be done if the Government has a
big enough following, and makes a big
enough disturbance, and appeals with suffi-
cient vigor to the British authorities ; also,
it can often be done amicably if the Premier
introduces the Governor to a nice girl, or
otherwise makes himself socially agreeable.
Under the proposed “ reformed ” system the
Council couldn’t be swamped by new
nominees until a certain fixed date, and then
only in a very small way, and it could howl
derisively at the Government and the
Assembly and the huge popular majority,
and tell them all to wait five or ten or fifteen
years, according to the circumstances of the
case. And as no Government or Assembly
lasts that time the reformed Council, if it
had a decent majority to start with, could
damn everything and everybody more than
ten times as enthusiastically as it damns
everything and everybody now.
* # * *
The only reason why things might pan out
better than this is that the popular clamour
and the fear of being mobbed in the street
might, in any serious case, break down the
Council’s opposition long before the five or
ten or fifteen years, during which it could
legally hold the fort, had expired. But that
doesn’t make Reid’s proposed “ reform ” any
less infamously stupid. The idea is to create
a useful and intelligent second Chamber
which will be more in accoi’dance with the
national sentiment, and if, instead, there is
created one which will be bigoted, Tory, ob-
structive, useless, uttei'ly out of accord with
public feeling, from five to 25 years behind the
time, and far less amenable to popular pres-
sure than the unreformed Council now exist-
ing, the fact that its worst features may be
alleviated with bricks at a very bad crisis
makes the foolishness of its creators none
the less glaring. A Constitution which is so
created that one-half of the legislative
machine may have to go after the other half
with a club to prevent a fifteen-years’ dead-
lock is hopelessly rotten, whatever success
the party with the club may meet with.
* • * *
Even if it was proposed that one-fifth of
the Leg. Councillors should retire every
year, and thus the whole Council be renewed
every Jive, instead of every twenty-five,
years, the idea would still be very crude and
stupid and unworkable. Supposing that
party strife narrows down at any time to a
bitter conflict on any one issue—Protec-
tion v. Freetrade, Federation v. Provincial-
ism, Bimetallism against Monometallism, or
anything else. If one party remained in
office through two consecutive Parliaments,
it would have appointed the whole Upper
House, and would naturally have appointed
men who were of its way of thinking on the
great party question of the moment. Then
that party—call it the Protectionist party or
anything else—would probably go down
with a thud, and a strong Freetrade Govern-
ment would come in, and the province would
find itself with a strongly Freetrade Assembly
and a strongly Protectionist Council. Even
if the whole Council was renewed every five
years, one-fifth of the members retiring
annually, it would be three years before the
Council majority would be in accord with
the popular sentiment, and before anything
could be done to put the popular will in
effect. This is, no doubt, better than having
to wait fifteen years, as would be the case
under Premier Reid’s proposal; still three
years is the whole tenn of a Parliament, and
if the country had to wait three years for
everything very little would be effected.
* * * *
Premier Reid’s Council of 25 - year
nominees is, by his own account, to be a
“check upon legislation”—a thing for
obstructing and holding back and blocking
the way ; in other words, it is not to be a
machine to do useful work, but to prevent
the other House doing it. But it isn’t to be
a check upon money matters ; it is admitted
that a check is needed, but the finances,
which most want checking, are not to be
checked. At the same time, the new Coun-
cil, while too much of a rogue or a bigot
or a fool, or too unrepresentative and
undemocratic, to be trusted in any matter in-
volving the expenditure of Is. 6d. of the tax-
payer’s money, is to have vastly larger
powers—if it likes to use them—than the
Assembly in matters relating to the hanging
or imprisonment of the taxpayer. Its
powers will be larger because the M.L.A.
who is an outrage on popular sentiment can
only hold his office in defiance of the people
for three years at most, while the M.L.C. is
to hold his for any period up to twenty-five
years. The reason always assigned why the
Council should not have co-ordinate powers
with the Assembly over Is. 6d. of the public
money is that it doesn’t represent the
people, yet it is proposed to give it co-or-
dinate powers to execute the people, to make
tariffs for them, to make laws for stealing
the land from them, to flood them out
with cheap nigger-labor, to order them 15
years’ penal servitude and two floggings,
and other things, and to give it far more
than co-ordinate powers to block every re-
form the people desire. The idea of entrust-
ing such vast authority to an irresponsible
body which is admittedly so degraded, or so
incapable, or so unrepresentative, that it has
to be absolutely prohibited from touching
the public cash, is weird indeed.
* * * *
To sum up : No power whatever should be
entrusted, either for five years, or twenty-
five years, or five minutes, to any House
which doesn’t derive its authority from the
people, and which isn’t directly responsible
to the people. No House which can’t be en-
trusted with the public money is fit to be
trusted with the public life and liberty. It
is absurd to talk of any nominee House being
a “check” upon legislation; if it merely
agrees with what the people’s representa-
tives do then it isn’t any check : if it is a
check it can only be by disagreeing with
what the people’s representatives do—as
when it threw out the Probate Duties Bill,
which is just what all the row is about. The
whole objection to the present Council is
that it is a check upon legislation, and if the
Reid Government creates a new unpaid, ir-
responsible Council which can’t be swamped
as the present one can, it will be a still
worse check. The only way out is either to
have no Council at all, or to have one which
can be trusted with full powers over all
matters —one elected on the widest possible
suffrage by the whole country voting as one
constituency. A nominee chamber with no
power might as well be dead, and one with
any power at all is an outrage upon every
principle of Representative Government.
Berry’s Pension and Berry’s Wife.
Now that Graham Berry has settled down to
the quiet enjoyment of his annuity—such en-
joyment as a deeply-embarrassed old man can
extract from that very moderate sum—the
people of Victoria may possibly have time to
realise that they cut an inexpressibly mean figure
over the whole business. Graham Berry is very
old. He was a great man in his day ; at one
time he was probably the greatest and strongest
Democrat in Australia. Public opinion has
moved on a good deal since the era when Berry
was a really active volcano, and, to the people
who look back now, he may not seem so very
remarkable a Democrat after all. But every
man must be judged by his own period, and the
great Democratic leader is the one who is dis-
tinctly ahead of his period, and keeps there,
and who has the power to keep the people
moving after him. A hundred years later
another man may be far ahead of that high-
priest of Democracy, and yet be a bigoted Con-
servative and reactionary, simply because the
community hasmoved agreat deal in the interim.
The man who consistently strikes six when his
contemporaries are striking three is the one who
keeps the world moving, and the man who
strikes nine when the rest of the world is
striking twelve is only a drag and a stumbling-
block. The mere fact that nine is in itself a
good deal more than six alters the situation not
at all. And it must always be remembered that
if the first man had lived in the second man’s
period he would probably have struck fourteen,
simply because it was his nature to keep ahead,
and if the second man had lived in the first
man’s time he probably wouldn’t have struck
at all.
* * * *
The Victorian people know that Berry, at
his best, served them bravely, honestly, and
well, with a strong will and a powerful brain
and a generous purpose. Whatever the old,
burnt-out cinder of the Tribune may or may
not be, it must be remembered that no man
lasts for ever, either physically or mentally,
and if the worn-out Berry is not wholly a
Democrat the real Berry of twenty- ve y .
ago certainly was. Therefore, the ic
people decided that it was not well to e
Tribune be a penniless, pathetic wreck in
last days, and that, though there was no ung
to that effect in the mere letter of the bargain,
he had a moral claim upon them whic i was
beyond all dispute. They acknowledge! 11s
by giving him a pension ; that act settled tn
matter so far as it could be settled, H>e
tion of whether Berry has or has not a claim
on the Victorian people can’t now be re-opene( ,
the only authority which could give, a decision
on the matter has admitted his claim, and there
is no honorable way of going back on t a
decision. And, having decided that the old.
war-horse deserved a quiet pasture and a decen
water-supply in his last days, Victoria turne
him into the smallest paddock it could find, and
economised the grass to the best of its ability.
Berry’s annuity of <£soo a year cost the
country £.3100. If it had been paid out of the
Treasury instead of being bought from an assur-
ance office it would not have carried with it
such a painfully direct intimation that the
recipient is only expected to live for years
still that is a mere detail. But the harmless
little annuity dies with Berry —and the ancient
ex-Premier has a wife who will probably sur-
vive him.
* * * *
The law, or the code of Christian ethics, or
something or other which this community pro-
fesses to recognise, holds that a man and his
wife are one. For the sake of a small economy
however, the Victorian law, in this particular
case, holds that Berry and his wife are not
one. If Berry has any claim on the com-
munity—and it is formally decided that he has
—then it is impossible not to recognise his
wife’s claim also. The purpose of the annuity
was to make Berry happy in his old age. It
wasn’t really to provide him with food and
clothes and a dwelling, except on the ground
that these were incidental to his happiness ; if
there was any reason to suppose that he would
be perfectly happy without food and clothes
and a dwelling then true benevolence would
consist in leaving him without these things lest
they should disturb his mind. And the Vic-
torian community proceeds to make the old man
happy with the knowledge that when he dies
—and his chances of life are officially estimated
at years—he will leave his wife utterly un-
provided for. So far as the world knows
Berry’s wife is as absolutely devoid of private
fortune as he is himself, and, probably, even in
his old age, with his worldly experience to back
him, he is better fitted to hustle with the world
than she is. a
» * * *
The people of Victoria practically invite tneir
old hero to dine with them. They lay a knife
and fork for him—a small knife, perhaps, and
a cheap iron fork with three prongs, but still
they do lay these implements. And they don’t
ask Berry’s wife to dinner, or lay any knife
and fork for her, which is a miserably shabby and
discourteous and unmanly, not to say ungentle-
manly, proceeding. It wouldn’t cost much more
to provide that Berry’s annuity should last his
wife’s lifetime also, should she survive him, and
Victoria, having acknowledged that there is a
moral responsibility upon it, can’t decently
leave it half-fulfilled. It can’t now refuse to re-
cognise its obligation in the matter of Berry’s
wife, unless it holds that Berry has no right
to have a wife—that only the prosperous publi-
can, and the affluent pawnbroker, and the
large, fat, bloodshot, successful land-grabber
should marry and replenish the earth with des-
cendants like unto themselves, and that the
patriot who never had the leisure or the dis-
honesty to make money should leave no heirs to
keep his name alive. In that case the re-
plenished earth is likely to become even a
harder globe to live on than it is at present.
* * * *
Of course there is the hard-driven Treasury
to be considered. In the old glorious borrowing
days every Australian Government was
generous ; the large, strong men of great
circumference and great drinking-power who
ran things with a shovel in those times never
needed to take any thought for the morrow.
It wasn’t their morrow, anyhow. Now Vic-
toria is making a harder effort than any other
province to go straight, for the time at least,
and money is very scarce. But Berry, at all
events, was less responsible than most of his
contemporaries for the sins of the old days, and
he was more responsible than the majority for
the few good things that were done then. And
if Victoria behaves meanly to one of its very
few good servants so much the less is its chance
of getting good servants. Even the narrow re-
sources of the Melbourne Treasury are not so
narrow that it can’t afford to discharge this
obligation, it having been practically admitted
that the obligation exists. And if it is dis-
charged there is always the pious reflection,
which should adapt itself to the mind of so
painfully godly a community as that of Smell-
boom city, that things often come right for
those who do justly and chance the conse-
quences, and Providence will probably see fit
to kill off some millionaire so that the probate
duty will make the loss good. Anyhow, the
situation is this : Victoria has decided that it
is the right thing to ask Berry to dinner. It
has asked Berry to dinner. And a community
which asks Berry to dinner and doesn’t ask
Berry’s wife is no gentleman.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Chinese Colony.
S.A. Ex-Gov. Kintore has been prattling
gently to London World about “a great
northern tropical settlement, stretching right
across the Australian continent, and developed
by Chinese labor,” which labor he “ doesn’t
think there would be much difficulty in con-
fining to the Northern Territory.” This
mainly goes to show how very little Kintore
learned in his travels. If the great North was
developed by Chinamen, and cut up into farms
belonging to Chinamen, and the wealth it pro-
duced belonged to Chinamen, the Australian
people would be no better off for the develop-


nient. And the Chow would no more stay, if
he could help it, in a purely Chinese settlement
in North Australia than in a purely Chinese
settlement in China. He wants to have the
white man to sell his cabbagee to ; in a Chinese
settlement, with every Chow in the community
growing cabbagee, there would be no one to
buy the noisome vegetable. Therefore, it
would be necessary either to perpetually guard
every inch of the border-line along the Chow
boundary—a work which would employ nearly
the whole male white population—or else
every Chinaman found down South must
be assumed to be an escapee from the
northern Mongol settlement and be treated
accordingly. That would mean imprison-
ing him for (say) six months at the cost
of the wdiite citizen, and then painfully
humping him back across his own border, also
at the white Australian’s cost. And when he
got across his own border there would be no
guarantee that he wouldn’t break out again
next day. It would probably cost £2O or £5O
or even more to board and lodge each captured
Chow, and then escort him back again across
the line, and there might easily be 1000 or
2000 to be thus dealt with annually. In other
words, it would be the same struggle on a vastly
larger scale which Queensland is pretending
to maintain on its w'estern frontier. The Chow
will come over from the Northern Territory ;
there aren’t gaols enough to put him in, or
money enough to pay his gaol expenses, or
police enough to haul him back to his own
place after he has done his six months, and if
lie is hauled back he simply comes over again
ad infinitum. Also, he can’t be fined because he
has no cash, and so the authorities have practi-
cally given it up. They simply let him come.
And Kintore wants Australia to deliberately
cultivate this trouble on a much larger scale,
with noprospectof gettinganythinginparticular
out of it. The S. A. ex-Governor shines here as a
particularly luminous ass. Personally he is
talking on behalf of some syndicate.
More Freetrade Blessing's.
In his last week’s speech at Hamilton (N.S.W.),
when he manfully shoved the political campaign
a step further forward, Premier George Reid
made a lamentably poor and inaccurate apology
for the fiscal faith which is in him. The
Bulletin presumes that it is in him, partly be-
cause he says it is, and partly because there is
so much room for things in George Reid that
this is as likely to be in him as anything else.
Commencing on the usual weary comparison
between N.S. W. and the awful example across
the border, he said, according to the Tele-
graph’s report:—
They had now, on the authority of Mr. Fenton, the
Government Statistician of Victoria, who put the figures
of N.S. Wales and Victoria together and compared them,
a statement that in the year 1896, which was the first year
of all this Freetrade, there were as many hundreds
(hands) employed in factories in N.S. Wales as in
Victoria.
It is almost needless to say, as a commence-
ment, that this statement is wrong, because no
one ever knew Premier Reid’s figures to be
otherwise than wrong, provided he could make
his case look better by not having them right.
The figures which Mr. Fenton actually did
issue showed 50,096 people employed in Vic-
torian factories, and 49,609 employed in those
of New South Wales. The N.S.W. statisti-
cian, however, only claimed 49,055 hands, but
either way Fenton didn’t say what Reid says
he said, and again the unreliable fat man is
convicted of a gross inaccuracy which tends
to help along his own political purpose. The
surprising aspect of the case is, however, that
he should have taken the trouble to make the
miserable assertion at all. New South Wales
is about half-a-century older than Victoria. It is
nearly four times as large, and mainly by reason
of its extra size and its extra age it has a much
larger population. And, in addition, it has the
inestimable blessing of Foreign-trade which
helps along industry, and makes the community
jolly and fat, and accumulates wealth in the
land, and abolishes the unemployed, and does
all manner of good things, while Protection
produces nothing but ruin. And all that Pre-
mier Reid is prepared to allege is that the far
larger and older and more populous province
which is booming under his great, enlightened
policy, finds work and wages for as many indus-
trial toilers as the smaller, younger, and less
populous one which is blighted by the bar-
barous policy of the other fellow. He can’t even
prove that, but he alleges it. If Freetrade is
only as good as the other thing, N.S.W., by
reason of its extra population alone, and saying
nothing of its extra size and age, should have
nearly 5000 more industrial workers than Vic-
toria. And if Freetrade is a lot better than the
other thing, then N.S.W. should have 10,000
or 15,000 or 20,000 more workers than Victoria.
The Foreign-traders’ theory is that their policv
is so many miles ahead of the other one that
the latter isn’t visible on the horizon by com-
parison. And in proof of it they declare that
their great and magnificent system, combined
with a start of about 50 years, nearly four
times the territory and 100,000 more popula-
tion to provide producers and consumers, just
enables them to keep up with a new micro-
scopic competitor who is ruined by the horrors
of an effete system. FiVen if that very humble
statement had been accurate, which it isn’t, it
is so very small and abject that it is a wonder
anybody should haye thought it worth his while
to make it.

No Hope.
The N.S.W. general election is supposed to
take place in June, unless it is sprung on the
community a little earlier, and the hopes of the
Protectionist party are high. Just by way of
being prepared for emergencies, however, the
Protectionist party may as well let its hopes
down again. It is quite possible that it may
get into power next June, but it can’t keep
there for any length of time. The situation it
has got to face is briefly this : Three-fourths or
more of the community get their information
from the four Sydney daily papers. Three of
these lie consistently in favor of the Freetrade
party ; only one—the weakest and distinctly
the least convincing of the four—lies for the
Protectionist party. These three papers, every
day of their lives, tell the great bulk of the
people all the incredibly awful things that the
Protectionist leaders didn’t say and didn’t do,
and relate elaborate fictions about their debts
and deficits, about the ruin of the people under
their management, the decay of industry,
the falling of wages, the increase of the
unemployed, and all manner of other in-
genuities. They report the speeches of
Treetrade politicians at length, and call a
gathering of two men and a dog a stupendous
outbreak of popular enthusiasm, and, as nothing
is so contag ous as enthusiasm, the news of the
gigantic gathering, which didn’t take place, to
welcome some Freetrading nonentity at the
hamlet of Bogged Cow', gives rise to real gather-
ings elsewhere. They briefly report large
Protectionist demonstrations to the effect that
“ a few people gave a cold reception to Mr.
Jinks, M.L.A., who made some unconvincing
remarks with the evident uneasiness of a man
who knows he is upholding a weak cause; ”
and, as the next most contagious thing
to enthusiasm is depression, this alleged
frost leads to real frosts later on. They
issue bogus statistics and state facts that never
existed, and avoid contradiction by the
simple process of not publishing awkward letters
in reply, and the public assumes that the reason
why no reply appears is because the case is too
strong to be rebutted. The one feeble and hope,-
lessly Tory Protectionist daily isn’t strong
enough to make any headway worth mentioning
against this avalanche, and until the N.S.W.
Protectionists have a Sydney morning paper of
their own, with lots of money behind it and a
brainy autocrat to run things, they may as well
give up hope of any long continuance in office.
No party can keep in power whose leaders do
the dreadful things which the Protectionist
leaders do—or which the three big Sydney
papers say they do, which comes to practically
the same thing.
The Thrift Curse.
“ Old Jack ” re the Victorian Charities Bill:
Dear B.—ln connection with the Vic. Charities Bill
there is much talk about the deserving and undeserving
poor. The Argus in particular has no mercy on the
pauper whose past- has been thriftless. And yet, come to
think of it, the improvident have contributed more to
the building up of the country than the prudent. We are
so apt to associate poverty of the individual with his im-
provident habits, as cause and effect, that vve forget that
what he failed to keep for himself was distributed
amongst the many. The spasms of abnormal prosperity
Victoria has enjoyed have been due to an easy flow of
money into the hands of the working classes, which
money, as a general thing, went into the pouch of the
trader who, in turn, invested it in some form of exchange
or production. If the original possessor had practised
the much-lauded thrift, imports would have been
confined to the barest necessaries of life, and the
intellectual character of the people would have degene-
rated. For the habit of thrift so-called frequently
degenerates into the habit of parsimony, than which no
vice more certainly kills the intellectual life of the indi-
vidual or of the community. In times when Poor Richard
was an honored prophet, the stupendous lie that a penny
saved is a penny gained might have carried weight. But,
with Socialism in the air, the converse of the maxim is
suggested. A penny spent is a penny’s worth gained.
You have had its value yourself, and have given another
a chance to enjoy its value also. The liquor revenue of
Victoria pays for police, gaols, judges, courts, and hang-
men, and the whole paraphernalia of justice ; and the
tobacco-tax more than half-pays for public education.
Here we have two items of thriftless expenditure which
are of vast service to the community. Thus do the
reckless and bibulous and smoke-dried confer a
public benefit in which their critics and contemners
take a share, yet to which these critics have no
claim as contributors. What, then, becomes of the
cuckoo-cry about the improvident poor? Where would
the rich be but for those who spend their wages
as they earn them? Is the wage-earner’s life to he
an automatic sweat from birth to grave, with never a
breath of ease, or pleasure, or generous aspiration? Is
he born for Savings Bank returns only? Is the theatre
barred to him, merry companionship, a good dinner ? Yet
these things cost money, and, being enjoyed, leave him
at last on the list of the undeserving poor, while his
fellow, who has screwed and starved himself body and
soul, put his money into a Gobung Bank, and so come to
the same pass as his thriftless brother, is stuck upon a
pedestal as a deserving pauper! For the average idiot—-
who is generally, for obvious reasons, successful in life —
is insensible to the argument that but for our follies the
world would thrive ill, and that out of the “vices” of
improvidence and recklessness society reaps a harvest of
profit and advantage.

New Australia— Lane’s Socialistic heaven on
earth—as pictured by James Grove, in Wel-
lington (M.L.) Post of Jan. 5 :
I resided at t*he settlement three months. I worked
with the members, lived on their coarse and inadequate
fare, mixed with them, attended their meetings and enter-
tainments, and learnt all I possibly could about their
methods, their grievances, their prospects, &c., and I
could only come to one conclusion, viz. That it was not
the country or the climate, nor the Paraguayan Govern-
ment, or the members themselves that were at fault, but
simply the social and political conditions they had set up
for themselves. . . . The whole community seemed in a
state of anarchy and confusion. The single men com-
plained that they were keeping other men’s wives and
families. The married women said “ their husbands kept,
them in old Australia, and they should keep them in the New
Australia.” This led to difficulty in the single men getting
their meals, and their washing and mending done, and they
threatened to take a day a week for each—that is, one for
mending and one for washing. Every one was calling
everybody else “ a loafer and a bad Socialist,”and anyone
who paid heed to what they said of each other would set
them down as a very low-minded, bad lot. . . . The
children roamed about the day long playing with frogs,
killing serpents, and gorging 'themselves with oranges
and other fruits; and corrupting the native children
with their bad manners, bad language, and precocity
generally, and if corrected by their parents they told their
elders that “ they did not belong to them, but to the
State.” (!!!) And the privacy of the home was destroyed,
for every little private affair under the Socialism or Com-
munism (for the members differed even about this) that
flourished in New Australia became public property,
and the women quarrelled and fought over the joints
of meat. I was domiciled next the butcher’s shop, so
I know what I am talking about, and many a time
I was awakened by the women assembled thereat at
break of day waiting for the door to open to get the first
choice, and the various male members, who rapidly suc-
ceeded each other in the official capacity of butcher, had
a very hot time of it. And written' complaints were
handed in at the weekly meetings while I was in the
settlement, showing that the complainant was served
with nothing but bone, and short quantity at that. Then
at these meetings, which the women had a right to attend,
being members the same as the men, the ladies would
occasionally call one another liars
Quotation may as well stop here. It is well
understood that to eradicate the old Adam is a
tough job ; but wdien he joins forces with the
old Eve, who will say, after this, that the
combination isn’t irresistible ?
THe point as to whether police-magistrate
Panton, of Melbourne, is a fit and proper per-
son to have a voice in issuing “ colonial ” wine
licenses will come before Vic. Full Court next
month. Justice Hood has (unwillingly) granted
a decree nisi calling upon sundry parties to
show that the said Panton is not disqualified
by bias, or risk of bias, from sitting as a mem-
ber of Licensing Courts, and the result should
put an end to much distempered discussion. Pan-
ton, P.M., is not accused of a desire to license
every person who wishes to open a wine-shop,
but he is accused of a fervid desire to encourage
the sale of Australian wine for the advance-
ment of the wine growing industry. And the
accusation may be accepted as proved. Mr. Pan-
ton liimself would be the last man to deny it.
He is biassed in favor of the “ colonial ” wine
t rade to much the same degree that magistrate
Lormer is biassed against the liquor trade in
general. Therefore, seeing that Lormer has
been removed from the Licensing Bench, ought
Panton to be similarly dealt with ? This pins
the Law, the shifty, straggling Law, on the
point which fanatics have not been anxious to
recognise as an all-important one. Is the Law
a teetotaller or a moderate drinker in senti-
ment ? If it deems the temperance doctrine a
thing worthy of national acceptance, then must
Panton be disqualified as a faddist. If it holds
that alcohol is a wholesome food for the public
body and mind, then must Panton be accepted
as a wise magistrate, whose enthusiastic ap-
proval of the wine industry amounts to patriot-
ism. A man may have an unfair bias towards a
false philosophy, but not towards the Truth. Is
teetotalism Truth in the sight of the Law ? If
the answer to this question is a negative, the
case against Panton falls to the ground with a
sickening thud. He cannot be “incapable of
justly appreciating the value of the objections
lodged by residents, or ratepayers, to the
granting of new wine licenses,” if he knows
those teetotal objections to be founded on
error, for anything founded on error has no true
value whatever. In cases where the objection
is not of a teetotal character, and the would-be
licensee rather than the license is protested
against, Panton’s notorious fondness for the
wine-industry cannot be supposed to influence
his judgment any more than the appetite for
meat disqualifies him for passing sentence on a
butcher.
The jury-foreman in the Carruthers v. Lons-
dale and Norton criminal libel case states
emphatically that—
“ There were ten jurors for a conviction and two for
an acquittal. Five of the ten desired that no punishment
should be inflicted, not because defendants had justified
the libel, but because they thought the press should have
larife freedom in dealing' with such matters. Ten were
willing to bring in a verdict of guilty, but five wanted a
rider added that the defendants should not be punished.”
That is, five jurymen thought Norton and
Lonsdale deserved punishment; and seven—a
majority of two —thought they did not. Apart
from the merits of the case, the five who
wanted the rider showed sound sense. The law
is full of technical traps and pitfalls, and a
juryman who thinks a defendant only tech-
nically guilty has no business to deliver .that
defendant to the tender mercies of the Judge—
even of such a soft-hearted judge as Judge
Rofe Backhouse. A jury has a right to say
not only whether a man is guilty or not guilty,
but also to say—if it finds him guilty—just how
guilty he is. In hundreds of cases in the past—
notably in many of those over which Judge
Windeyer presided—a jury has allowed itself
to be impressed by the Judge’s heavy jaw, or
by his masterful personality, or by its
faith in his fairness, or by its veneration
for his office, into merely recording its opinion
that a man was technically guilty according to
the law. Then the Judge arose in his might
and crushed with years of hard labor a man
whom a jury-majority thought deserving of only
a nominal sentence. The Bulletin is glad to
see that a more sensible habit is growing ; and
it now tells every man who reads these lines
that as juryman, and to the extent of his power,
he is individually responsible for the sentence
as well as for the verdict—and that he sins
against citizenship if, by failure to impress his
view of a righteous sentence upon the jury and
Judge, a sentence is passed w'hich he regards as
unrighteous and unjust.
Item from report of an election meeting held
last week in Sydney by G. Black, M.L.A. :
A wrangle followed over the questions. One was, “Is
it not better to tax foreign products than our own land ? ”
Mr. Black replied that only the unimproved value was
taxed.
The Bulletin believes in and has ajways advo-
cated a land-tax, but, all the same, this cant
phrase about it not being the land that is taxed
but only the unimproved value makes it feel
very hot and tired. Is there anything to be
ashamed of in a land-tax that the people who
advocate taxing land should try to avoid say-
ing straight-out that they do advocate it? Of
course it is the unimproved value that is
taxed; it must be either that, or the im-
proved value, or the area, or something,
and if taxing the unimproved value isn’t tax-
ing the land, then taxing the improved value
or the area isn’t, taxing it either, and there is
no possibility of taxing the land. To be more
beastly accurate still, it isn’t the unimproved
value of the land that is taxed, but the im-
proved or unimproved fellow who owns it.
Also, as a similar piece of accuracy, Dibbs, ex-
cept in a very few cases, didn’t tax any imports;
he put 10 and 15 per cent, on the unimproved
or imported value of a lot of things, but, accord-
ing to the Single-taxer’s phrase, that wasn’t
taxing the things themselves at all. Yet the
Single-taxers keep on saying that Dibbs did
tax imports. Anyhow, it takes a great deal
too much time to call it “ a tax upon the man
who owns the land, said tax being levied ac-
cording to the unimproved value,” and people
will probably go on calling it a land-tax—
which is near enough, anyhow.
All admirers of the great doctrine of cheap-
ness should seriously consider the Sydney ’bus-
horse. Ten years ago, the price of a ride any-
where in Sydney was 3d. Then the ,old ’bus
co. paid respectable, though not lavish, divi-
dends ; the drivers worked long hours for a
moderate wage ; and the two animals in the
shafts preserved a semblance of cheerfulness
while they hauled a vehicle licensed to carry 11
passengers. Then came the era of penny and
twopenny fares, and the driver’s hours were
lengthened and his wages cut down 30 or 40
per cent. Also a seat was stuck on the roof of
each ’bus, and two gaunt, staggery horses now
drag 20 passengers instead of 11, and drag them
longer hours on less rations. The average ’bus-
horse now is an animal that can’t be stopped
without serious risk of his falling down when
he stands still he mostly hangs suspended
by the harness, and the driver holds him
up, partly by main force and partly by
dreadful threats of what he will do if the
animal drops. The Jehu is soured by.
long hours, and very brief pay, and the diffi-
culty of inciting his half-dead team to its
destination in reasonable time, and the dire
necessity to keep the takings up to a certain
average. He knows that if he takes his eye off
the team it may fall down dead at any moment,
and if he keeps his eye on it he will miss possi-
ble passengers, and'if he doesn’t have a' con-
stant eye on the people inside they won’t pay
their fares, and if perdition was anywhere
handy he would feel more than half-inclined to
drive the whole outfit into it and be done with
the business.
The Great Half-Caste Conspiracy.
Thus writes “Yours languidly, C.J.T.,” from
“ Hell End,” Queensland :
Dear B.~That Queensland will rather lose than gain
by federating, is a bald-headed assertion which the
writer will attempt to prove. The seat of Federal Govern-
ment will be nearer the Murray than the Q’sland border.
The general tendency is to the lengthening of Parlia-
ments. Humanity is in the habit of conforming to its
surroundings. Now, as the Federal members will pro-
bably put in most of their long term of office at the
Federal city, our Northern members will, by becoming
imbued with the sentiment and methods of the South, be
alienated from their trusting constituents in the North.
And it is not well that the ways of the South should
supersede the wa3's of the North, because little but the
language is common to the two di visions. Because Nature
omitted to put a mountain chain from Sandy Cape to
Cossack (W.A.) some people persist in wanting to run this
country on Hinglish principles. It would be just as
reasonable to federate India and Siberia, and govern the
former from Tobolsk. The South doesn't know the North,
but we Northerners do know .you, because we troop down
once a year to give you some of our surplus cash.
* » * *
Commercially, you are a rushing sort of folk, and
beastly particular, spelling* business with an aggressively
large B; demanding “references” and introductions;
and being insanely anxious that your individual clocks
and watches shall all keep the same time as the town
clock. We are a sit-on-the-counter-and-talk-politics sort
of people, spelling business as badly as we can ; thinking
nothing of “characters” arid “introductions,” and not
caring a damn if no two timepieces in the district agree,
because our great unwritten philosophy is that Life is a
beastly nuisance, to be taken as lazily as possible. The
Exeter Hall element has a large and emphatic sav in
your policy, whereas we are comparatively free from'the
medueval taint—barring such times as ToV.er gets a touch
of hysterical virtue, and plays to the family circle. Our
justice also is not of the Botany I lay variety. Our
benches may be a bit hot in times of storm, but in
normal times we don't go in for Mount Rennie or Suffif.ld
sentences, and no one gets three years for stealing 9d.
worth of baking-powder. We have no Wikdeyf.rs,
Stephens, and so on, and we don’t want them,
which is why we have to borrow a Jeffreys from
you when we have an exceptionally bad case on
hand. Again, we have no “ Rocks ” and “ Colling-
wood” “pushes,” our larrikins being Y.M.C.A. chaps
to yours. And the much-wailed-over Queensland worker
is really more independent than his Southern fellow. We
can always tell a southerner by his aggravated respect for
the boss, and his tendency to “ spoil the job” by “rush-
ing.” And the boss from the South is conspicuous on his
part by the amount of “ side ” he puts on.
* * * *
Granted that our northerners touch their hats to the
Chows, and our southerners get off the sidewalk for the
Kanaka —surely The Bulletin w ouldn’t have us so un-
grateful as to treat our saviours with public contempt.
Granted that there is an awful smell in Brisbane, the
victims are not so ill-able to stand the shock as was the
case when your Sydney and Smellbourne banks closed.
There is even a redeeming point about our fraud, in that
it was worked in such a large, airy, if-you-don’t-like-me-
chuck-me-out sort of way. Also, Stoddart lost his silver
watch in Brisbane, but it was not taken by a Queens-
lander, for to be a successful pickpocket argues energy
and enterprise, and no one but a newchum would accuse
a Northerner of those vices. And, anyhow, no one born
under the shadow of the Q.N.B. would go in for a silver
watch. Besides, these things belong to Brisbane, which
is in Queensland, and the writer is whooping for Queens-
land— with the largest “Q” you ever said. You are in
the habit of anathematising our leading men—that is
because you take them seriously. We don’t!—in fact, we
take nothing seriously—Parliament, judicature, Society,
churches, or Labor-party. Nelson is dear to the hearts
of Queenslanders as being the embodiment of the “ never-
mind ” national sentiment, and even Tozer and Barlow
are all right if you make allowance for the Ananias in
them. The Bulletin has always preached the gospel of
Irreverence, and the North is the only comfortable home
of Irreverence on this earth, yet the B. wants
us to conform to the old-fashioned ideas of the
very hotbeds of tin-god worship—the Southern pro-
vinces. It is a pleasing Australian custom to sneer
at the ignorance of our affairs displayed in the
British press, but the Australian press and public
show a far less-excusable ignorance of Australia.
Sydney D.T., for instance, recently stated that “Thurs-
day Island is practically a Japanese settlement," an
announcement which is about as new and surprising as
that “Captain Cook is dead !” And vet you pretend to
know all about the North. Also, the D.T. goes on to say,
“ There is scarcely an inland town but has its Japanese
brothel . . . thus disseminating unspeakable diseases.”
Now, there s scarcely a Queensland inland town that ever
saw a Jap., and in any case the Japs, are proverbially free
from disease as compared with whites. Also, the fact,
that the North imports Japs, for the hysterical!y-adjec-
tived purpose proves that we haven’t enough degraded
white women to supply the demand. Also, you Souther-
ners should shut up your heads on this subject, for you
can afford to export degraded women—and not because
the demand is small, either.
* * * »
The Bulletin is often impartial enough to publish
matter antagonistic to its pet ideas. Take a step further
and let us have a cartoon by Hop. entitled “Queensland
after Federation” —various beasts of prey representing
the Southern provinces, picking the bones of a baby-'
alligator. In the background a “returned” Kanaka
working for his missionary at the typical Christian salary
of one fig of bad tobacco per annum. And the Kanaka’s-
best girl with a half-caste infant amazingly like unto the
aforesaid fat missionary. Kanaka soliloquises, “No booze,
no white Mary ; can’t even have my own Mary to myself.'
Damn Federation ! ” We can’t take Federation seriously ;
it’s too thin. We are going in for Separation, and then a
United Northern Australia—the three Queenslands, Nor-
thern Territory, North W.A., and New Guinea, with the
seat of Government in the Never-Never. You go ahead
and federate—like to like. We ain’t pining to be bossed
by the Botany Bay provinces. Also, you are herewith re-
quested to cease from your irreverent remarks about our
Queensland burlesque co. You annoy the performers, and
they are very sensitive. Besides, if we choose to 'sup-
port them, that proves us satisfied with the show.
Some day, we may feel cool and serious enough to
decorate the Brisbane lamp-posts with the members of
that co., but there’s plenty of time, and, as you’re not
paying the piper, it’s none of your business, so never-
mind. Finally, life in Australia'is. already verv dull, and
under your scheme, it will hardly be worth while to get
out of bed. But, with two great, antagonistic Federations ;
no extradition treaty ; exclusive Immigrant Acts, strictly
enforced ; and two big standing armies yearning for glory
—well, there will be fun in the land !
