Frank Thornton announces, in a painfully
sudden fashion, that his last appearance in Aus-
tralia will be made on 2nd Feb. “Charley’s
Aunt finishes on I riday, and the brief remain-
>ng fragment of the season is to be devoted to a
very short spell apiece of “ Sweet Lavender,”
Private Secretary,” and “Miss Brown.”
Whether the farewell is absolutely final, and
Parson Spalding shakes the alluvial deposit of
Australia off his boots for the rest of his life, and
proposes not to come back at all but rather to
see this continent hanged first, or whether the
Secretary and his Bun may be expected to drift
along again in the saddening procession of years,
are tilings that do not definitely appear. The
Bulletin hopes for the best in a shattered frame
of mind, and reckons that it will have a drop of
something short to keep it up. This country
isn’t growing brighter. Some of its gods are
getting very mouldy; and some have got into
trouble through being out late with a girl; and
some have been licked through the other fellow
getting in on the jaw ; and some have paid £d. in
the £ ; and some only made 10 runs at a crisis,
and said the wicket had crumbled. Most of the
others are now pretending to be dead, so as to
avoid explaining where the deposits went to.
Under these circumstances, if the Secretary and
the Bun are really going for good, it will be a
severe blow.
* * * *
A dramatic season commences at Sydney
Lyceum on Saturday, and that establishment will
be closed on Thursday and Friday with a view to
making adequate preparation for the event.
Therefore by the time these remarks appear in
print Hobson and Jobson and the Cat should
have received the reward of their misdeeds—
which reward will probably consist in being re-
moved to the place whence they came, and there
severely hanged on their own recognisances under
the Hardened Offenders Act, and alsoordered tofind
two sureties of .£5O each and costs. The sudden
w-ithdrawal of “ Whittington ” makes it unneces-
sary to blight that show with any further com-
ments. 1 he new dramatic season opens with
“ Work and Wages,” produced “ by arrangement
with G. Riguold, Esq.” Which is a pleasant
change after the many things that have appeared
by arrangement with Billolloway, Esq.
* * * * #
The two surviving pantomimes seem good
for a considerable time yet. “Jack the Giant-
killer ” should be able to keep up for a long while
on the strength of the ballet element alone. There
is a large amount of symmetrical limb in it, and
the limb, in every case, has a nice girl attached to
it; the illuminated ballet, where the angels sud-
denly blaze forth into a mass of electric light, is a
brilliant effect,,and the Hulines are something
above the average in the musical way. The
tableau of “ Britain’s Empire, ” which is supposed
to show all the hundreds of thousands of Victoria’s
warriors—white, brown, black, yellow, speckled,
skewbald, copper-colored, and otherwise—all
ready to fight for their beloved Queen, and
stretching from the footlights right back to the
3’“ar 1, is a fine bit of color, and it would have a
better show if the public had had time to forget
the famous Waterloo tableaux at Her Majesty’s a
few weeks ago. Still, the view of this great armed
circus prepared to perish on its last camel in
defence of Victoria makes the public feel good.
After seeing it the said public is more than half-
inclined to rush out and at once join Jawbone
Neild s new regiment, which is now advertising
for fecruits not over 50 years of age to replace
certain warriors who have indignantly chucked
the beastly thing up.
* * * *
“Babes in the Wood” goes steadily at
Her Majesty’s up to date. The two boss
humorists, Coleman and Burke, are perhaps a
shade more ragged than they were three weeks
aso. When they started they looked like Hungry
Higgins and Weary Walker who had fallen out of
the American tramp joke, and apparently a few
more dogs have rushed out at them in the course
of their pilgrimage .sinoe then, and further depre-
ciated their wardrobe. This is in the early part
of the show, of course, before they rise to
sudden sinful affluence on the proceeds of
their crime, and become tw r o aggressive
bounders living on cigars and cocktail. Next
to Coleman and Burke probably the most
popular single feature of the show is still the
unobtrusive retirement of Lily Titheradge and
Carrie Moore to bed. Everybody seems more or
less glad at this item. They retire, apparently,
in freshly-laundered night-dresses each evening,
and the frills on these interesting garments are
beyond any shadow of suspicion. And neither of
them looks under the bed first to see if there is a
man there, or looks into the wardrobe to see if
there is a cat there—which gives the show com-
pletely away. No play in which a girl goes to
bed without first looking for a man and a cat can
appear decently life-like.
*# * *
Fitzgerald Bros’ Circus and Hagenbeck’s
zoological show continue at the old address and
break out nightly into fresh developments of
horse. The Tivoli show is likewise in the best of
condition, and the “ Factory Girl of Sydney ” is
unfolding her groan of woe at the Royal Standard.
Melb. Princess’s is once more rejoicing in
full attendances, and the ill-fated firm of Kate,
Pete and Co. will carry on business until Wednes-
day next, if not for longer. “The Manxman”
doesn’t grow more impressive the oftener it is con-
sidered—the tricks of melodrama can be seen at
work and the unnecessary padding of Wilson Bar-
rett’s part becomes rather tiresome—but the show is
a first-class novelty, nevertheless. Kate Cregreen
lives in the flesh—a strong, Swinburnian girl, tak-
ing the roses and raptures of love as they come,
and not afraid to inform Pete that he has lavished
his chuckle-headed endearments on another fel-
low’s child. Kate, being virtuous to a fault,
suffers accordingly. As an interesting sufferer,
she draws tears from many females in the
audience, whilst wise men feel glad to think
that the ladies are thoroughly happy all round.
Old Caesar Cregeen, an illiterate pharisee,
proclaiming the Wor-rd from the summit
of his own beer-barrel, is another vigorous
reality ; likewise the small cad, who reckons he
lias bought up the barmaid when he orders three
sixpenny drinks, is an eternal fact in human
nature, probably for the reason that the Kitties of
this world never venture to hurt his alleged feel-
ings with a bottle. Philip, the seducer, a victim
to his aunt’s respectability and other circum-
stances, is only a flabby sort of sinner in the play.
Wilson Barrett is a heavy Pete, anyhow. His
elocution seems to be rather overweighted—one
detects an elderly roll in his voice—and his cheer-
fulness looks rather as though it were borrowed
for the occasion. An experienced, level-headed
actor will, of course, always be effective in a fine
acting character, and Mr. Barrett is pretty gene-
rally popular. Some people would like him better
if he were not stich a distinctly leading man, and
didn’t linger so long over his bits of “ fat.”
** * *
“The War Cloud now looming Darkly in
the Air,” &c., has warned Dan Barry to prepare
for hostilities. On Saturday he staged an army
of thirteen infantry, or more, at Melb. Alexandra,
and started “ The Relief of Lucknow,” a play
understood to have been founded on the Indian
mutiny. Dan Barry’s revival of this gory subject
is somewhat incomprehensible. The gallery, on
Saturday, came to the wrong conclusion that
Ranjitsinhji had just declared war against
V.R., and was waiting round the corner for a
chance to murder the English Eleven in their
sleep. A person resembling Ranji appeared from
time to time, with a stealthy cat-like tread, and
stabbed Captain Stoddart, or somebody, under
the shoulder-blade. Also, he was an object of
terror to several married ladies who had never
seen him bat, and begged to be riddled by British
bullets before Ranji could bring up his insurgents.
“ The Relief of Lucknow ” is not written in the
spirit of a time when half the Melbourne popula-
tion is wearing Ranji, on a cigarette advt. button,
next to its heart; but the gunpowder explosions,
and the awful valour of the thirteen warriors in
going behind the scenes to fight and coming back
with bandaged limbs, will probably earn a divi-
dend for the management. Miss Dora Fothering-
ham played the heroine. She meant well, and
had a lot of solid faith in the indestructibility of
the MacGregors and the MacLarens—not to men-
tion Charlie M'Leod—but her performance
tempted The Bulletin’s representative to put
himself to the sword. The tail-end of the above
paragraph was written in blood.
The small panto, at Melbourne Bijou col-
lapsed suddenly last week, and “ Dick Whitting-
ton,” at the Royal, is left with a monopoly.
“ Dick ” will supply an annual want for some time
yet. It might have been a less-satisfactory show
than it really is. Moreover, the variety duet
between Maggie Moore and Harry Roberts has
developed into a large item, and the occasional
jokes are more frequent than at first. Veteran
Edwin Kelly resumed his place in the co.
last Saturday after a fortnight’s absence. The
Bulletin sketch labelled Kelly in last week’s
page of panto, illustrations was really a striking
of Herbert Ashton, who reigned in the
veteran’s stead, but didn’t figure on the pro-
gramme.
** * »
Miss Lilian Tree has been added to the
Opera House bill, and sundry new people are
appearing with Harmston’s Circus, which has
begun to hold out preliminary threats of depar-
ture from Melbourne.
** * *
The panto, at Melb. Bijou died suddenly
on the Monday night of last week. It looked
like a delicate proceeding from the first. A some-
what youthful bookmaker was said to have backed
the bill for costumes, whilst the lessee (a lady)
made arrangements of a speculative character
with landlord Wilson. The combined efforts of
these three parties kept the show going for a
fortnight, and, with a promise of further support
in cash and free use of the theatre, the co. would
have held on, but for the filthy lucreous demands
of the orchestral band. The band were sordid
creatures, who stipulated for first grab at the
receipts, to which concession the stage performers
wouldn’t agree, so the season stopped short for
want of music. How like a band !—as the
Duchess of Plaza Toro remarks in “Gondoliers.”
Fiddlers, tromboners, &c., get no personal glory
from a pantomime, and, unless their craving for
salary is satisfied, they shoulder their instruments
and depart.
** * *
Harmston’s circus has suffered an exas-
perating series of disasters to the tent since it was
pitched in Melbourne. The canvas, probably in-
jured by much exposure to the Eastern sun,
seems to tear easily. Nevertheless manager
Bob Love wears a cheerful countenance, and
runs the show in a sociable spirit. On
Twelfth Night the company were invited to
jubilate and feed together at the Democratic
Club. In the course of the year there are
three festive occasions, when the lady acrobats
make a point of smiling heariily at the funny
clown’s jokes (which in the ordinary way of busi-
ness they can’t do), whilst the strong man cracks
wheezes with his iron jaw—and good old Twelfth
Night is one of these.

a fashion—by Dan Barry, at Melb. Alexandra,
was a popular jingo drama in the days when Dot
Boucicault’s mother could figure as the sweetest of
young heroines.

“ Harry Musgrove has expressed his serious intention
of returning to Melbourne and starting a sheaf of libel-
actions against sundry newspapers and base bawlers,”
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent. “ He
states he has the accounts of the tour in perfect order,
and that in the opinion of legal men here he has merely
to present them and cry ‘ Deliver ! ’ Brother George,
with a boom gathering round the Shaftesbury, does not
see his way to release his manager, so the trip may be
delayed. But——”
Pete Hughes, who was the baldest-headed theatrical
agent on this rolling globe, is said to have developed
some hairy oasis in his wilderness by means of a “ re-
storer.” George Bruce, of the Melb, softgoods business,
had previously appied the same “restorer” on hs tract
of scalp, and proved its virtues—or so the yarn goes. It
sounds incredible. Pete was reduced to a fringe ol hair
around the top of his neck, and if the patent liniment
has caused the roots of Pete’s frince to send up suckers
through the crown of his head, it must be strong enough
to raise thistles from a billiard ball.
He the tank drama. The recent revival recalls a
Poverty Point anecdote of a Melb. manager, whose exces-
sive “ nearness ” approached almost to genius. The
miserable sinner who had to fall into the tempestuous
tank, and be hauled out at the last moment by the god-
like hero, contracted bronchial troubles, and had to spend
half his wages on cough-mixture. With this excuse, he
pleaded for a rise. “What!” gasped the boss, “ more
money because of a bit of a wetting?- Good God, man !
I was thinking of charging you sixpence a night for the
bath.”
A young actor named Bransby Williams is said to have
made a great impression at the London ’alls in a series of
Dickens’ character sketches, making up as Micawber,
Quilp, Chadband, &c., without leaving the stage. After
a quite thrilling burst of Quilp, the actor, on being
recalled, presents himself as a fair likeness of Charles
Dickens meditating in the now’ Empty Chair at Gadshill.
Evidently a much more fetching “ turn ” than that of the
dreary egotist imported by Garner for Sydney Palace
Theatre—the “lightning-change” gentleman whose im-
personations required so many long intervals of curtain.
Haresfoot : “ No Thespian autobiog. ever delighted me
so much as old Fred. Belton’s, in Sydney Mail. He told
how, in management at Edinburgh, he engaged a London
star as Fairy-in-Chief for his Xmas panto. He was aghast
when she came, a doubled-up old witch, but at night she
was a thing- of beauty. So does Geo. Vanderhoff write of
his horror when he rehearsed ‘ The Hunchback,’ with
preternaturall.y ugly Miss Cushman as the heroine. At
night, his breath was taken away when she appeared at
the wing a vision of loveliness, in' golden curls.’’

demand are big enough. Meanwhile such old marks as
‘ Virginius ' and ‘Richelieu ’ stick up, despite time-serving
critics, who pretend to show that Pinero and Jones have
wiped them off the slate.’’
“The Manxman " was first dramatised by Mr. Lewis
Waller in ’95, and produced by him at London Shaftes-
bury. Waller acted Christian, wherefore he dwarfid
Pete. Evidently Caine’s very elastic masterly diama
may be written round any one of three character?
—Pete, Christian, or Kate, or, for Shakespearian
art, it may be balanced so as to give all three their due
weight, which would be equal. Mr. Barrett’s Pete drama
gradually squeezes Kate out from the commanding posi-
tion she has in the first act, but even when she retires to
the background she remains the central point of interest;
H. : “The play of ‘The Manxman,’ like ‘Mrs. Tart-
queray,’ stirs the most painful chords of our nature.
After a tumult Of admiration, its faults begin to steal up.
Such Work is too physical and ahilnal ever to challenge
the great intellectual products of the stage. Uneducated
heroes and heroines, like Pete and Kate, can never engagb
the loftiest acting, which must be subtle, and will be
hlore so as civilisation advances.”

Two comedians at a certain playhouse had a fight on
the stage one night just before curtain-rise on Act 11. of
their panto. They had about finished wrecking each
others make-up when the excited stage-manager
quenched them by threatening to ring up and give the
front of the house a sight of the tussle. Cause—a woman,
as usual.
A well-to-do business man, staying at a fashionable
southern hotel, there became acquainted with a lady
well-known in the song and dance line. He fell in love ;
she reciprocated. His wife became jealous and rageful.
Meeting Miss Fantastic-toe on the hotel staircase landing,
it was : “ Creature ! ” “ Pooh ! ” “ You hussy 1 you
ought not to be allowed to mingle with respectable
people.” “Pan! you old cat! he likes me better than
you. Why don’t you go and die?” Slap! Smack!
Scratch ! Tear ! Scream ! Yell! Blood and hair and
fragments of dress fabric. A clutch and a swaying, and
a kerwollop down the stairs, and two torn visions of
loveliness were only saved from broken bones by the hus-
band and a friend who buffered and retained them. The
husband held the song-and dance lady ; his friend saved
the wife. The umpire’s award was that the married lady
had won the scrap by 10 points to nil. As soon as Miss
Fantastictoe’s face was healed, however, the husband
outrageously escorted her into the dining-room and
seated her in the place of honor at his own table. The
wife packed up and chose another hotel. That husband
makes his wife an allowance now, and has been “ doing”
other cities with his new fancy. A vulgar story, but true.
The management of a prosperous silver-coin show has
raised the salary of the experienced “actah” who plays
the juvenile lead from ss. to 7s. 6d. a night. This is an
entirely new departure, as the previous rule was to im-
mediately sack any individual who wanted a rise, there
being plenty of broken-down mummers ready to take his
place.

and amused the inmates. Daisy Chard’s whistling and
H, Shine’s songs were rapturously received by the lunatics.
“Bob’’Love and Mrs. Harmston-Love, of Harmston’s
Circus, no matter upon what part of the earth their tent
may be pitched, always entertain their friends on Xmas
night, New Year’s night, and on Jan. 13 - “ Bob’s ” birth-
day. This year’s celebration of the last-named festival
took place at Melb. Democratic Hall, and the regard for
Love took tangible form in the presentation of a gold
medal, and, from the ladies, a gold sovereign-case. Joe
Brown, who, as Love’s agent may be described as Cupid’s
arrow, hit the mark with much eloquence.

Elsie Hall was received at her native Toowoomba by
the senior member for the district and the whole of the
available population. She afterwards gave a concert
to a crowded audience, but, despite the fact that she
delighted her hearers, they compelled her to submit to
the inevitable illuminated address.

servation in the British Museum. Anyone who

Edison Electric Parlor, 162 Pitt-street, Sydney,
can make records of peoples’ voices ad lib., and
also secure a fine selection of musical and other
records of the highest quality.*

“SURPLUS.”
The term “ surplus ” is applied, in life insurance affairs,
to the balance, ascertained periodically, between ths
assets and the liabilities. Out of this balance or surplun-
distributions of profits are made to the policy-holders eee
titled to participate; due provision being made in th,
valuation for the rights of future participants. The
natural tendency of distribution is to diminish the surplus
for the moment; but in the case of a prosperous com-
pany, the decrease is speedily more than covered by
accessions of profit from various sources. It is clear, how-
ever, that the more liberal the periodical distributions of
profits, the more gradual will be the growth of the sur-
plus. Conversely, if a life insurance company withholds
from its policy-holders the full benefit of the proportion
of profits immediately available, and prefers to accumulate
in the interest of future participants, it follows that the
surplus will increase more rapidly in proportion. It re-
quires but a moment’s consideration, however, to perceive
that it is easy to keep in hand more than the future will
require under any possible circumstances; and, when
that course is followed, injustice to present participants is
inevitable.
Now, The Mutual Life of New York, while careful to
make abundant reserves for future bonuses, has always
been equally careful to distribute to its policy-holders the
full immediate benefits which they are entitled to claim.
The Company has never sacrificed the interests of policy-
holders entitled to immediate participation, to the forma-
tion of an imposing but empirical surplus, never likely to
be required even in the remotest future. The surplus
shown by The Mutual Life at the end of 1896 was
£6,105,444. It is a steadily increasing surplus, which,
needless to say, is more than ample for all the demands
for which it has been created. But, supposing the Com-
pany had, throughout its history, acted on the principle—
which has been adopted in, at least, one prominent in-
stance —of reserving a large proportion of the surplus at
every distribution, and, in addition, reducing the sur-
render values allowed for discontinued policies, what
does the reader suppose would have been the amount of
The Mutual’s surplus in 1896 ? Not six millions, but up-
wards of 24 millions sterling 1 This is not guesswork.
The figures cannot be challenged, for they are based upon
the official report issued by the Insurance Department of
the State of New York, which gives annually the exact
record of every company doing business in that State.
Since its organisation, The Mutual Life has paid in
bonuses and surrender values to its policy-holders
£44,445,244, which is more than twice the amount paid for
similar items by the next largest company.
Observe, then, what a magnificent surplus The Mutual
could have exhibited to-day, had an illiberal policy been
pursued in relation to the assured—a surplus exceeding
that of any other office by more than 150 per cent. But,
fortunately, the Company preferred to sacrifice a mere
useless and vainglorious display to the solid interests of the
policy-holders. Who shall say that, in taking this course,
The Mutual Life of New York has not. chosen the
better part ? and that, in so doing, it has not well earned
the high reputation which it has attained in every part of
the world as “the Policy-holders’ Company,”' “ the Com-
pany which does the most good ? ” In view of the real
facts of the case, we feel confident that the intelligent
reader will not allow himself or his friends to be misled
by the parading of sums which are of no use to the pre-
sent generation, and in excess of any possible needs of tlv
generations to come.*
The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The N.S.W. 3 per cent, loan of £1,500,000 went
iff successfully last week, yet despite its success there
was nothing in the way of a record about it. The stock
fetched, nominally, £lOO Bs. 4d. per cent., and as 7s. 2d.
per cent, is to be deducted for accrued interest this means
that the price was really £10C) Is. 2d. Westralia’s famous
3 per cent, loan of 1896 sold at £lOO 16s. Bd., less 12s. 4d.
accrued interest, or £lOO 4s. 4d. And only two months
ago Premier Turner got off a small 3 per cent, loan in
Melbourne at £lO3 Bs. Id. These figures do not include
expenses. Allowing for ex.’s on the lowest scale the pre-
sent N.S.W. loan probably realises £9B 13s. sd. to the
Treasury. The Westralian 3 per cent, loan of 1896 is said
to have realised slightly over £99 to the Perth Treasury.
The N.S.W. 3 per cent, stock sold in Sydney yielded
£99 17s. 4d., or £1 3s. lid. per cent, more than the price
obtained in London, and the .small Victorian loan of last
November gave the Treasury about £lO3 7s. There-
fore the statement,; made both by the S.M.
Herald and Evening News, that the new loan
shows N.S.W.’s credit to be higher than that of any
other province in the group, is a good way out. The
Victorian loan was such a small affair that it is, perhaps,
hardly solid evidence, but for all material purposes it was
Reid’s locally-floated loan, and not the present British
transaction, which showed, &c., &c.
Curiously enough, Sydney Daily Telegraph an-
nounces that “ the price obtained for this loan is the most
satisfactory one ever obtained, for a 3 per cent, loan by
this colony. The next best one was floated in October,
1895, when the amount required was £4,000,000 and the
average price obtained £96 18s. 3d.” Apparently there is
a desire to say as little as possible about the great success
of the local loan, and to drop the awkward fact that Pre-
mier Reid deliberately offers N.S.W. securities to Bull at
a lower price than he demands off the local investor.
The real trouble seems to be that N.S. W. under
its Freetrade policy is wholly dependent on an influx of
money from abroad. The exports don’t show a sufficient
surplus over the imports to cover interest on foreign
indebtedness, and, when the periodical loan isn’t being
floated, the stock of money in the province depreciates
to a dangerous extent. Since 1893 Victoria has borrowed
nothing abroad, and since October, 1895, N.S.AY., until the
present loan was floated, only raised £452,000, apart
from borrowings for conversion purposes, and not count-
ing subscriptions from other provinces to her local loans.
The stock of coin in both provinces slumped badly
through the stoppage of the usual influx of borrowed
capital, but N.S.W had far the worst slump.
Sept., ’95. Sept. ’97. Decline.
Coin in Vic. banks .. £7,776,980 £7,342,096 £434,884
Coin in N.S.W. banks.. 7,313,399 5,928,670 1,384,729
And yet N.S.W. borrowed £452,000 abroad to keep the
pot boiling. The present 1£ million loan is apparently
floated, not for necessary and immediate public works,
but to prevent the coin in the province getting down
to panic point. It is necessary, of course, to invent
works as an excuse for the loan, but the real trouble
seems to be that N.S.W. is importing too much and ex-
porting too little. And it must either borrow (which
is easy and convenient), or burst (which is hell), or
economise (which means a serious diminution of the
“marvellous prosperity” of the Foreign-trade regime, as
shown by the trade returns).
* * * *
Westralia obtained its ,£1,000,000 3 per cent,
loan at £96 6s. 4d., which, after allowing for accrued
interest and expenses, probably means about £94 15s.
This is a very great drop from the record figure obtained
for the ’96 loan, yet considering the incredible manner in
which Westralia is piling up its debt there will probably
be a still worse fall ahead. In the last two years Wes-
tralia has been blewing borrowed money three times
harder than the worst previous Australian record, and
some previous Australian records have “ laid over ” every-
thing else the world ever saw. And by way of postscript
to its big loan, Westralia’s revenue for the last quarter
was ‘ 695,092, and expenditure £955,784. Also, for the
last 12 months the revenue has been £2,843,175, and ex-
penditure £3,236,044; shortage, £392,869.
* * * *
According to the N.S.W. Public Service Board’s
report, the miserable Superannuation Fund is now
diminishing at the rate of £70,400 per annum, so in seven
years it will become extinct. And then Government will
either have to put up the needful money, or stop payment
of the promised pensions and shunt the ancient ex-civil
servant into the Benevolent Asylum.
• * * •
The Trustees, Executors and Agency Co. (Mel-
lbourne) shows a profit for the half-year ended 3112 ’97 of
£1705, as compared with £1756 at June, ’97 ; £1779 at
Dec., ’96 ; £1702 at June, ’96 ; and £256 at Dec., ’95.
(Consequently the net results have not improved in the last
18 months. The usual 5 per cent div. gets away with
£l5OO, leaving a balance of £205. The earnings of the
business are £lOO more than they were last half-year, and
receipts from interest on investments have improved £3B,
so things are really improving, only an unfortunate rise
in the expenses has swallowed up the increase for this
half-year. Two matters seem to keep the nose of this un-
fortunate institution perpetually at the grindstone. It
owns a leasehold property which cost it last half-year
£327 more than it produced in rent. Also, there is a
mortgage of £35,000 on the co’s property, and it has
£35,000 invested with which to pay off the liability when
it falls due, but the interest it receives was last half-year
£2BB less than the interest it had to pay. These two dead
losses swallow up very regularly £615 or £620 per*'half-
year—equal to 2 per cent per annum dividend ; and
when they are got rid of, some time in the dim future,
things should improve materially. The Trustees, Ex.
and Agency Co. still says nothing about any writing-off to
cover depreciation of its £32,000 invested in deposits and
shares of the Australian Deposit and Mortgage Bank,
which very much depreciated item continues to appear at
its wholly bogus face value. There is now £4975 at credit
of profit and loss, even after paying the dividend, and
without trenching on the £40,000 reserve, and the board
should certainly apply this towards writing down the
sham value of its unlucky investment. There is nothing
to be gained by keeping inflated paper-values in the
balance-sheet.

The New Zealand Insurance Co. comes out
rathe! badly—in part as the result of the Melbourne fire—
though not nearly so badly as it might have done. Net
premiums (£283,629) are £344 less than last year. Interest
and rents (£18,845) are £54 less. The net profit for the 12
months is £11,074, so the insurance business has not only
yielded no profit but has swallowed up over £7700 of the
interest earnings. The co. pays adividend of 11 j per cent.
(£22,500) so it ends the year with £11,426 less than it had
at the beginning, and the balance at credit of profit and
loss goes down from £14,159 to * 2733. To a co. like the
New Zealand, however, with £200,000 paid-up capital and
£235,000 reserve this doesn’t matter much for once in a
way.

Melbourne Argus, in a recent article on the
horrors and unprofitableness of State fire insurance,
pointed out that even the prosperous Victoria Insurance
’Co., taking its whole life through, had only earned a
steady 7-\ percent, on capital, and it asks the State how
it would like to do business on these terms. Let’s see !
If the State took over the whole fire-insurance business of
the province, and its capital bore the same relationship to
the amount of premiums as the Victoria’s does, it would
probably start the department with a nominal capital of
£600,000. The capital needn’t cost it anything ;it could
simply deposit £600,000 of State securities with the
management to be realised upon if necessary, and it is
10,000 to lit never would be necessary. At 7j per cent,
the State would make £43,500 a year. In 15 years the
accumulation, with interest, would reach over £1,000,000.
And the Argus warns the State against falling into this
horrible condition. Great Gaisar!

Holdar M. : “ Writer wandered up to the Syd-
ney (Barrack-st.) Savings Bank ae recent hot morning to
draw the interest on a mm that had unfortunately
vanished during the year. The money was needed to ap-
pease a petulant landlady, and, as he walked along,
this scribe conjured pleasant visions of that dame’s
delight in taking delivery of his cash. He further
imagined a gaily-garbed, beaming young bank official on
the alert to attend to the matter. These fond anticipa-
tions, however, were violently outraged as Dibbs’s
treasure-house hove in view. Around the door of that
stronghold raged a dusty, clamoring mob, unable to effect
an entrance while the adjacent atmosphere fairly hummed
with ‘ blanky.’ Inside was packed a perspiring populace
apparently demanding to know the amount of its interest
—which had just been assessed. Dishevelled clerks with
a hunted look in their eyes essayed to stem the torrent of
impatient humanity, but their efforts were mostly futile.
Aggressive, robust women, hugging dirty-colored bank-
books in an advanced stage of decomposition, refused to
regard Augustus’s appeal. Others instituted irrelevant
inquiries at the wrong counter, and shrieked in general
defiance, while captious children rushed round trying to
locate their angry parents. Damshicago’s soothing
presence was badly needed, but he didn’t appear.”

* * * •
House-agents assert that the custom is becoming
general among the working classes in Sydney and suburbs
to discontinue paying rent from the first week they fall
out of employment. An additional grievance is that a
growing percentage of the same class decline to leave the
houses without the formal notice, the court notice and the
ejectment order being served on them.

The bill in terms of which the Maoriland Govt,
has bought the right to the cyanide process for £lO,OOO
passed the Leg. Council and then became law on Dec. 21.
The royalty to be charged by Govt, is not to be less than
1£ nor more than 2) per cent, of the value of the gold or
Silver won. And,, when the sum received in royalties
equals the £lO,OOO paid to the patentees the process will
be free to anyone to use. A good way out of the trouble.

* * * •
The much-boomed Sulphide Corporation’s zinc
extraction (Ashcroft’s) process has fizzled out, and it is
doubtful whether even the smelting portion of the ex-
ceedingly costly works at Newcastle will be used or
whether the concentrates from the mine—the Central—
will not be shipped to Europe like Block 10’s, the
British’s, and others. The Sulphide Corporation has got
rid of £650,000 (£500,000 was subscribed and £150,000
raised since), and of that all but £250,000 has been spent
on works—now it would seem mostly useless. The
Central, enormous though its ore bodies are, will have to
strain to get along under such a load of dead-horse as that.
* * * *
During 1897 the Broken Hill mines swallowed
17,846,000 superficial feet of timber —99 per cent, of it
Oregon, from America. The open-cuts were going to
reduce the consumption of timber immensely ; but they
haven’t. Instead, there was a big increase from
12,626,000 superficial feet in ’96. All hope of substituting
N.B.W. hardwood, to some extent at any rate, has not yet
been abandoned. In Westralia, too, Zeb. Lane and a
party have been in the jarrah country getting filled up
with facts and other things with a view to displacing the
soft wood in the Kalgoorlie shows if figures pan out all
right.
• * * *
British B.H. directors in their last report just
out from England announce that they have decided to
keep up a reserve-fund of £15,000. Also they announce
that “ a process invented by Mr. Cowper Coles for the
extraction of zinc and all other metals,” which has been
under observation for two years, promises so well that
they “ have thought it right to furnish some assistance
from the co.’s resources in testing its value on a business
scale, and they have individually also subscribed for the
same purpose.” The experiments are not concluded yet.
The net profit of the co. appears to have been something
like £12,000 —that was, though, only to the end of June
last. As heretofore the directors adopt the excellent
practice of setting out the stocks, &c., wherein the reserve
is invested and the rate of interest yielded.

The Broken Hill paper rejoices because that
town’s exports last year were worth £1,950,000, and its
imports £1,260,000, while a whole European kingdom,
Servia, figures for £1,840,000 and £1,395,000; respectively.
Also, Broken Hill’s “foreign trade” was about a third
greater than all Tasmania’s. And, finally, the output was
worth within a trifle of twice as much as the gold product
of all N.S.W., according to Mint returns.

In 10 years the Baker’s Creek mine, N.S.W.,
produced gold to the value of £570,000, of which £252,000
was paid in divs. Last year’s gold, £43,000 ; divs.,
£15,000. Remarkable that the most consistent div.-payer
in the province should be working small and erratic veins
that few experts thought would live when first opened.
* * * *
Disappointed, if not disgusted, shareholders
continue to be advised that the North Nymagee, N.S.W.,
is all right as a copper-mine, and are referred to the
“ very encouraging reports ” from the manager. The
last is a little too sensational : “ The ground sunk through
has a very kindly appearance for copper, and shall not be
surprised to strike a body of ore at any time. Only two
men have been employed in No. 4 shaft, consequently
very little sinking has been done. They have been en-
gaged in preparing a place to erect the windlass.” Booh !
N.S.W. Mines-Minister Smith takes credit for
the expeditious execution of gold-leases. A much greater
reproach is the time taken to clear the title of a N.S.W.
mining lease once it is disputed. The writer is told that
the Homeward Bound owners have just got their sa. lease
back after five years’ delay. At the time when the co.
was negotiating in London for £40,000 additional work-
ing capital, a cable came intimating that the property had
been forfeited simply because an agent had neglected to
pay the year’s rent—£s. Negotiation at once ceased,
and the representative in London, knowing what an effect
the truth would have on London investment in N.S. W.
mines, to save the province, explained that the news he
had received from the mine was so unsatisfactory that he
could not accept the offers made. He returned to N.S.W.
where litigation began and only recently ended. Mean-
while, mine and plant, on which over £40,000 has been
spent, lay idle. » * *

An inch of rain seems to have quite changed the
policy of the directors of the Great Western, Oobar. Be-
fore, it was to erect a cyanide plant, sink a new shaft, and
do other very desirable things. Now, a small div. has
been declared and crushing, and the old policy of tearing
things out by the hair of the head has been resumed.
• * * *
The plant for treating ore from the Overflow
mine, N.S.W., by cyanide is well forward, and a start will
be made in a few weeks. The share-market is down to
zero, so also is the enthusiasm of the squatters who largely
own and control this show, though their faith remains un-
changed. If the mine has been properly sampled—how
few N.S.W. mines are! —the yield should be about Joz.
per ton.

The Camden Exploration syndicate, repre-
sented in N.S.W. by John Howell, is wet-nursing the Mt.
Kimo gold-mines, in the prospectus of which an esti-
mated annual profit of £97,500 is mentioned, and reference
made to the existence of lloz. tack. Purchase-price
£120,000, cash £lO,OOO, working capital £30,000. The D.
T. London correspondent says it is about time Howell re-
turned to London to be banquetted at the Savoy, photo-
graphed and interviewed after the manner of certain
other mining men whose names are well-known though
not given. Nothing would kill Howell quicker than a
series of Savoy dinners, followed by interviews and pic-
tures.

The Victorian gold-yield for 1897 is returned as
812,7650 z best since ’B2. Gippsland is the main
cause of the continuous improvement of the past six
years, the opening up of that hitherto almost inaccessible
country by track-cutting having led to a lot of small dis-
coveries. Yet to anyone who has experienced the horrors
of one of Turner’s new Gippsland tracks it doesn’t seem
much to be thankful for.
* * * •
Average Bendigo balance-sheet. —The Clarence
United : Receipts (mostly in this case from calls), —£2586.
Expenditure—management, directors and auditors’ fees,
£B2; advertising, printing, stationery, postages, and
office charges, £24 ; all the rest wages, tools, and other
things which represent genuine work.
A party of alluvial prospectors recently put
through six trial lots of some tons each, taken from quite
a number of places and consisting of gravel and stonings
from several of the oldest and richest worked-out alluvial
claims of Ballarat. The average was just a fraction below
3Jdwt. per ton, and that should leave a good profit on ex-
tensive handling. But to get the true average of debris
so scattered is generally very difficult, and requires careful
and extensive sampling by an expert at the business.
• * * •
The 40-head battery at Menzies Barossa mine,
near Gawler (S.A.), will be ready for work at the end of
January and crushing will then begin at once. This is, at
present, the only S.A. gold-mine being worked on a really
systematic and comprehensive scale, and many hopes
cluster round it.
* * * *
S.A. shareholders in the Eveleen (Northern Ter-
ritory) silver-mine have got cold comfort out of the in-
quiries from the co.’s liquidator in Sydney.—The assets,
consisting of mining plant, ore, &c., which the directors in
the reconstruction prospectus valued at £lO,OOO, were un-
accounted for ; the debts admitted amounted to about
£2400 ; the liquidator had made a ss. call on 26,000 shares,
which had produced £1950, out of which he had paid ss.
in the pound to creditors ; after allowing for the assets in
hand and the costs and expenses yet to be incurred an
amount of £1686 would be required, and to meet this a
call of 7s. per share had been authorised by the Court m
Sidney and made. And what a boom the Eveleen
boom was a few years ago !
• • * *
Plans of the Mount Lyell field are epidemic
just now, and J. B. Were and Sons have issued one as a
supplement to their monthly Stock Exchange Intelli-
gence. The Lyell district, as shown on paper, is more
beautiful than'Joseph’s coat of many colors, which his
brethren sprinkled with the blood of a kid. This simile
by way of suggesting that a little kid-juice, sprinkled
here and there on the map, would not be out of place.
* * * *
“Mount Lyell Kelly’s promise to give .£5OO
towards cutting a track to Port Davey providing the Tas.
Govt, did ditto, is a pretty safe promise—for him,” writes
a Tas. correspondent to The Bulletin. “In the first
place, the Govt, of the Isle of Morpheus is so sick of look-
ing for tracks, and cutting tracks, and hearing about
tracks, and paying for tracks that aren’t worth a rap, that
anything like a decent sheep-walk to the above-named
God-and-man-forsaken spot won’t be started this century.
Secondly, when you get there, well, you get there, and
that’s about all vou can say ; and then the only thing to
be done is to return by the track, or build a big smoke
and wait for a half-foundered steamer to take you off ;
for if you attempt to proceed you will starve on dead sea-
slugs, or eat your mate’s dog, or himself, arid eventually
roll yourself up in sea-weed and die miserably. And
thirdly—what’s the good of a track to Port Davey, any-
how ? ”
* • * *
Booming alleged copper-mines anywhere in the
Mt. Lyell district is still the rage in Melbourne, where
many persons will surely be singed before the year ends.
Some good mines outside the big one will be found, but
some palpable duffers have already a big market-value.
Nothing sickens the genuine investor more than a visit to
the field to find that the block in which he has thrown
away his money is 2000 ft. higher or lower than the next
lease, on the strength of which it was traded off.

» * * *
W.A. Mines Minister Wittenoom has resigned
the directorship of the Fremantle Smelting co. “He
says he was not aware that the co. held any mineral
leases at the time he consented to become a director.”
Perhaps next time Mr. Wittenoom is asked to become a
director he will read the prospectus.
• * * *
“ The Premier having got news of the flotation
of the W.A. loan, has given instructions that the Cool-
gardie water-scheme be proceeded with at once.” It will
take five years at least to finish. That, and the cost, are
the two great certainties of the scheme. Its success is
not assured. Some of the biggest mining engineers in
Australia still hold the view that it would be safer,
quicker, and otherwise better, to spend less than half the
money this gigantic work will involve in surface-water
conservation, even if it is found necessary to cement the
dams against absorption, and to cover them in against
evaporation. * * *
“I should say about 100 out of the 8000 on the
field are doing well on the cement here” (Mulgarrie,
W.A.), writes a Bulletin correspondent, “and there is
no doubt some of the claims are sensationally rich. I
know a claim where they took over 300 oz. in a day. Pity
the stuff isn’t spread over more of the field, as it would be
better for the majority if 10U0 men were getting 4oz. each
a week than if 100 were getting 400 z.”

How the imported manager dies off in the per-
fumed West! To the long list has been just added 0. T.

* * • *
Woodw-ard, ex-Govt. Geologist of Westralia, re-
gards latest developments at the Collie Coalfield as very
cheering. The Govt, is putting down a bore, and lately
passed through, beginning at 15ft. from the surface, 7ft.
of weathered coal, 4ft. of shale and 9ft. Cin. of bituminous
coal, of poor quality. A Ift. seam of fairly-good stuff has,
however, been found in a similar shallow sinking on the
Collie Proprietary, and prospecting is being hurried on.
* * * *
If the returns sent east as coming from the
alluvial claims at Kanowna, W.A., are correct, the outlook
for alluvial mining in that camp is bright. In the Donegal
claim it is reported that they came across some pheno-
menally rich cement, and the gold from one day’s work
weighed 3370 z. Another claim is said to be returning
lib. of gold daily. Another got 3350 z. for a week, and 75
tons crushed from one cement claim gave 3240 z. Also
officially reported that the cement and washdirt at grass
will yield several thousand oz.
* * • *
The latest Kalgoerlie surprise comes from the
North Boulder. Said that an old dump was crushed to
get it out of the road. The first return was an oz. to the
ton. “As the dump was got into it increased to 30z.,
and at present the stone is worth 9oz. per ton ; the tail-
ings from the mill averaging 4oz. per ton. The level from
which it came is being prospected, no stone approaching
these figures having been seen in the oxidised region
since the co. was floated.” How some men must curse
their luck and ignorance in connection with the early
days of Hannan’s ! They were looking for rough gold in
white quartz—such as was being got elsewhere —and
never thought of prospecting what they regarded as
country. And the eventual discovery of the richness of
these formations was more the result of accident than
knowledge.
9 * * *
The first lot of Bayley’s tailings having cyanided
30dwt. per ton at a cost of 125., a Coolgardie paper
reckons that the 10,000 tons on the dump should return a
profit of £40,000. The writer will bet they don’t. There
are few dumps in Australia of even value, and fewer still
in which the gold-contents are so uneven as in Bayley’s
heap. Some thousands of tons were crushed for a less
number of dwts. to the ton than some hundreds of tons
went ounces. It is not the usual practice anywhere, es-
pecially in Westralia, to start any kind of plant on the
worst stuff. If the heap averages 15dwt., the co. will do
well.
• * * *
“ The number of shares in the Great Boulder is
1,700,000 and the value of each share is about £1 3s. od.,
so that the rise and fall of Is. a share increases or de-
creases the value of the property by * 85,000. These
figures seem tremendous, and, had the position been fore-
cast three years ago, pessimists would have laughed to
scorn the idea of such aconsummation.” —W.A. paper. And
there are a few other mines in the country, to a degree,
like it. But allusion is seldom made to the awful number
of Westralian alleged mines in respect to which the pen-
dulum has swung the other way.
» * * *
From all accounts, adventurers going to Klon-
dyke this year will find the diggings much easier of access
than the pioneers found them. Several projects for im-
proving the route by land have started. There will be no
lack of steamers, and other arrangements for wholesale
transportation are well forward. Obviously, the steam-
boat co’s are impressed with the prospect that nine-tenths
of the people who go in with the spring will have to come
out with the fall; hence it means carrying them both
ways. It will have to be a very fine field if it turns out as
much gold this year as men will have to turn out of their
pockets in reaching it. Whatever the end, the booming
of the Yukon this year is a certainty.
• » * •
America leads again. In 1891 the Michigan
Legislature enacted that all railway companies«working
in the State must sell 1000 mile tickets, good for any
member of purchaser’s family and available for two years,
if the 1000 coupons were not exhausted earlier, for 20dols.
(£4) each. The companies refused, and fought, and
appealed to the State Supreme Court, but latest advices
tell that the Court upheld the law and verdicted against
the companies. Exactly the same process has been gone
through in New York State with the same result.

It is only the crudeness, even yet, of our
civilisation which could cause such surprise that
that N.S.W. politician, while about to take unto
himself a fresh spouse, should have allowed the
divorced one to still address him as “Dear .Joe.”
In the fact that two people can’t, or won’t, or
shouldn’t, continue to each other in the marital
relation, the world, as it grows wiser, will see no
cause why they shouldn’t remain acquaintances,
or even friends ; neither is there any solid reason
why a second conjugal partner should execrate
either the person or the memory of a first. The
present writer knew a second wife who used with
her husband to make periodic pilgrimage to the
tomb of No. 1, and dutifully help him to heave
the sigh and shed the tributary tear. “ Make the
betht of it, thir, and not the wortlit,” said
Dickens’s lisping showman ; “ and, when we’ve
thaid that, I think we’ve exhauthted the philo-
thophy of the thubject ! ”

A terrible suspicion takes shape in the
minds of the Yic. people that Gov. Brassey is not
the infallible tarry navigator and resourceful A.B.
that he has been cracked up to be. T. B. never
takes his precious Sunbeam out of the mill-pond
into open sea without running foul of some jagged
end of creation, or butting into the mud some-
where, or breaking his barque’s knees, or other-
wise disabling his gaudy toy. When H. E. is
cruising, his faithful subjects are continually being
distressed by cables to the effect that the Sunbeam

breakfasts thou dost spoil !
* * * *
And now listen to the Herald in hysterics
—the old lady has not printed anything so earnest
since her five-column—or was it 15-column? —
ravings over the beautiful, delightful, dazzling,
sparkling, glittering, majestic uniform of that
gallant captain at the historical monkey-soap
wedding :
Listen to the roar of her [Royal Arthur’s] cannon !
What is all that outburst of cheering ! that feverish en-
thusiasm at the appearance of a steamer coming
up the harbor! A man-of-war the flagship! the
biggest fighter afloat in the southern seas ! “ Hurrah !
hurrah ! for the Royal Arthur ! ” they shout. The mul-
titude who were down on Sunday to meet her, and who
wouldn’t miss seeingher for the half-millionof gold.she cost,
were here again yesterday. The shouts of welcome grew
louder, the hurrahs more frequent, until this amplification
of Herculean power in the form of steel chooses to stop
coming on towards the mass of human faces that line the
shore. Ah ! “ Britannia rules the waves.” That’s the
secret. Rules them in peace and in war, and the war-
ships keep the peace. And while the choruses of welcome
from the shore continue, can be heard high above all the
singing of the tars, “For England, home, and duty,” as
the haughty Royal Arthur swings with the tide at her
moorings.
“The amplification of Herculean power in the
form of steel.” Immortal!
* * * »
Many Sydney johnnies would be astonished
to learn how little their tailors really have to do
with the supply of vestiture beyond taking mea-
surements, looking agreeable, and ultimately
dunning them for cash. “ Order ” clothing is
made up in factories on distinctly “ slop ” lines
at the instance of many well-known _ outfitters.
Since the advent of the two-guinea suit-to-order,
several factories have taken exclusively to this
branch of business, on the sub-contracting system.
After subtracting percentages, the suave shopman
passes the job along to a factory, and the proprie-
tor of the latter establishment gives it over to the
“ boss ” of a table, who makes his profit out of the
toil of five-bob-a-week apprentices and improvers,
* * . *. , , ,
Mayor M Eacharn is going to make his
Federation banquet a very big affair. His is the
only big guzzle yet announced. Gov. Brassey is
simply going to give a hop, and is glad to get off
so cheaply ; whilst Premier Turner has chartered
one of the bay excursion boats for a federal
“ blow ” at half-a-crown per head or thereabouts,
But Mayor M‘Eacharn, being opulent, is going to
blaze out, so that the popping of corks may be
expected to affect the earthquake-recorder at the
Observatory, and the sizzle of cooking flesh will
make the people in Mars sniff the air. At least,
from the way Corporation people talk, these
things may be expected to occur.
Australia is a great country right enough.
On the same day last week Brisbane (Q.) was
almost under water; several towns; in north-
western N.S.W. were nearly drowned out; several
more in south-western N.S.W. were going round
with their aggregate tongue hanging out two feet
in the last stage of water-famine; and almost
every settlement in Eastern Gippsland (Vic.) was
fighting for its life against stupendous bush fires,
and coining off very badly in the conflict.
* * *
The Adelaide hospital rumpus revived
again the other day. Dr. Morrison, one of the
lately-imported juniors, resigned, owing to quarrels
with the resident staff, and made definite charges
of malpractice. This was a chance not to be
missed by the Ass. crowd, and the Register did
its best to stir up public indignation. But the
third test cricket match happened along, and the
public, in the heat of the discussion as to whether
Howell ought to displace M‘Kibbin in the Aus-
tralian XI., refused to be indignant. Had Dr.
Morrison been a great bowler or batsman, the
Kingston Ministry would probably have had to
resign in favor of one formed by G. Giffen and
Ben Wardill; but it wasn’t so, and this latest
little trouble therefore died before it was even
weaned.
* » * *
A new form of oath. At Cairns recently
a Japanese witness of Buddhist persuasion de-
clined to take the oath in the usual manner, and
a sked for a glass of water. On receiving it he
murmured, “ If I lie may I die, and be punished.”
He then sipped at the water and commenced to
give evidence. This makes about the fortieth
form of hanky-panky used in Bananaland before
you perjure yourself.

wl ey so wrought upon the canny

Sandy was visibly drooping more and

with his card of membership to tha

maimber o’ the soceeity, I’m entitled to
a refund o’ the admeosion-chairge I paid
this mornin’; sae I’ll joost trouble ye
for the sum o’ one shullin’.”

* * • *
The most paralysing spectacle which has
happened this century to the little town of Mudgee
(N.S.W.) was when Premier Reid went to get his
hair cut, with two armed troopers for an escort.
Mudgee is a quiet town, dreaming on a grassy flat
somewhere by the shores of the moaning Cudge-
gong—the river with the large, fat, oily prawns
in it. The streets are a Sabbath day’s journey
across, and the low houses on the other side need
to be looked for with a telescope. There is grass
in most of these streets —also goats. And down
one of these silent thoroughfares came the terrify-
ing spectacle of Premier Reid, escorted by two
huge armed swashbucklers, going to get his hair
diminished by the threepenny barber. Nobody ever
needed an armed escort in peaceful, sleepy Mudgee,
and the vision of anybody going with a powerful
military following to have his hair cut anywhere
is about as weird as that of a general going out in
full uniform, with his staff around him and a
battery of artillery behind, to get a medical
opinion about his corn. Has Geo. Reid got the
sense of humor with which he is commonly
credited ? What man with a real sense pf humor
would select Mudgee as a place on which to ad-
vance with a strong armed force, and have his
head trimmed and nicely oiled, and perhaps singed ?
AT THE TEST-MATCH.
VOICE FROM CROWD : “ Hi, Ranji, loosen yer
collar — you're going black in the face! ”
Another case, in N.S.W., of a 'woman
masquerading as a man. A celebrated instance
in Yic. was complicated by the discovery, after
the woman’s death, that she had been married, as
a man, for many years. People found an easy
explanation till startled by the announcement
that there had been a child. Of course they could
only conclude then that the wife was a man mas-
querading as a woman.

THE SONG OF THE SCORCHER.
(With the Usual Apologies.)
(To A.M.N.).
There’s a legion that never is listed
(Except now and then at the court),
Who drive City Fathers distracted
By setting all bye-laws at nought.
The fathers who bred us and reared us
With order and decency crammed,
But we give the go-by to their bye-laws
To learn how to scorch and be d d,
Dear boys—
To learn how to scorch and be d d.
So some of us chivvy the slavey
Who takes the kids out in the pram.,
And some of us go for the ag6d,
And some of us shave past a t ram ;
We’ve most of us tackled a cripple,
We’ve all of us scorched through the town,
But the one we regard as our hero
Is he who once ran a Judge down.
The roadway is rough and may jolt us,
The asphalt is easy to ride,
And why should the mighty tin-wheeler
Give place to pedestrian pride ?
The whole of the road was our portion,
As many unfortunates tell,
But, Lord, we have fought for and earned it—
The right to the footpath as well.
Then a health, and don’t drink it in whispers,
To our wholly unauthorised horde ;
Here’s death to each decent civilian
By the “ cads-upon-castors ” abroad ;
For we know though the summonses issue
And we’re filed on the criminal shelves,
Yet the legion that hasn’t been listed
Will send us as good as ourselves,
Dear boys,
Ten thousand as good as ourselves.
Melb.
R. K. DEE.

BRISBANE, QUEENSLAND.
One of the consequences of the vice-
regal system is the snobbish prominence
given to a number of small officials imported
from England as aides, secretaries, etc.
Recent S.M. Herald paragraph
Captain Sloane-Stanley yesterday left Sydney
for the Blue Mountains, where he will be the
guest of Sir Frederick Darley for a few days.
Who is the captain with the hyphen, any-
how?

On the value of the average man’s visual
evidence. T’other day a Rockhampton cab-driver
noticed a sack containing a body floating about in
the Eitzroy. He also saw, quite distinctly, the
face of the man whose body occupied that sack.
Then the police fished out the sack and found in-
side the carcase of a dead dog. . . . Saw it with
my own eyes—l tell you !
* * * *
Piety in the lump is a dreadful dose for a
young country. Bananaland has lately run riot
sabbatically. ’ In Ipswich, about a month ago, a
young man was fined for whistling outside a
church during service. Whereupon, Townsville,
seized with holy emulation, arrested a poor old
woman who happened to pursue a stray hen into
the churchyard while the godly were a-praying,
brought her up before the beak and fined her
three pounds. The hen cackled abominably just
when the congregation got off their knees to sing
a hymn, and the whole thing fell flat. Hence the
indignation. Also, it was just about 105° in the
shade, and somebody had to suffer.
“Peeping Tom,” writes “0” to The
Bulletin, “is a familar type, but the female of
the breed is rapidly developing. The other day
at Sandringham (V.) I came upon four girls up
a tree overlooking the men’s baths, and they
seemed to be enjoying themselves. By-the-bye,
the Government Gazette annually prints a
warning to men not to bathe or stray in the
vicinity of ladies’ baths, or other bathing-place,
but no notice is taken of the possibility of women
wandering close to men’s ditto. Last summer a
couple of other fellows and myself were bathing in
the open sea at a secluded spot, when two females
came and squatted themselves within a dozen
yards of our clothes. They didn’t shift either
when, after getting tired of staying up to our necks
in water for an hour, we came out and dressed.
* * * *
N.S.W. postal authorities have just warned
departmental officials that all persons sending
telegrams must personally affix the stamps to the
message-form, and that while any single deviation
from the rule will be visited with a fine, repeated
breaches thereof may mean dismissal. And, as
no damping-sponge or roller is provided by the
department, the public will have meekly to put
out its tongue and salivate its own stamps under
the haughty supervision of its own servant behind
the counter.

may yet be a social thunderclap.

C.M.G. : “Please, can you tell me why it
is that one woman, or two, or a dozen, can squat
on the sands, as near as possible to the open
palings of a ‘ for-men-only ’ baths, and glare
steadily over the top of a penny sentimental at a
host of nude males disporting in the briny, with-
out giving the law the least concern, whereas a
man, as soon as he is found bringing an inflamed
and inquiring optic on an opening in the outer-
wall of a ladies’ wash-house, is grabbed by a dis-
gusted constable, and given a month, or so ?
Do you remember that when a couple of
Melbourne athletes were fined for bathing on
a quiet bit of beach beyond St. Kilda Bluff, it
was a female witness who declared she could not
go away ‘ because she had the perambulator with
her’?”

* * * *
At Melb. District Court, the other day, a
brmaid charged unlawful assault and impolite
language against a fishmonger to whom she was
returning a decomposed crayfish. Extraordinary
fact (writes a man who seems to know all about
it) that all Hebes have a devouring passion for
crayfish, although, in 19 cases out of 20, they are
martyrs to indigestion. And yet not so extra-
ordinary. Internal anguish often gives an inter-
esting, not to say poetic, turn to their mechanical
smile. There is more than one authenticated case
of a barmaid wringing an offer of marriage from
a sympathetic customer by means of a gentle
melancholy which she had readied up for the
occasion. Half a crayfish and two cold saveloys,
taken at six o’clock with a cup of tea, sealed the
fate of Gussie when he came two hours later,
bringing a spray of flowers for his divinity.
“ Acute indigestion is commonly mistaken for
heart disease in the sufferer,” says a modern
medical authority. Well, Gustus feels certain
that the windy, spasmed darling is dying of love
for him.
9 * * *
‘ ‘ Thousands of carcases have been float-
ing down the river lately,” writes a Bourke
(N.S.W.) correspondent. He adds : “We drink
river water.” Also, in Lake Speculation, near
Menindie, lower down the river, the fish in the
body of naturally-dammed-up water there have
been dying by thousands. The suggested cause is
the concentrated mineral salts in the much-re-
duced and fast-evaporating water. Anyway, the
lake stinks horribly, and stock will not approach
it. And this was the lake which was to supply
Broken Hill with an unlimited drinking supply !

The pair" 1 proximity of the lock-up cells
to Brisbane police-court gives rise occasionally to
most irreverent interludes. Just as Constable
O’Hooligan is deposing, with all due solemnity,
“ He had a bottle o’ whisky under his arrum, yer
worship,” a voice will break in from the adjoining
limbo : “ Sargint, ye divil, give us a drink and
we’ll kiss yez.” Then the majesty of that court
could be packed in a thimble.
* » » *
The other day at Ballarat a vigorous
bench sentenced a 15-year-old boy to £lO or
seven days for firing stones from a shanghai,
though “no one was hurt.” Same day in Mel-
bourne the leader of a “ push ” was convicted of
breaking in the front door of a hotel because he
was refused drinks on Sunday, knocking the land-
lady senseless on the floor, and attacking the
licensee. He also, according to the finding of the
court, committed perjury on a large scale in con-
ducting his defence. The bench said it was a
“ very gross ” assault, and fined him £2, also £1
10s. damages and £1 10s. costs. Five pounds in
all looks pretty cheap for bashing a woman insen-
sible, wrecking her teeth, and partly wrecking
her house as well, especially as the other five or
six ruffians got off gratis. What would the bill
have been had the case been only “moderately
gross ? ”

tigers with distinct advantage to the future com-
munity.

N. : “ Mrs. Gruncly is murdering an in-
fant, in a wooden baby-farm consisting of two

the ‘ poor little child of sin.’ Yet look at all the
miserable legitimates walking about, howling for
the graveyard.”

T ADY offers Home and 7s. weekly to experienced
Governess, advanced subjects, Eng. preferred.
K.D., Dailv Telegraph.
•yy ANTED, Cook, 20s.
This isn’t as bad as it looks in .hkl print, because
the cook (at 20 shillings) is up at cold dawn
lighting the fire for breakfast (unless the house-
maid lights it), while the governess (at seven
shillings) comes down, much later (unless she has
to be on the scene at 6 a.m. to hear the brats
scuttle through their music lesson), to eat it.
Also the cook says “ Yes’m,” while the governess
calls her “ Jane.” And it’s worth much more than
the extra 13 shillings a week, to the average
woman, to be in a position to say “ Jane.”
• • • *
“ The electrophone is coming into exten-
sive use here,” writes The Bulletin’s London
correspondent. “It is only available for tele-
phone-subscribers, any of whom may have it con-
nected with their houses per telephone-line, on
giving 48 hours’ notice, for a moderate fee. All
the chief theatres, music-halls, concert-rooms, and
churches are on tap, and on the wintry, slushy,
splashy, bone-chilling nights it is a godsend. On
Sunday mornings you can lie a-bed and say ‘ hear
hear ’ to the preacher as he slangs the church-
truants from his wooden perch. You can hear a
ballad at the Queen’s Hall, laugh at Robey—the
comic star just now—at the ‘ Pav.,’ drop in at the
‘ Circus Girl,’ have a bit of Jap. at ‘ The Geisha,’
dodge a coruscated cornetist at the Empire, miss
a scraggy Alhambra ballet, take a composite dose
of the ‘Scarlet Feather,’ and follow Ada Colley’s
vocal acrobatics on the high trapeze, all the while
seated by the family hearth in your ‘bananas’
coloring a clay. A novel hearing, startling in its
coincidence and contrast, I had on Saturday even-
ing when, as the applause broke out after Ada
Crossley’s song in Langham Place, the Empire
was switched in with Ada Colley la-la-ing the
‘ Cavalleria ’ intermezzo. It was a hint to voice-
judges. Had one of the singers heard it, she
would be ‘ a pot of tea and a toasted crumpet?
Yes, sir,’ in a ‘ A.8.C.-shop ’ now.”

* * * *
London Saturday Review discourses on
Australia’s old friend Lord William Nevill, an
absconder from England in connection with the
bills which cost the notorious money-lender Sam
Lewis .£ll,OOO :
Lord William Nevill is the fourth son of the Marquess
of Abergavenny. Those who know the family will admit
that while Lord William Nevill was not the weakest
member of it, yet Lord William Nevill himself was never
distinguished for strength or health of body or of mind.
In person he was very tall ; about six feet in height and
painfully thin. Even in the prime of life, say at 25 or 30,
he had no physical strength ; he was, indeed, extremely,
even painfully, delicate. He seemed to be always very
young; there was not iti him the strength of manhood.
Accordingly when his friends heard, some ten years ago,
that he had fallen in love with Miss Murrieta, and that
at the same time he had turned Catholic, they smiled in-
voluntarily. It was so like Willie Nevill to assume the
religion of his betrothed. For some inconceivable
reason this amiable weakness was said to have
excited Lord Abergavenny’s wrath. When the
marriage came off every one said that Lord Wil-
liam Nevill had done very well for himself; and he had
in every sense. The young < ouple went into a very nice
house in Brechin Place. S.W., and for some time knew no
more of the cares of life than are to be met with by those
who live in London with a great name, good position and
most ample income. It was rumored that the Murrietas
supplied the income and furnished the house,
hut had also paid off Lord William’s youthful debts. But
in a moment fortune changed, and the gay pleasure skiff
had to face comparatively rough weather. It never, we
suppose, struck Lord Itevelstoke when as head of Barings’
he went “ nap ” on Argentine securities that his rashness
would bring Lord W. Nevill to ruin. When the house of
Baring fell it dragged down with it the house of Mur-
rieta, and Lord and Lady William Nevill had to face the
world without that infinitely expansible tri-monthy
cheque. Even then to a less fortunate man the prospect
would not have seemed gloomy. The bearer of a great
name in England does not usually find it very difficult to
make money, and Lord William, as the evidence in the
trial showed, found it easier than most. Mr. Sam Lewis
declared that Lord William had had £'90,000 from him in
less than three years ; that one young gentleman alone,
having backed bills for Lord William to the tune of
£40,000, paid like a man and made no complaint. Pre-
sently it was said that Lord William had become a sort
of social guide and sponsor to an Australian millionaire,
and had received a cheque for £5OOO for bringing a Prince
to the colonial’s dinner-table. Now we know that such
cheques were of the nature of windfalls, and that his real
banker was Mr. Samuel Lewis.
THE DEVOUTLY THANKFUL LOVER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
So Nell was married yesterday !
Let’s fill a bumper mellow,
And drain it to old Hymen’s sway—
And to the lucky fellow.
Time was when I was “ gone ” on her :
When each day I’d discover
Fresh charms to make my pulses stir,
And- fool-like—act the lover.
Her eyes were bright as stars at night,
Her lips were like to coral,
And Nell was, in her lover’s sight,
As beautiful as moral.
But now with joy we drink Ms health,
Whom Nell did most prefer,
And wish him lots of luck and wealth
Who’s lately married her.
I loved—for Nell was fair and tall,
And sweet as fragrant clover —•
But now I love her most of all
Because—she threw me over.
THE BREAKER.
A SPRIG OF MINT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A sprig of mint with homely meanings fraught,
A savoury thing, a kitchen herb, and yet
When ’twixt my idle fingers I have caught
A leaf and pressed it, straight I am beset
By haunting childish memories. On a wet
And fragrant bank of mint I lie ; athwart
The creek the snowy ti-tree blooms are met
And fleets of fallen petals are in port;
An elfin charm is filtered through the air,
A hum of cloistered gnats in emerald cells,
And drowsy chantings that the gadflies share.
And this sad heart, that on a sudden swells
At the remembered fragrance, was it there,
And did it lie and dream as memory tells?
