A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, January 18, 1898.
Mr Dear Mtee—
A haughty silence reigns over most of Sydney’s
suburban mansions this month while their usual
occupiers are away fighting flames in Tasmania, or
flies in the country. No high-toned town family
would own up to living in its own luxurious
crib overlooking the ’Arbor all the year round ; to
do so would be to “go under” socially at once.
And so the foolish wealthy troop away in droves
from their comfortable homes with cool, lofty
rooms and shady gardens, and squeeze themselves
into high-rented, stuffy, hot country cottages
with an outlook of dreary gum-trees and burnt
grass ; where the mosquitoes worry inhabitants
all night, and the visitors paralyse the oldest resi-
dents by never wearing anythingbut store-clothes ;
and bore each other at deadly-dull little shivoos,
the exact copies (plus flies and heat) of what they
endure all the year round in town ; where ice is
beyond the dream of avarice and the butter is
always oily ; where the fly dieth not, and the
beetle is never quenched. And yet hundreds of
women endure this discomfort annually, and drag
their miserable families into it too, and are repaid
for all the expenditure if their toad-eating is re-
warded by being “ called on ” by some Naicer
people than they know in their own suburban
street.
Girls who enthusiastically planked down their
subscriptions in the cause of Science, and joined
the recent confabs, are now gratefully feeling that
they have had their money’s worth. Besides all
the talkee-talkee in the tents, and the entertain-
ments, as promised, there have been a concert, an
all-day excursion, and a harbor-trip or two thrown
in. The Royal Society entertained the Associa-
tion for the Advancement, &0., at the University,
on Friday evening last, and the party was a vast
success. The Great Hall was filled with an inte-
resting collection of scientific parlor-tricks, at
which a throng of bewildered guests gazed and
tried to look intelligent. An explanatory expert
in swallow-tails arose at intervals by each exhibit,
and explained the wonders of his section. Some
men were realistically seeing snakes in one corner
all the evening; a calculating machine looked
clever, but was confusing to outsiders. To most,
telegraphing without wires was the really interest-
ing exhibit. Mr. Pollock, who was in charge of the
apparatus, was followed by groups every time he
discoursed on the clicking and buzzing invention.
At all the lectures the intelligent questioner was
to the fore, and at the telegraphing display he
was rampant, being an excited person who thought
he was going to see an X-ray show. Intermittent
lantern-views with explanatory remarks were given
in an adjacent hall, which guests found after
walking through groves of fairy lights. Astrono-
mer Russell spoke a piece on star-photographs
illustrated by pictures thrown on the sheet, in
which the spangled firmament on high in sections
looked as if it had broken out into a bad attack of
measles; and the lecturer pointed out, with a long
pole, an extra large measle in the corner which
he knew by sight, and named everything from
Alpha to Omega, and the audience applauded as
if it had known the speck intimately from
childhood. Prof. David talked of coral-boring,
and showed views and specimens of S. Pacific
items, and the intelligent questioner—a lady this
time —came out strong and bored the professor
instead of the coral. The gowning was almost
unanimously black, but two or three satins shone
out amidst the sable hue ; one woman wore flow-
ing yellow drapery which flopped around her like
silk dusters on a clothes-line.
The Herald artlessly remarks m its Monday’s
account of the Scientific picnic to National Park
on Saturday: “ Those who preferred to roam
about and admire the beauties of the Park—and
these embraced by far the larger number of those
present —joined in excursions.” Being embraced
by the beauties of the National Park has caused
a lot of unpleasant notoriety lately. After that
insinuation it comes quite natural to read that the
expeditionists are to visit the Blue Mountains.
The Park, by the way, is one of the chiefest
honeymoon resorts —the Accommodation House
at Audiey is never without a newly-wedded pair
or two. The place offers ideal honeymoon attrac-
tions, as it combines the charm of intense aloof-
ness with a cheap and easy return trip to town in
case boredom sets in. But the Saturday-to-Mon-
clay trippers are the chief residents of the beauty-
spots of Port Hacking river, and, owing to the
advt. the place has lately had, they are flocking
there now in large crowds.
“Lewis Carroll,” whose death is cabled, will be
better remembered than the Rev. Chas. Dodgson,
not only by children, but by grown-ups who
remember “ Alice" and “Through the Looking
o}lass.” Those two juvenile classics are like
oysters in their appeal to the taste; just as you
either hanker exceedingly after oysters or are
utterly indifferent to them, so children either fall
in love with Alice and take the Griffin and the
Mock Turtle and the White Green and Bill the
Lizard and the Walrus and the Carpenter into
their hearts and make friends of them for life, or
else they can’t take kindly at all to them and feel
rather spiteful towards them. It is curious that a
Common love of “ Alice ” forms a bond between
two people who haven’t read the book for years.
A Melb. girl, daughter of D. D. Wheeler, ex-
Hansard chief, once owed a rattling good time to
her knowledge of Lookmg-Glass land. It was
when Rudyard Kipling was ip Melb. ; he was
visiting their house, and she quoted something
from “Wonderland.” “She knows h«r Alice,”
said R.K. delightedly, and invited the whule
part}' to the theatre and a small tea-party after,
gave Miss W. a copy of “ Plain Tales,” inscribed
with a parody on “ The Walrus and the Carpen-
ter,” and the touching quotation “ It was the best
butter! ”
The impressiveness of a recent Woollahra wed-
ding was badly curtailed owing to the non-arrival
of the bridal flowers. After waiting for the
florist’s nian to nut in an appearance, the party
was obliged to drive off without the garden-stuff
except a substitute hastily faked up from the
house-garden for the bride. In the midst of the
wilt-thou-have-this-woman an unabashed small
boy with a basket stumped along the aisle and
said loudly, “ Here’s the bokay ! ” Amid a general
giggle somebody took the flowers, and the boy
stumped out again. In a little while the irre-
verent youth returned with a brown-paper box
and went up again to the altar rails remarking
abruptly, “ Here’s the button-holes ! ” The flovver-
menial was so unconscious that he was breaking
up holy matrimony that there was nothing for it
but to take the “ bokays ” and distribute them on
the spot, while the choir sang “ The voice that
breathed.”
The other day at Red fern a polite man ap-
proached a solitary woman struggling under the
weight of a large tin box, and obligingly offered to
carry it into the train for her. She consented.
The kind gentleman disappeared into the crowd
and hasn’t since been seen. Now the lady is
wildly advertising in the lost-column for the
gentleman to return her property “to save further
trouble.” When will women cease to believe in
the polite (mostly meaning the aggressive) man ?
I know of one case in which a person on the prowl
to succor beauty in distress was badly beaten.
Beauty, unable to see a cab, was carrying what
the’Murkans call a gripsack, much heavier than
it looked, to Milson’s Point jetty. The prowler
drew near with an elaborate wave of his hat and
asked if he might assist her. “Yes, certainly,”
she promptly said, and put into his astonished
hand her luggage. His object was to “do a bear-
up,” but she, a few paces in the rear, turned a deaf
ear to all his polite remarks, and the discom-
fited male had to trudge along Campbell-street
perspiring under his porter’s work with the decep-
tive gripsack, until he dumped it sulkily down on
the ferry, and the business-like young woman
added insult to injury by taking out a neat purse
and cold-bloodedly offering him sixpence.
There is in Sydney a Foundling Hospital which
acts the Good Samaritan in an unobtrusive way to
as many nameless infants as it can afford to sup-
port. Until recently it was at Bel voir House,
Strawberry Hills, but has lately shifted camp to
Lane Cove-road, North Sydney. The ladies’ com-
mittee, including Mrs. Mackin, Mrs. Jas. Toohey,
Mrs. Wiles, and the hon. sec. (Miss O’Brien), and
others, met last week to discuss a new building,
as the temporary present home is crowded out.
The babies in the house now number about 30,
and the plan of the promoters is to rouse the
mothers’ interest in their offspring by keeping
mother and child together. Experienced found-
ling-authorities say that the mothers.are usually
young, of miserable physique, seldom capable of
either nursing or earning for their children, who
are wizened little creatures needing much coaxing
to enable them to keep their slender hold on life.
This Foundling Hospital committee struggles
against much coid-shouldering from self-righteous
folk, who loftily snort on all such as “ a fostering
■>f vice.”
The Women’s Suffrage League is becoming a
strong enough factor to be courted by politicians.
At the Protestant Hall meeting the leader of the
Opposition made a bid for feminine favor by in-
viting representatives of the worrying sisters on
to the platform, and they applauded whenever the
Cause was advocated by the speaker. But I
never knew a woman yet who was a protectionist.
Freetrade means cheap gloves. So, if the fran-
chise should come in under a Lyne regime, he may
only expect the ungrateful sex to round on him
and vote for anybody who promises them
made-in-Germany shoes. The up-to-date woman
claps but she doesn’t cheer publicly yet, and the
published statement that there were “cheers from
the sec. of the League ” brought a statement from
that lady that she had done no more than tap her
gloves together in a lady-like way. Miss Rose
Scott, the sec. aforesaid, is a dignified, intellectual
lady whom it would be as profane to imagine
hooraying shrilly on a platform as it would be to
picture His Ex. doing handsprings to amuse a
house-party.
The Premier, in all his peregrinations lately,
has been anxiously posing as a domesticated man,
trotting out “my wife” and “my children” in
every speech, and having the former in tow at
every function as a guarantee of good faith. “ We
are a happy family, we are, we are, we are.” This
dinning of domestic unitedness has only to be re-
iterated loud and long enough, thinks the wily
George, and it will make everybody oblivious of
that little well-advertised domestic unpleasantness
in the Cabinet circle.
In the recent chess contests between city and
country clubs played in Sydney, only one chess-
woman was counted in as an expert at that
lengthy and complicated game. She is Mrs.
Houlding, small and bright, wears glasses, and
hails from Narandera ; though on the losing side,
she won in single combat on the chequered board.
Naturally she is from the country. No city
woman would have the long hours of leisure neces-
sary for her to learn to move her knights and
pawns to victory.
A scion of “ one of the Nicest ” passed through
a very distressing experience during last Christmas
week. He and his mother were invited to form
two of a toney house-party at the Hon. Mrs.
Blank’s mountain residence. Previous to de-
parture, Algernon had become financially embar-
rassed to an unwonted extent. “The old man”
was not to be approached, and Algernon “ popped ”
his best dress-suit to meet a pressing need. Then
cash eventuated, and it was “ got out ” in time for
the visit. But “Uncle” had by some accident
left still pinned on. It was at
Mrs. that the fond mother
(a well - meaning clumsy person) remarked
to Algernon, When T took your dress-suit out
of the portmanteau laso night 1 found such a
funny little ticket with a nno, on it pinned to
the coat. Whats that for? nd Algernon,
with a creepy sensation along nis replied
“ Oh ! you see, at the last University barkp it* was
so hot, you know—a lot of our fellows letiKa ur
coats with the doorkeeper—put tickets on ’etn,
you know.” Then there was a pause, while
several people looked hard at Algernon. The
fond mother, with a puzzled expression, presently
resumed, “ Yes; but, Algernon, there was a
ticket on the trousers, too ! ” And Algernon
loudly demanded the butter.
The marriage of Miss Kathleen F. N. Lockhart,
daughter of a well-known border resident, the late
Norman P. Lockhart, of Albury, with Mr. Chas.
Nelson, of Pt. Piper-road, took place on Jan. 13,
at St. Joseph’s, Woollahra. The bride was arrayed
in the orthodox white satin, with an extra long
train, carried by a small pink-clad girl, and some
diamond fixings. Four girls, also in white, with
large and feathery hats, followed the bride, who
was handed over to the happy man by her brother,
Mr. Norman Lockhart, of the Landaninkumtax
Dept. Mr. Lysaght, of Wollongong, supported
the bridegroom through the ordeal, and, after the
ceremony, the wedding feast was held at the house
of the bride’s mother.
Allan’s Musical Magazine No. 2 has gone
into a third edition with a good variety of assorted
alleged songs, as sung by Tivoli favorites, and is
first in the field with the newest craze, “Susie-ue,”
Ada Reeves’s song, arranged as a jerky dance-
measure. The newest idea for dance music is to
get a popular tune—in any time—and fit the hop
to it, calling it a “ Washington Post ” dance. The
“ Village Blacksmith ” appears once more. There
must exist at present a wild craving on the part
of the public for that horny-handed son of toil, for
he stands under his spreading chestnut in every
songbook published during the festive season.
At high-toned weddings now rice is never
thrown. Confetti are the smart substitute for the
vulgar vegetable product—they are tiny, circular
flakes of all-colored tissue-paper, which flutter
elegantly on the newly-wedded pairs in high life,
and are blown off by the lightest breeze, conse-
quently they don’t give the bride and bridegroom
away by tumbling out of their clothes all along the
route.
Three Australian faces decorate a recent number
of the ’Murkan magazine, Munsky’s. In a com-
petition with the photo’ed features of charmers
from both sides of the North Atlantic, the pictures
of youth and beauty from this end of the earth
come out much on top. The daughter-in-law of
Judge Holroyd, of Melbourne, is shown in saint-
like profile ; the other two are Daisy Holly, the
pretty heroine of many gorgeous ballets, and a
halo-hatted Miss Louise Daly, also of Melb.,
hitherto unknown to fame. A confused but com-
plimentary par. in the accompanying letterpress
credits much of the boasted “British beauty’’
visible at the dead-and-gone jubilee to the visiting
womankind from “S. Africa, N.S. Wales, and
Australia.” The “and” sort of puts us outside
the pale. I suppose the Munsky scribe takes this
province to be an island in the S. Pacific, bounded
on the north by a rabbit-proof fence, and on the
other three sides by our ’arbor.
A few Australian “society” journals now
frankly head their social weekly record of hole-
and-corner suburban jumble sales and bun-strug-
gles with the statement that such items are
inserted at so much per inch. This looks like the
thin end of the wedge for bursting up the small-
beer chronicle of social doings altogether except
as an advertising column. One of the pests of
every newspaper-office which sheds the dignity of
print on twopenny-halfpenny festivities is the self-
advertiser, who chronicles her own parties in
manv-adjectived rapture, and sends a copy of the
highly flattering account to each of the papers.
The woman with periodical parties, which are
monotonously recorded month by month in almost
identical words, is a weariness to thousands of
long-suffering “constant readers;” also, the
lady who was received by London’s Mayor
some time ago, and energetically wrote out for
every paper the thrilling episode of her meeting
with His Washup.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“One of these days when Melbourne has re-
covered its lost population, and lots of people are
looking for larger villas to spend more money in,
we shall have a crop of female house-and-estate
agents, who will convert the rooms off the shop
into a servants’ registry-office. The two lines of
business worked by one management ought to be
quite as profitable as a tea-room, anyhow. Three
women opened a house-agency and servant-supply
stores in London, some months ago, and they were
being rushed by customers when last heard of.
For any nice girl, not too young, whose thoughts
incline to widowers in easy circumstances, the
new idea looks promising, because poor bereaved
creatures are likely to be leaving the place where
sainted Maria breathed her last, and why not con-
sult a sympathetic agent in skirts re the change of
residence? If she strikes him when his grief is
hot she may make a deep impression.
“Just now, a muggy, self-important city of
Central Bananaland is all agog over a coming
wedding of unheard-of magnificence. A local
magnate, the largest by a long chalk, has at last
given the signal for his daughter to join in the
bonds, after a long series of forbidden engage-
ments, rumored “ affairs,” filial mutinies and
parental riot acts.
“The New Man, of whom you see crowds on
the Block, attires himself in a little straw hat
with colored ribbons, broad colored sweater, no
waistcoat, big pants turned up, tan boots, and big
hook stick hanging on his arm ; also he wears a
clean shave d la Wilson Barrett.
“ The story goes that an Australian professional
lady, who, some time ago, was understood to be
marrying a man of considerable wealth, con-
cluded her honeymoon by voyaging with her
husband to his native land, only to find that
he wasn’t her husband at all, but that of
another lady who met them on the wharf with
a string of children in one hand and a threat of
bigamy proceedings in the other. Under the cir-
cumstances, the bridegroom bade the artiste a
long farewell and returned to the bosom of his
family, while the shapely bride —went to work
again.

their little shins badly against Maggie Moore, as
Dick Whittington. One urchin was well-up in
the history of puss-loving Richard beforehand,
but, when the stage-hero appeared, the youngster’s
jaw dropped, and he looked as if he had found

44 They do say that the deafness, or selfishness,
shown by a Westralian potentate when he went
up-country lately to open a mine, or railway, or
something, was absolutely horrid. A suite of
rooms in a local magnate’s mansion had been
devoted to the visitor’s use, and when a ball was
given in his honor, one of these apartments was
unavoidably taken over and arranged as a supper-
room. Early in the evening, so I’m told, a most
awful thing happened. The principal guest got
tired or bored, and went and locked himself up in
his ‘suite,’ thus barricading, horrible to relate,
that crucially-important chamber—the banquet-
ting-hall. And nothing on earth could induce
him to open the door. Certainly he afterwards
said he was in bed and asleep, and, as it’happened,
there were two or three* rooms between his inner
dormitory and the hungering multitude outside,
which may have deadened the sound. Despite
all the commotion, he gave no sign then or
afterwards, even expressing no surprise or curiosity
next morning at breakfast when his hosts
lamented excitedly aboutjthe over-night horrors of
the scratch refreshments, gathered in at the last
minute from byways and hedges, so to speak.
And such a collation as wasted its sweetness on
prison air, just through that martinet’s lack of
consideration, has rarely been catered for, even in
the Golden West. Ice and fruit and goodness
knows what, from 4 out of the everywhere into the
here.’

as you.”

Frank Rogers, Q.C., the new N.S.W.
District Court Judge, was the first to take an
LL.B. degree from Sydney University—over 30
years ago.
Bread-or-Blood Vosper is already a director
of a Collie W.A. coal company, and chairman of
directors of a brewery. Such is the demoralising
effect of politics.

on the Australian station—is in charge of Com-
mander F. P. H. P. W. Freeman. More than a
fifth of the alphabet !

“ Westralia,” The Bulletin’s now gener-
ally-accepted word, has fallen under the displea-
sure of A. J. Wilson, of the Investors’ Review.
The word, he remarks, is only less abominable
than Westralian mines.
Admiral Pearson, who revisited Sydney
Town Hall the other day, remembers standing on
the site of the Hall before the foundation-stone
was laid. He was then a lightly-built young blue-
jacket, a few years out of his teens.
Dean Marryat (Adelaide) is now 71, and is
showing the weight of years. The Bishop is still
away in England, and he has on his shoulders all
the burden of the diocese with no extra pay to
compensate him. The Dean is a nephew of Capt.
Marryat (the naval novelist); his brother was an
admiral, while his sisters married Governor Fox-
Young, of S.A., and Dr, Short, Adelaide’s first
bishop.
From Maoriland Govt.-whip Mill’s paper .
The deceased [R. B. Foote, a very old settler], who leaves
a family of ten to mourn their loss, was a gentleman
Holding the highest appreciation of reverence, and de-
voted himself to inculcating the spirit of altitude of at-
tainment in all who had the honor and pleasure of his
acquaintance.
One of George IV.’s mistresses, and her
family, resided in old Hobart Town. Her family
had nothing to do with her husband, a
gentleman who had taken a thousand or two to
accept the royal leavings, and bring ’em to this
part of the world. He was a little, weazened man
with a fixed smile and slinking walk. But the
lady and her sons and daughters ! Ye gods, they
were royal ■ Splendid animals ! Showing how it
would be possible to perpetuate the nuisance by
cross-breeding. Mrs. , like her royal para-
mour, had a taste for drink. She was generally
fuddled, but always royal. Once, in St. David’s
Cathedral, Parson Bedford was denouncing the
vices of the upper classes. “ Quite right, Parson
Bedford,” cried the dame ; “ look at me ! Cast
off, when I ought to be settin’ on a throne, with a
crown on my ’ead. ” She was a fine woman, bub
Cockney-bred.
An Adelaide correspondent :
May I add to yours re Fred May, the S.A. mechanicV'
genius ? At 23 he became superintendent-engineer of thte
immense Moonta mines. Built up Martin and Co , Ltd.,
of Gawler, and increased their workers from ICO to 350.
Left them and forged ahead with his brother on their own
firm of May Bros, and Co.—erecting the first portion
of the factory with his own hands. At his death (he died
suddenly) he employed more than 200 men, with a fort-
nightly wage of over i 900. Anan of tireless energy, and
perhaps unequalled in the Auslralias as a mechanical
engineer.
Boucaut, the new S.A. Knight, could have
been decorated long ago, but always neglected to
reply to vice-regal intimations. He practically
admits having said “ yes ” now to oblige the (in-
evitable) lady. They all “admit" that, or else
they say that the crime arose entirely through
drink. Boncaut is 66 years old, and was
Prefliier of the province £2 years ago, while he
appointed himself Judge in’7B. Way, two years
earlier, was Attorney-General under him, and so
claimed the succession to the late Chief Justice
Hanson.
Gov. Brassey recently stated in conversa-
tion that when the Vic. Governorship was offered
to him he was expressly told that the Colonial
Office did not wish him to be lavish. They said,
in fact, that Hopetoun had been far too expensive
a Governor, and had made it difficult for anyone
to follow him, and that they would be glad if
Brassey would tone things down. If this be true,
the Colonial Office people certainly knew their
man in selecting Brassey, who, people think, would,
if he could save money by it, call for tenders for
cutting his hair.
An unpretentious tragedy of the back-
blocks. Wentworth (N.S.W.) Standard office
was burned one evening during the hot spell. The
disaster proved such a blow to he proprietor, J.
B. Woodhead, that he was seized with paralysis
of the brain, and died the same night. The un-
fortunate little Standard was interesting, too.
It was Speaker Abbott’s perpetual trumpeter ; for
it the world moved round this great man. Still,
“barracking” for Joseph didn’t pay all the
bills ; wherefore between-times poor Woodhead
went into his tailor's shop next door and made
pants for Wentworth, irrespective of party and
creed.
A request was recently made by the
pious Maoris of the North Island that the
Diocesan Synod should include King Ma-
huta’s name in the Anglican prayer for the
Royal Family ! The cloth was horrified,
though the daily papers let the disappointed
brown oonvert down easily, merely saying
that “ the scheme fell through ! ”
Quite in keeping with the scanti-
ness of our knowledge about the living
personality of N.S.W. Gov. Phillip was the
difficulty in locating his last resting-place,
which has only recently been discovered
in the floor of Batharnpton village church,
near Bath (Eng.). The house, in that city
wherein Phillip died is said to be haunted
by a spectre in naval uniform, which
it is a pity cannot be imported to Aus-
tralia.
The recently-appointed Bishop of
New Guinea has just landed from ’Ome a
specially-indented laborer for his mission-
field. Interest, however, centres not so
much in the man as in the plant he has
brought with him, which is quite new.
Said plant consists of an up-to-date bicycle,
with an electric lamp— to be employed in
running the pastoral rounds. An interest-
ing item in the rev. gentleman’s kit is a line
of 2000 paper-collars, which will give him a
change a day for over five years, unless he
purposes to trade them off among his fellow-
shepherds, or present one to each nigger on
conversion.
William Gisborne, who died a few
days ago in London, aged 73, was a very
old Maoriland billet-holder, and dated back
beyond the days of Responsible Govt. He
also held a portfolio in Fox’s Ministry
from ’69 to ’72, and was in office for three
months under Grey, but as a Minister he
never amounted to much. Perhaps, if his
front name had been Donald, he might
have had a better chance to show what he
was capable of. In those days the chief
reason why there was any Ministry at all
in Maoriland was the necessity of finding a
portfolio and a salary for Donald M‘Lean.
Out of nine successive Cabinets—those of
Stafford, Waterhouse, and Pollen, Fox’s two
Cabinets, the two Governments of Vogel
and the two of Atkinson —old, commonplace
Donald M'Lean held a portfolio in eight,
and when he hadn’t a portfolio Donald Reid
had one instead. And poor Gisborne, being
mild and English and not Donald, was
quickly crowded out.
B.S. : “That par. from The Bul-
letin's London correspondent, stating that
Passmore Edwards is about to retire from
London Echo, recalls a curious nickname.
The Echo, some five years ago, only kept
one reporter on its staff, depending for
copy on contributions and news - agency
stuff. This one reporter was the smallest
man on the London press. He was worked
almost to death, and used to rush up at
the end of a meeting or a law-case and ask
other scribes for ‘ the bare result—just the
bare result.’ This habit, and his diminu-
tive size, led to his being called ‘ the Bare
Result.’ ‘Here comes the Bare Result’
used often to be heard in Fleet-street as
the little man in the green-black coat and
the shineless belltopper hurried along. By
the way, the first editor of the Echo was
Horace Voules, now Labouchere’s associate
and part proprietor of Truth, and the writer
of many of the smart things for which Labby
gets all the credit.”
PRACTICAL PALMISTRY.
SHE : “Is palmistry true?"
HE : “ Well, I should smile; I'll guarantee to tell
any girl's character by pressing her hand in
the dark. ”
SHE: “ But if she had any character she wouldn't
be there with you! ”
“Tom” Slattery’s ideal public man is
John Hubert Plunkett, the one-time N.S.W.
Solicitor-General, now in an unmarked grave in
Devonshire-street cemetery. Plunkett, who
came to Australia as the nominee of Daniel
O’Connell, fought stormily for r ligion as well as
for politics. Slattery watched over the last
years of Plunkett’s long-lived and lately-deceased
widow.
Bertram Mackennal has now been form-
ally commissioned to execute the Diamond Jubilee
statue of our Gracious Queen for Ballarat. Also,
“Sir Andrew Clarke and Captain Bridge, R.N.,
have consented to act as advisers in London.”
Poor Mackennal ! They will, presumably, drop
into his studio when he is at work on the Queen’s
face, and advise him to put a little more august
majesty into the royal countenance.
Fred M. Bailey, F.L.S., the Q. “Colonial
Botanist,” a scientist of high rank, gets .£3OO a
year—the same as entomologist Trvon, or Chief
Agricultural Clerk Scriven, or travelling dairy-
man Mahon ; just half the salary of fruit-expert
Benson, or of Under-Secretary Peter M‘Lean, a
bung politician; £450 less than agriculture-in-
structor Shelton. “ Some men get the luck.”
Pastor Fitchett got £5OO down from
Smith, Elder and Co. for the English rights of
his “Bleeds,” otherwise “Deeds that won the
Empire.” The Primitive Methodist blood-wal-
lower’s introduction to the firm was in this wise :
A copy of the Australian edition of his “ Bleeds ”
was sent to London Spectator, and J. St. Loe
Strachey got it for review. Shortly after, J. St.
L.S. was appointed editor of the Cornhill, and
reader for Smith Elder. He had so much liked
Fitchett’s “ Bleeds ” that he commissioned him to
write up the Battle of Minden for the Cornhill,
and recommended the publication of an English
edition of the “ Bleeds.”
Dear Bulletin,— Extract from the journal
(in my possession) of a well-known Tas. botanist
and F.L.S., extending oyer years 1847 to 1874
“ London, April 21, 1859. In the tea-room (Lin.
Soo.) after the meeting I met Dr. Cobbold, the
son of the Rev. Mr. Cobbold, who wrote the
account of Margaret Catch pole. He told me that
he tried to obtain the professor’s chair for natural
history at Melbourne, in the University. He said
that his father’s book was bought up and destroyed
by the Reibey family at Sydney—old Mrs. Reibey
being the heroine ; but that the book had become
more famous by its suppression, and a cheap
edition of 35,000 copies had recently been Dub-
lished.”—o. H. Read, Tas. V

not necessarily what they

marked the born leader of
men, which they don’t ?
then actor Mario Majerom
should be the present

cles keep on running the

Henry Dobson, of Tas-
mania, comes back to the Federal Con-
vention a burnt-out man and covered with
bruises through being tossed by a cow.
Dobson wasn’t a shining Liberal at his
last appearance, and being burnt-out and
run over by a cow are calculated to em-
bitter a man, and bitterness (in a Fat
Man) mostly leads to acute Toryism—if any-
thing can make Dobson more Tory than he
was before.
Prof. Marshall Hall is about again
after his long illness. He carries round a
black beard, which makes him look much
older, and something like a study after Velas-
quez. But his enthusiasm is undamped, and
he is busy writing music for a Greek play,
and “ tomerotic ” poems for the edification of
young ladies and club-smoking-rooms.
C.: “A friend lately applied for an adver-
tised Govt. Educational billet, and wasquestioned
by a certain Minister, who should be an authority
on such matters. But amongst the applicant’s
references was one from Dr. Benson, late Arch-
bishop of Canterbury, and that spoiled his chance,
because, as the Minister remarked, “if this is
from the Archbishop of Canterbury, and you say
his name was Benson, why does he sign himself
‘Edward Cantuar’?”

That able criminal lawyer, Mr. F. E,
Rogers, Q.C., who is being appointed a N.S.W.
District Court Judge, is, like most born humorists,
of a somewhat rueful countenance —with the
underlying comedy, however, easily discoverable
upon a closer inspection. It must have been the
humorous aspect of the features, at all events,
which induced “Moonlight,” the bushranger, to
complain during his trial for his life that Mr.
Rogers was laughing at his unfortunate situation;
a supposition which greatly pained Rogers—about
the last man in the world to make mock of
misery. Mr. Rogers resigned his <£2ooo per
annum Chairmanship of the Land Court because
Government refused to give him a Supreme
Court Judge’s pension-status, but it is very possible
that his numerous friends may yet have the grati-
fication of seeing him upon the Supreme Court
Bench.

Dear Bulletin, —The white parent of a
half-caste Kanaka, who distinguished himself in a
recent shipwreck, is an ex-sea-captain now living
in Sydney, and who, many years ago, was in
charge of a ship carrying some convicts—whither,
‘or whence, history sayeth not. The convicts
mutinied, murdered the crew, and ordered the
captain to navigate them to the islands, and, being
a prudent man, he did so. When satisfied as to
their course, the convicts deliberated, decided that
he had behaved himself well, and put him ashore
on the first large island they came to. He was a
musician and took his violin with him. A
threatening crowd of savages greeted his arrival,
but Orpheus played to them till they thought him
a god ; brought him unlimited pigs and yams, and
bowed in adoration. Finally, he married the
chief’B daughter; succeeded him, and ruled the
island for years, till a ship called in and he sailed
away—leaving, as was natural, traces of his stay
in the lighter complexions of the rising generation.
—S.K.

It is satisfactory to learn that Joshua
Bros’. Boomerang Brandy is meeting the same
favor in Australasia that it does in Great Britain
and Europe. For the month of Dec., ’97, this
brandy more than doubled its sales for any
previous month in this country, and with the
splendid record and recommendation that it now
has to its credit it should go on “doubling” till
time eternal.*
Sands and M‘Dougall’s “ Melb. Directory”
for 1898 is a massive volume, clothed in crimson
and gold, and containing 1516 p.p. of solid read-
ing matter, likewise a map of Melb. and suburbs
up to date. Moreover, The Bulletin, after long
and patient research, has discovered one feature of
new and special interest in this valuable array of
reading matter. It sets apart a page for the new
addresses of all firms and individuals burnt out by
the late conflagration, the number of victims
being 97.

Aboriginalities.
Dear Bulletin, —On my way to Manilla,
during harvesting, I stayed one night at a
selector’s place a cßorab. Knowing the people, I
was, after partaking of a good meal, invited into
the best parlor, newly decorated and carpeted,
“ for Christmas. ” Shortly afterwards a boundary-
rider called, and he also was asked inside. His
hungry-looking cattle-dog, Spot, followed at his
heels and slunk down near his owner. Pretty
soon “ Clancy ” lit his pipe, began puffing vigor-
ously, and, as a matter of course, spitting
vehemently. Mrs. looked tragically un-
comfortable, and gazed at Spot, his owner’ and
her brand-new carpet in evident alarm. She
stood it bravely for about a quarter of an hour,
and then, mastering her nerve for a mighty effort,
said : “ Tom, would you mind—er— spitting in the
fireplace ? That—er—carpet was only- put down
—yesterday.” “ I’m not spittin’ on the floor,
ma’am,” said Clancy, quickly and quite seriously—
“ Pm spittin’ on Spot l ” —Steele Penn.

On the great fly-bite question :
Dear Bulletin, — Re “ R.H.’s ” statement in “ Aborigin-
alities,” of Jan. Ist, that a fortune awaits the man who
discovers a cure or preventive for fiy-bunged-eyes, I
can recommend a treatment which I first saw tried in
Cambridge Gulf country, the fatherland of flies, and have
since repeatedly used upon myself. The moment the
warning itch is felt, place a speck of moistened tobacco—
not much larger than a pin-head, in whichever corner of
the eyg— almost invariably the inner one—the itch is felt.
Leave the tobacco in the eye for a moment or two]
and the itch and swelling will be gone by the time
the brief irritation caused by the tobacco has ceased. This
cure, it might be said, is almost as bad as the disease, but
the sharp momentary smarting of the tobacco is infinitely
preferable to the lingering irritation of “ bung-eye,” to
say nothing of a swelling which frequently produces com-
plete temporary blindness. As to the fly that does the
damage it is, as far as can be judged by the naked eye,
exactly like the ordinary house-fly, but smaller. In Kim-
berley (W.A.) it stings the lips and back of the hand as
well as the eye, producing similar effects. I have more
than once seen a fly sting my hand, and felt him too for
that matter, hence I speak positively. — A.P.B.D.

A reminis-
cence : Sonne years
ago, on taking
charge of a thresh-
ing-machine, I
noticed amongst
the crew a middle-
aged man of ex-
clusive, not to say
aristocratic, de-
meanour. A ripen-
ing acquaintance
drew from this
Edwards the in-
formation that he
had two daughters
of rare beauty and
desi rableness—
the elder one re-
cently married to
a respectable and
prosperous gar-
dener, the second
fancy-free. Being
younger then than
now, and consti-
tutionally impres-
sionable, I diplo-
matically seized
an opportunity of
appointing Ed-
wards to the captaincy of the elevators, vice
Red Mick on the razzle-dazzle. I found myself
putting on my best behaviour while in the old
gentleman’s presence, and indulging considerably
in day-dreams. But one day, while the machine
was stopped for lunch, a Chinaman came up at
the double, and, without taking Edwards aside,
informed him of a grievous accident which had
befallen his son in-law, the envoy’s brother. It
appeared that the unfortunate Sling Muck, camped
with his load of vegetables at some distance from
home, had turned in tor the night under his
spring-cart ( not on top of the damp, lumpy,
scrunching cabbagee, as this inexperienced paper
would be prone to represent); when the vehicle
tipped, breaking both the botanist’s legs, and
holding him in that tight place for some hours.
Welly ni\ The appreciative Aryans received
these tidings with approval; and a few days
later saw the captaincy of the elevators resumed
by Red Mick, vice Marmaduke Edwards, retired.
—Tom Collins.

Writes “ Steff Cassander ” :
About four miles from Wellington (M.L.) is the town-
ship of Karori, or Dismal Town, as visitors have dubbed
it. The houses and “ farms ” are stuck about on top of
hills or down in gullies, and the scattered place is only
approached by a very hilly road. The same lugubrious
families have resided there for the past 30 years, scarcely
any new settlers ever coming that way. The ground is so
poor that they have to bring their own horse-feed from
town. To add to its festive appearance, Wellington has
recently laid out its new cemetery on the most imposing-
looking hill of the lot, and there are already quite a
number of marble monuments sticking out of the dried
soil. Goods are carried to Karori along with the passen-
gers by the daily coach —a conveyance of very ancient
design. One trip out to this township I sat alongside the
driver ; he would only talk of the people who were buried
and the dimensions of their funerals. He had been
driving this coach for 20 years, and the new cemetery had
got possession of all his thoughts. However, as he had
various goods to deliver,-he gave me a rest now and again.
He had to pull up and leave a tennis-racquet at the school-
master’s, a sausage-machine at the post-office, a package
that looked like two bottles containing something good at
another citizen's (Karori is a prohibition district), a small
tombstone at the aforementioned graveyard, and a dried
barracouta at. the blacksmith’s. I was glad when he got
rid of the tombstone, anyhow.

Editor Bulletin, — On the Denison dig-
gings, in ’6l, we had pegged out four men’s ground
on Welshman’s reef. Dave and Sam went up the
mountain to cut a few logs to put round the
bottom of the tent, and “Bony Bill” stayed in
camp to make a damper. Got it mixed up on a
sheet of bark about two feet from the fire, and had
the ashes all ready, when—crash ! —a 10ft. stringy -
bark log came end on, spearing down the moun-
tain, then bump against a tree, and rolled
broadside on over the round ball of dough, flatten-
ing it out; then another half-turn, with damper
stuck to the log, just landed it in the hole in the
fire that was prepared for it. “ Blow me,” says
Bill, as the log rolled on into the creek, “ I never
thought Dave would think I wanted a rolling-pin
to make a damper.”—R.F.
Dear Bulletin, — Re the story
about the aboriginal black who could
not only track cats, but distinguish
one cat’s track from another. In
1884 I was camped between Warrego
and Paroo Rivers (N.S.W.), and saw
a half-caste (Albert), about 20 years
old, who knew every one of his mas-
ter’s horses, 45 in number, by their
tracks. I had 20 horses in my party,
and Albert could, after a few weeks,
tell my horse-driver (an Australian,
born white, and a fair tracker) which
horse had gone along, by its tracks.
“ That one Farmer gone along there,”
he would say, and he was always
right. An adjacent tank - sinker
(Davis) had 36 horses, and Albert
could always distinguish between the
tracks of Davis’s horses and mine.
Mr. iWakefield Barton, of Wanaar-
ing, told me that, when Albert was
in his employ, he (Barton) lost a
mare for some months—supposed to
be stolen. Albert was late coming
back with horses one morning, and,
on being asked where he had been,
said “ After Blossom (the mare lost
months before) ; she gone along track
to Paroo.” This proved to be cor-
rect, and the mare was found. He
actually remembered the tracks of a
mare which he had not seen for about
half-a-vear. — H. W. Ford.
* * * •
A droughty - Northern - back-
blocks episode : For months we had
been scooping holes in the sand cf
the creek to get water. Then we had
to get supplies from a hole some
three miles away per water-cart. One
day there was rain a few miles up
creek, and our water-drayman, seeing
the creek rise, turned back without
filling his cask. At the crossing the
water rose into the dray, and, to keep
dry, our carrier got into the barrel.
But the water rose higher still,
floated the barrel out of the dray,
and nearly drowned the drayman.
From drought to drowning.
* • * *
Dear Bulletin, —My long experience on
the Darling teaches me that the numerous local
“looneys” didn’t originally get “ ratty ” there,
but, asaru’e, were mad when they first arrived
at that muddy-banked river. Cities and coast
towns also have plenty people with wheels in
their heads, but they put them into asylums
whenever possible. That is probably the main
reason why the Darling and her back-country so
attract lunatics. There they can wander about
undisturbedly, and talk to themselves, and their
dogs, and their billy-cans till they’re hoarse, with-
out interference. The number of cranks of all
sorts and from all parts of the earth hidden in
hollow logs, deserted huts, and bends of creeks
and rivers out back is simply astonishing, and
knocks Coghlan’s lunatic statistics clean out of
shape. The common belief in the country is that
everybody on the Darling is ‘’balmy,” and the
popular saying, “He’s from the Darling—he’s
‘got ’em,’” probably originates from the well-
known Darling-pea plant, which drives horses and
cattle crazy after eating it. And this recalls that
a lot more Darling peas grow' on the Macquarie
and Castlereagh (N.S. W.) rivers than ever grew on
the Darling, or anywhere else to my knowledge.
—6 xB.
* * * *
A Maclean (N.S.W.) beach-miner claims
a most remarkable recovery. He was felling
a tree in the bush when a limb struck him
on the head and laid him out. When he recovered
consciousness he made for the nearest house, ten
miles off, and then found that either his dates
were very much out or he had lain unconscious for
six days. It had rained heavily most of the time,
which was probably the reason why he wasn’t sun-
struck or moon-blinded, and why snakes and ants
didn’t get at him ; and his luck in not being dined
upon by crows was something phenomenal.

Apropos recent Bulletin yarn concerning
locking-up of an aboriginal to chop policeman’s
firewood. Writer knew a drunken tailor in a coast
town jf Bananaland who thus described the
method by which he was incarcerated. “You
see, boss, I can go on the booze forty bloomin’
times and never get into trouble. But the day
old Sergeant Murphy’s pants want repairin’, or
his Sunday coat gets out-at-elbows—then booze is
sure to land me in the jug. I got it reckoned
that the Murphy family wants patching up every
two months—and every two months I’m run in for
a week’s hard.

NEPHEW: “ Uncle, what do they call soft drinks?"
UNCLE: “ Oh, lemonade, ginger-beer, and that stuff!"
N.: “ And what do they call hard drinks?"
U. (with a reminiscent gurgle) : “ The drinks that I get on

get that I know of! ”

Right-thinking folks may sneer and snuffle,
but without “blanky ” the bush Australian is ab-
solutely lost. He can express more with that
little, innocent word than many people can in two
columns of high-flown technicalities. From an
old mate I once received the following letter :
Dear Dick, —Blank it!
Ever your cobber,
Bill Mayne.
I understood. I knew what poor Bill meant
better than if he’d filled in four pages.—R.H.

The Holy Roman Empire, said Yoltaire,
was so-called because it was neither holy nor
Roman, nor an empire; and “Parramatta lime-
juice,” according to the Melb. manufacturers
thereof, is so designated because it isn t limejuice
and isn’t made at Parramatta. The Bulletin,
for its part, fails > see any distinction between
vending citric-acid and-water as the juice of the
lime and any other way of obtaining money upon
false pretences.
M‘llwraith’s was the first Australian Pre-
mier’s voice raised against the general taxpayer
being saddled with the expense of Government
House balls. “Let those who dance there pay, ”
he said.
The two propeller-blades recently lost at
sea by the German s.s. Koenigin Luise weighed
each 2f tons; and as the metal is manganese-
bronze, some idea may be gained of the. money
put into these huge modern locomotive hotels.
The Australian Cycle and Motor Car, Ld.,
has made a move which will make itself felt
throughout the bicycle trade in Australasia, the
splendid American Humber being now retailed by
the firm at .£26 for ladies’ machines and <£2s for
gent’s.*
Great Stock Sales. —The numbers of
stock sold through the Homebush Yards for 1897
are over 113,000 cattle and over 2,596,000 sheep.
Of this number, Pitt, Son, and Badgery, Ltd.,
have sold 34,549 cattle and 531,239 sheep : the
next firm being 5614 cattle and 79,238 sheep less.
As this splendid position is the result of good con-
duct of business and best prices obtained, the
numerous constituents will be pleased at the above
figures.*

3/8. Senior, chemist, George-street, Sydney.*
Suffield is still in his living grave, and the

with all its feet to make him fit that grave.
Suffield is a victim not only of Windeyerism, but
of Government by Contradiction. Both N..S.\V.
political gangs, the ins and the outs, are interested
in justifying the actions of their respective Minis-
ters of Justice. A pamphlet fully dealing with

One In Every Five.
About one person in every five suffers from
some form of liver disease. Sallow complexion,
pain in right side or under shoulder-blades, coated
tongue, bitter taste in the mouth, flatulency, sour
stomach or indigestion. Warner’s Safe Cure is
not the only specific for indigestion, caused by a
sluggish liver, but it has effected many cures
where other so-called “ specifics ” have failed, and
of years standing. Mr. John Baird’s (Kennedy
Terrace, Brisbane) experience is an illustrative
instance of the value of this medicine, and in a
letter dated Nov. 27, 1897, he says: “Having
suffered for several years from indigestion, I was
induced to try Warner’s Safe Cure, and after
using about 12 bottles I felt like a new man. I
have advised many others to give it a trial with
equally satisfactory results, and confidently recom-
mend it to anyone suffering in a like manner.”


THE COMING RESURRECTION.
"Mr. Alfred Allen has decided to stand at the ensning N.S.W. general election and a committee has been formed to secure his return. Mr. D. O'Connor, M.L.C., was among thoae
on the platform at Mr. Barton's meeting on Wedaesday. Mr. John M'Elhone," M.P., writes: 'A number of electors of Fitzroy division have lately asked me if I would be a candidate at
the next general election. Ishall be a candidate for this division at the next election.' Tom Slattery has announced his intention of opposing Reid."

delicate sensitiveness of her baby’s pretty pink skin, and uses MINERVA. Why should adults be less wise in regard to them-
selves’ MINERVA SOAP maintains the natural texture of the skin, and, because of its rare medicinal properties, acts as an
antidote for stubborn prickly heat and itchings and chafings of all kinds. Of a peculiar, healthy aroma, which lasts right to the
end, it is an elegant looking article, and is, moreover, the greatest cleansing soap in the market.
Mr. W. A. DIXON, Registered Analytical and Consulting Chemist, Laboratory, 97 Pitt-street, Sydney, says“ I have
analysed several samples of Minerva Soap, and have to report that it is a superfatted, milled Soap, impregnated with soothing and
antiseptic medicaments. It is evidently made of the Purest Materials, and is Absolutely Neutral- that is, it does not contain any
free alkali such as is found in cheap soaps, and which, whilst it promotes the formation of a lather, renders the skin hare and dry.
For this reason Minerva Soap may be used with safety on the most delicate skin. It contains a very low percentage of water, and,
therefoi’e, a high percentage of actual soap—higher than any other I have examined.

WHO ARE THE HAPPY,
WHO ARE THE FREE P
*
«•
You tell me and I’ll tell thee:
Those who have tongues that never lie,
Truth on the lip, truth in the eye;
To Friend or to Foe,
To all above, and to all below.
These are the happy, these are the free,
So it be with thee and me.
What Higher Aim can Man attain than Conquest
over Human pain ?

POLITICAL
POINTS
The test matches come into serious conflict
with the’Federal Convention, which is struggling
hard to gain the attention of Young Australia.
If John Norton isn’t in next N.S.W. Par-
liament, it will be his own fault. For (say)
Waterloo, he would be a “moral”; rumor says
he has designs on Black's seat for Gipps.
It is muttered that, just before the general
election, a political bombshell, now being packed
and fused, will be exploded against a prominent
politician. Of course, there is a woman in it.
Two women, in fact.
“The managing committee of the Melb. Women’s
Hospital consists almost entirely of women, yet
women doctors find it almost impossible to secure a
footing in the institution. The appointments are
-made by ballot, and if there be in the field a male
doctor possessing what is called ‘a good appearance’
and pleasant manners, he may rely upon the support
of a majority of the lady managers. Within the last
few months three lady doctors of ability and unim-
peachable character have tendered their applications
for subordinate positions on the resident medical
staff of the institution, bid they have all been igno-
miniously beaten at the ballot box by the male can-
didates for the positions."— Ex.
According to S.M. Herald (10/l/’9B)
Tomjoe Byrnes, of Scalyland, declares that the
boundary-line cannot divide the interests of
Queensland and N.S. Wales. He will find that
the color-line will, though.
The details of a motion, pledging the
N.S.W. Govt, to reduce the duties on wines and
spirits, occupied the attention of a coterie of
Sydney publicans last week. Reid and Want v.
the holy joes of the Freetrade Cabinet is ex-
pected to be the issue.
Premier Reid, in his speech at Hamilton
(N.S.W.), said he was “the first Minister who
had pluc-k enough to put a 3 per cent, loan on the
market.” Which is wholly untrue; it was Premier
Seddon, of Maoriland, who floated the first 3 per
cent, loan, and Reid only followed in his wake.
Poor little Denmark was foolish to ask the
Powers (as per cable) for such a guarantee of per-
manent neutrality as Belgium’s. Belgium owes
hers to her position as point d’appu i for both
France and Germany, whereas no Power has any-
thing to gain by Denmark’s permanent neutrality.
Towns still actively running for the billet
of Federal capital : Albury, Wentworth, Corowa,
Bathurst, Goulburn, Eden, Parramatta (N.S.W.),
and Ballarat and Maryborough (Vic.). Others
that have lost wind temporarily are expected to
recover and come on again as soon as Convention
affairs hum.
If the Reid Ministry lasts till the 19th of
Aug. next, it will be the longest-lived N.S.W.
Government up to date. At present, Parkes holds
the record, his third term having lasted four
years and 15 days. Dibbs, whose third Ministry
lasted two years, nine months and 11 days, occu-
pies third place.
At the end of each session, a certain mem-
ber of N.S.W. Assembly sends a letter to every
voter in his electorate, in which he sets forth some
of the noble things he is doing for his bleeding
country, and asks for a continuance of sup-
port. And yet they say that members don’t earn
their “ six quid.”
Now said that Treasurer Holder has
changed his mind, and that, deaf to the persua-
sions of Kingston, he will appoint himself S.A.
Agent-General when Playford returns. Playford
will, in that case, if he can win a seat in Parlia-
ment, go back to his old office of Treasurer, which
he vacated when he went to London.
The N.S.W. Premier says that owing to
the loss of the probate-duties it will be necessary
to stop payment of the municipal subsidy. But,
seeing that he himself shoved through an Act
which declares that the subsidy shall be paid, the
chances are that the enraged municipal fathers
will sue him for the money and recover it by levy
and distress on G.R.’s shiny pot-hat, rather than
go without. A tigress deprived of her young isn’t
a circumstance to an alderman done out of his
subside.
Certain letters mixed up with a big “dum-
mying scandal ” are in type in Sydney, and when
they get out—which they must do when N.S.W.
Parliament grants a Commission to enquire into
the matter—the ruin ot\a number of pretty big
people will be assured. The exposure of the
scandal will shake squatterdom to its centre.
The Courier on the return of Tomjoe
Byrnes j “ A good Queenslander is a good
Briton.” Considering that Tomjoe rose to power
on the vote that is essentially anti-British, and
considering also that for Tomjoe to avow himself
a ‘ good Briton ” would undoubtedly cost him the
Irish vote that has made him—-the humor (or
irony) of this remark is fine indeed.
At the third Convention, Tasmania will
probably have to put up most of the time with
nine representatives. Braddon is ill, and Dobson
has been much shaken-up by a cow, so they will
probably have to take spells turn about. Any-
how, nine representatives is just the number
which Tasmania (and N.S.W.) ought to have—
the symbol of nine tails that beat as one.
One Bulletin prophecy, anyhow, seems in
a fair way to be realised. The N.S.W. general
election isn’t absolutely due till the latter part of
July, but this paper said that if the finances were
very awful it would probably be held before the
Ist J uly—the day when the deficit is supposed to
come to light. The finances seem moderately
awful, anyhow, and the election is fixed for June.
A recently-announced knighthood recalls
that the recipient, although a bigoted Tory, once
won an election for the Labor-party. At the time
woman’s suffrage was before Parliament, the
Tories fought it tooth-and-nail, and one of their
strongest weapons was a brilliant series of articles
in the Ass paper, contributed by the Tory afore-
said, proving that woman had been at the bottom
of all the devilment in the world’s history. The
women got their vote, however, and, when elec-
tion-time came round, the Labor-party re-printed
those articles, and circulated them broadcast
through the province, with the result that the
women voted Labor to a “man.”
Boss Tozer has been shunted to the Q.
Agency-General, partly to avoid meeting Tomjoe
Bvrnes (the two of them couldn’t live in the same
House), and partly to escape being killed at next
election. This is one of the signs that foretoken
the end. Then Premier Nelson resigns and
mounts a stair; Foxton takes the chairmanship
of the Land Board (District Court Judgeship),
a billet he himself created for himself, and is keep-
ing vacant for himself ! Thynne resigns from the
Council in order to come again as a candidate for
the Assembly ; Philp doesn’t care to press for-
ward ; Barlow is like Lazarus was when he had
been in the grave five days ; Wilson is nobody :
Dalrymple is a scallywag wit ; and- Tomjoe
Byrnes is sent for as Premier ! Disorder in the
’Ouse. Dissolution. Rage in the country ; and
Tomjoe will have been Premier for five minutes,
so to speak.
THE OULD SOD.
PAT : “I see Gcorgs Rade’s put a tax on the pore
widdys."
MIKE: “ Begorra, it 'ud be betther to tax thim
absentee landlords. The country's run over
wid thim!"
Melbourne Leader (David Syme’s weekly)
still girds at Premier Turner because he doesn’t
float big loans and start another boom. “ The
snail’s pace which George Turner approves in his
love of economy,” wails the Age’s weekly echo,
“is beginning to dishearten and disgust the
people.’ The bitter grievance against Turner is
that he has actually not floated a big loan for three
years. Yet a time will probably come when these
Srovinces, which have already by far the biggest
ebt on earth, will have to do without a big loan
for 300 years. Why not try to get used to the
feeling now ?
The criminal prosecution of Messrs. Lons-
dale and Norton, entrusted to junior counsel on
the last occasion, will, it is understood, be con-
ducted by senior counsel in April next. The
counsel who appeared for Minister Carruthers, in
the preliminary proceedings, expected such a fee
for going to Armidale that Govt, feared to be
taxed with a lavish expenditure to save a colleague
and ruin a bitter personal and political opponent.
An apologue of the late Henry George’s.
English nobleman comes across poacher. Lord :
“ How dare you come on my land, sir ? ” Poacher :
“ Your land ! How do you make that out ? ”
L. : “ Because I inherited it from my father.”
P. : “And pray how did he come by it?” L. :
“It descended to him from his ancestors.” P. :
“ But tell me how they came by it ? ” L, ;
“ Why, they fought for it and won it, of course.
P. (taking off his coat): “ Then, I’ll fight you for
it.”
Col. Secretary Brunker has succeeded in
earning the undying enmity of Sydney evening
pressmen by reserving special tit-bits of informa-
tion for the morning journals. On more than one
occasion Saint Nixon has been badly bowled out
in these gracious little acts of favoritism, and
quite a lively five minutes is said to have resulted
when a reporter recently drew the Minister’s
attention to some important military documents
which he had unblushingly stated to be unavail-
able, although they lay on the table directly before
his eyes.
Sydney Telegraph alleges that an audi-
ence of 2000 or 3000 people at Hamilton gave a
unanimous vote in favor of Premier Reid’s scheme
of Council reform. How many of these people
knew that his “ scheme,'’ as described at Mudgee
(provided somebody wasn’t very drunk on that
lamentable occasion), consisted in appointing
councillors for 25 years, and, as only one of
the present councillors has hung on so long as
that, the only difference it would make would be
the firing out of old George Cox, who doesn’t
matter much anyhow.
Tom Slattery, with his empty boast that if
the sitting member, Premier Reid, refuses to meet
him in King Division he (T.S.) will “ publicly
brand him as a coward ” is a bad case of prius
dementat. The blathering attorney who egged on
the Kyardinal to his political doom rushes round
whooping and shaking hands with himself over his
assumed defeat of Reid some months hence;
whereas Reid quietly smiles and says lie is glad to
find that Tom Slattery is to be his opponent.
Which is, no doubt, true, for, of all the men who
might have any real chance in the constituency,
Slattery is the very worst who could be pitted
against Reid : the latter has and has always had
the personal (and, to a large extent, the political)
goodwill of the very classes who are supposed to
regard Flattery Slattery as a man of mana. Flat-
tery Slattery is simply turning the grindstone
while the Freetrade dailies sharpen the knife to
cut the Protectionist throat. To mix the meta-
phor, he oue'ht to be muzzled.
Will the unionists and others never see
that thecontinued injustice(as with tlieTypo. Asso-
ciation and with the Q. Teachers’ Association) of
making a lower scale for female labor than for
male labor, when that labor is of one and the
same value, must lower the wages ? It is a case
of either lifting the female wage to the male wage,
or of lowering the male wage to the female wage.
There can be only one market
value for any kind of labor.
Pat Man will take care
that it is the lowest value
obtainable. Greed that is
blind and tries oppression
and exclusion falls down,
and must fall down, badly
every time.
One of the large ideas
of Jay Gould was to fur-
nish China with rail-
ways. He impressed
Premier Chang with
theideathat it would
be advisable to keep
Britain, Russia,
France, and Germany
out, because they
would seize territory.
There was no fear of
the U.S. doing so.
Consequently, it would
be wise to let him es-
tablish a bank in China,
say, 150 million dollars
capital, with the railway
work as its backbone.
This came to the ears of
the Germans. Krupp and
the Rothschilds combined
requesting Chang to enter-
tain rather the same offer
from them as sounder and
more feasible. The British
diplomats got wind, and
Britain sat on both schemes
without being able to edge in one of her own.
Russia had then not a tithe of the present influ-
ence. Chang resolved that China should exploit
her own railways.
British army item :
In May three batteries were ordered for South Africa,
and in order to send them out 73 per cent, of the men
and 90 per cent, of the horses were begged, borrowed, or
stolen from other units, and no less than 20 batteries were
torn to pieces in order to provide 18 guns, and this in a
time of profound peace. As to the infantry, what was
the evidence given by the military hierarchy before the
Royal Commission : “Not a battalion fit to go on active
service ”; “An English regiment at home is like a
squeezed lemon.” Take the men who came up to London
for the Jubilee, for whom the War Office has reduced the
standard to sft. 3Jin., chest measurement 32in. ; read, the
Report of the Inspector-General of Recruiting, where
over 20 per cent, of the recruits are special ( i.e., under
6ft. 3Jin. and 32in. round the chest).
And so on—excellent reading in France and Ger-
many. But what about Australia, which relies
on Britain to protect her?
“ The Victorian revenue for the half-year ending
Slat. Dec., 1897, is returned at a total of £8,374,262.
Compared with the corresponding half of the pre-
vious year, when the receipts were £3,224,[?], this
shows an inereas of 2149,931. With nearly £150,000
of an improvement in the.first half year, the estimate
appears likely to he realised, but it must be remem-
bered that practically the whole of the increase comes
from the abnormal source of probate duty paid on
the estates of some of our wealthiest citizens." — DAILY
PAPER.
The Australian is the only colonial Judge
on the Privy Council who is without a title or
special salary to support the expenses of his posi-
tion. That is Australia’s Way. Canada and the
Cape have paid knights.
Brisbane (Q.) Bishop Webber says that
the reason why he goes outside of Q. for parsons
is because Q. can’t produce them. “It has no
means of production no university, no theolo-
gical college. Why he goes outside Q. for cash, is
because absentees draw much of their wealth from
Q., and those of them who are of his church
should, therefore, support his church :
Take a map of Queensland and mark off on that map
the properties owned outside the province by thousands
of English shareholders of the great financial institutions
in Lombard-street, in Bjshopgate-street Within, and in
Scotland, as also in Victoria and South Australia ; how
much of the province do you think you would have left?
Query : Should a mere District Court
Judge, who hasn’t the same independent position
as a Supreme Court Judge, and who is dependent
on the Ministry for his chances of promotion to
the Supreme Court bench, try cases in which
Ministers are deeply concerned? Justice needs
not only to be absolutely clean but to look abso-
lutely clean also, and the public can’t help realis-
ing that, if the case should call on the District
Court man to say or do something very awful about
a Minister, that Minister, being merely human,
would probably see him in a place too unpleasant
to mention before he would assist at his promo-
tion. The public in general is a low character,
and full of evil imaginings.
Brisbane Courier predicts a total change
of political front at next Bknanaland elections.
“ Time,” says that organ, “has smitten the fads
of the Labor-party with merciless severity. . . .
The electors will no longer be asked to vote for or
against ‘Socialism in our Time,’” The truth
is that the Courier and many of its kidney have
become utterly disgusted with the squatterisation
of the few admirable points of the late Land Bill
—to say nothing of the disgraceful way in which
the Centralian lands are being sacrificed and
settlement blocked by the renewal of pastoral
leases at ridiculously-low rents. The squatting
influence is more powerful in Q. Parliament to-day
than at any time for the past 20 years.
The voice of N.S.W. Opposition-leader
Lyne:—
He regretted the strong observations made by Mr.
Barton and Mr. R. E. O’Connor with reference to the
Federal Enabling Amendment Bill increasing the neces-
sary number of voters to 80,000. If Federation was worth
having at all they should get 80,000 votes and a great
many more. (Cheers.) If they did not get 80,000 votes,
then the time was not ripe for Federation.
Quite so. And, as the other side of the case :If
provincialism is worth having it should also get
80,000 votes, and if it doesn’t get 80,000 votes then
the country doesn’t deserve to have it. (Cheers.)
Yet the Lyne crowd has fixed up a law whereby
it takes 80,000 votes to endorse Federation, yet
one vote or no vote at all may commit the pro-
vince to provincialism for an indefinite number of
years, or perhaps for ever. (Loud groans.) What
has provincialism ever done for N.S.W. that it
should get 80,000 start in. the race? And why not
fut things the other way, and enact that the
'ederal Constitution shall be accepted, even if
nobody votes for it, unless 80,000 electors vote
against it ? (Great applause.)
“The degraded Dreyfus is making himself
heard from his mud-spot in the ocean, and the
Semitic shriek has long ere this gone round the
world,” writes The Bulletin’s London corre-
spondent. “Delicacy for France’s feelings has
prevented the English newspapers printing the
damning evidence against the military Judas, but
a military embassy attache explained the awkward
situation briefly to me. The French frontier is
defended by isolated forts so arranged that if one
be taken the others may demolish it with their
guns. Underground galleries connect these forts,
allowing of their garrisons being increased or de-
creased by these hidden ways. For six months it
was notioed that every massing of French troops
was met by a move of Germans directly opposite
them across the border. Disguised French officers
sent into Germany were sent back at the frontiers t
others were allowed to pass and shepherded night
and day. A publication of the full evidence meant
war probably, and certain discredit for the mili-
tary authorities. Some of the agitators know the
facts and desire the latter; the others are honest
and ignorant. Dreyfus had squandered his wife’s
fortune on her substitutes.”

A Lady Journalist’s
opinion of
Dunlqp Tyres
The Dunlop Pneumatic Tyre Co. Ltd.,
247 Swanston-street, Melbourne. "’
Dear Sirs,—•
Punch Office,
Dec. 23rd, ’97.
“I have been a cycliste for three years and (with
brief and dreadful interval of a few months) have ridden a
Of course they have been eminently a success all the time.
Al^' e , toUred ) over a » reat of Victoria and into South
W-c ’ and Ju ne u° Ver r ° ads not even ma rked in Broad-
bent s ma P . Whether in the mallee, down by the sea, or
° n the mountains, Dunlop Tyres are ever to be relied
upon on the worst roads.
My last experience of their lasting qualities was on
Saturday evening, December 18th, when riding on a
suburban road. A road hog without lights to his cart,
was on his wrong side, and before I could escape, had
driven right across my bicycle. Howl escaped sudden
My bicycle almost completely
ecked. But that it is built by an eminent English firm
it would have been reduced to scrap-iron. Both rims
were buckled, dress-guard and gear-case looked as if they
had been struck by lightning, back stays and both
dropped bars were injured almost beyond repair.
But, strangest of all, neither of the Dunlop Tyres was in
the slightest degree injured, though they, like myself,
must have been only the fraction of an inch beyond the
devastating wheel of the road hog’s cart."
Yours truly,
(Signed) Mrs. DONALD MACDONALD.

The Dunlop Pneumatic Tyre Co. Ltd.,
486 Kent Street, SYDNEY.
Melbourne, Adelaide, Perth, and Christchurch N.Z,
Ed. Bulletin, —Another phase of Klon-
dyke life. A much-travelled man tells me that
the heavy mortality amongst the Indians of N. W.
Canada and the Alaskan territory is chiefly due to
small-pox and frost-bite. Also that the inhabi-
tants of that region always dodge the subject of
small-pox, preferring to lock their skeleton in an
inquiry-proof cupboard as far as possible. Is it
all true?— R.B.H.
If the Westralian Govt, remains inactive a
little while longer, Coolgardie will be able to have
rabbit-pie for Sunday’s dinner. “I have to re-
port,” the telegraph master at Eyre wires to the
Lands Minister, “that natives have brought in
10 young rabbits which they took from a nest at a
point at least 40 miles west of Eyre.” Westralia
gallantly put a whole <£looo on the last Estimates
to meet the rabbit invasion.
E. Adelaide election, for the vacancy caused
by M’Pherson’s death, takes place this Saturday
(22nd). Candidates are “Londonderry” Shaw
(Tory), Hutchison (Labor), and one Salmon, who
announces himself as “Independent Labor,” but
is really a red herring. As S.A. politics, owing
to the Hospital row and Labor mistakes in past
contests, are much mixed, it will be a close fight,
despite the fact that E. Adelaide is the strongest
Labor centre.
Brisbane (Q.) papers still persist in talking
that old Bully Palmer won't resign from the Presi-
dency of the Leg. Council, and that the Govt,
can t make him. Don’t these editors know the
Constitution of their own Parliament ? The Pre-
sident of the Council is appointed by the Governor,
who can remove him, and appoint another in his
stead. So, if Premier Nelson wanted to shunt
Palmer, he could do so in five minutes for
“ Governor ” means “ Governor-in-Council.” But
Nelson doesn’t want to shunt old Bully—yet. The
Govt, wants to provide for the Old Man, and, if
Palmer doesn’t die before the hour of the trans-
formation-scene, a bill will be rushed through.
And Palmer will still remain Lieut.-Governor, for
so he was appointed by commission in ’93.
Curious position ! The Lieut. - Governor, being
actual Governor in the real Ex.’s absence, can ap-
point the President of the Leg. Council ! Nelson
had better give the old man a big allowance, or,
next time he’s Gov. de facto, and Nelson is Council
President, he may fire Nelson out and re-appoint
himself.
It lately transpired that in case of in-
vasion N.S.W. has enough ammunition on hand
to enable its regular forces to keep up a tolerably
active fight for about three hours, and then
there would be nothing left but bricks and
Brunker. The other provinces seem to be in
about the same situation, or in a worse one.
There is an ammunition - factory in Austra-
lia, but it is situated close to Melbourne, a
badly - defended seaport down South, and in
case of invasion it would probably be abolished
in one round. If it wasn’t abolished it is only
a very small place, which can’t do much more
than keep up a peace allowance of ammuni-
tion, and as a good deal of its material is imported
it leaves the country still more or les9 dependent
on foreign supplies. Besides, in case of trouble,
the Vic. Govt, would grab the contents of that
factory for its own use. And when the “ troops ”
had shot away their three hours’ supply, it is a
cheering reflection that the next lot could be de-
livered here in six weeks if the people at the other
end hurried up and it wasn’t intercepted on the
way.

hairy enough for her ? ”
S.M. Herald reports—
Banquet at Marriokvillk.— On Thursday evening a
complimentary banquet was given to Mr. It. Q. Brereton
who has recently been appointed—
What? Premier? Peer? Right Noble Worthy
Grand Kafoozlem in the Lodge of Ancient and
Accepted Chopsticks ? No—
who has recently been appointed a justice of the peace.
Great Scott!
The irrepressible Randolph Bedford, hav-
ing failed to get into Vic. Parliament, and having
extracted a tidy solatium therefor from David
Syme, is now enthusiastic over a Elondyke
scheme. Everything is included in the idea-
transport, supplies, mining, floating, financing,
and journalising, and the Reckless is just the man
to put the thing through. “ It’s no good talking
about the dangers and all the rest of it,” he says.
“ A man can't dodge his fate, and the odds are if
you funk any place you’ll stay at home and get
run over by a blooming steam roller.”
Finnerty, Westralian goldfields R.M. and
other things, lets no dog bark in his court. In a
case the other day he said the question was
whether the defendant (charged with selling scrip
at auction without a licence) was an agent or an
auctioneer ; he would, he said, refer the case to
the Crown Law Officers for an opinion, and mean-
time he warned other unlicensed persons selling
scrip of the risk they ran. Defendant’s counsel
promptly intimated that he would take the risk.
Whereupon the R.M. : “ Oh, as you talk like
u l^ ne defendant -£25.” And he added, too,
that he wouldn’t stay execution pending an appeal.
Victoria intimates formally that it takes
no further interest in Wales’s dreary white ele-
phant, the Imperial Institute, and its contribution
towards the expense is to stop short. The Insti-
toot has fallen so very flat of late years that until
this casual mention of it most people had for-
gotten that there ever was such a place. And
when one thinks of the roar of loyalty with which
it was inaugurated, and the wine, and the “ silken
bonds,” and the large fat cigars, and the grease
that got on to the audience’s whiskers, and how it
stood up with all the liquors awash in its stomach
and rested its hands on the table and declared
that this was the most memorable epoch—hie—
poch—in history, and that it would be for ever
remembered, and its influence would go on spread-
ing through all time—well, it looks like a pretty
bad fall. 1
The firemen of A.U.S.N. Co’s. s.s.
Bulimba took a sensible stand in refusing to sail
from Melb. on Sunday, and seeing the captain
« d first—though doubtless he’s not to blame.
The ship-company dodge nowadays is to sail last
thing on Saturday night—and arrange trips to
suit so that every ounce of labor can be screwed
out of the men, and the abomination of a weekly
rest-day can be thrown right out of the calendar.
But the other day the Bulimba came into Melb.
late, and apparently couldn’t get her cargo in on
Sat. night. The captain was “ quite taken a-
back ” when the firemen “ refused to work Sunday
m port.’" Bulletin congratulates the men, and
hopes their union will stick by them. The public
certainly would. No shipping company could
afford to fight this matter in open daylight.
