SPORTING NOTIONS.
So far, nothing has been heard from George
Adams in the matter of protecting his sweep-pat roris from
racing blackmailers. “Tattersall ” draws a large income
from the Australian sporting public, and, hitherto, his
name has been synonymous with probity and honor. But
the “ information ’’-leakages in his office —most of them
must be from his office—are disgusting many of his best
friends and supporters. If you draw a horse in “ Tatter-
sail’s” sweep nowadays, you risk receiving l q demand
front the owner for half the prize, accompanied with the
significant threat that, if you don't agree, the horse
won’t run. That sort of thing stinks. You putyour money
down in the faith that you will receive the advertised
prize, and it is disgraceful that you should be bled by
the horse-owner—acting on information Which, as'already-
remarked, must, in the great majority of cases, be received
from some of “ Tattersall’s ” officials. George Adams has
so far dealt honestly with the Australian public,, and no
one grudges him the handsome commission on his enter-
prise. Let him continue to see that his clients are dealt
by squarely, and draw his sweeps on the day of the race,
or there will presently be room in his business for some-
one else with better methods.
• * • *
There is one man at least in Australia who is
not a staunch believer in the starting-barrier. Last week
his prad was backed down to 2 to 1 on ; indeed, so heavily
was the neddv supported that it almost looked as if the
other owners were quite satisfied there was nothing else
in it. But they all reckoned without the barrier. At the
word “ go,’ - the webbing took a couple of half-hitches and
a tom-fool’s knot round the favorite’s neck, and, by the
time he got clear, the field was well on its journey, several
of the jockeys taking a sly and anxious glance now and
then as to how the favorite was making up ground. He
made a gallant struggle, but only got close enough to
come prominently under the handicapper’s eagle eye.
Hard mozzle, without a doubt.
* * * •
Jack Cohen, the sad-eyed bookie who rides a
bike, is going on a six months trip to Europe. “With
Messrs. Arnfield and S. Allen retired, and J. Cohen absent
in England, the Victorian ring will be considerably
weaker than it has been for some considerable time,”
says Melb. Age. “ Retired ”is a sweet way of expressing
the wipe-out. of Arnfield and Allen aforesaid. If the ring
is “ weakened ” by the disqualification of two members
for cronk practices, how much the more reason for
legalising the tote !
* * * •
Brighton, Sydney, had an unusually big gather-
ing last Saturday, the attraction being a £5O trot, run off
in two divisions. Unfortunately, the event looked, and
proved, a foregone conclusion for Dr. Fitzgerald’s mare
Kathleen, who, starting 4sec. behind, won her heat and
the final without effort. Kathleen is by Childe Harold
from Compliment. The mare trotted at a 2.45 J gait, but
she won with such ridiculous ease that the go was no
criterion of her pace.
• * * *
P.M.G. Duffy, protected by the “ privilege” of
his place in Vic. Assembly, said something that sounded
very like a criminal libel on Tattersall Adams the other
week. They were discussing the sweep-advt. question,
and squatter Staughton casually remarked that some of
the V ctorian money posted to Hobart came backin prizes.
Whereupon “Mr. Duffy did riot think that much of the
money sent to sweep-promoters found its way back again.
It was practically lost to the colony.” Duffy’s foolish
thought was practically an allegation of fraudulent sticki-
ness against Tatt.’s business fingers. Ninety per cent, of
all the cash paid into a Tatt.’s sweep is available for re-
distribution among winning ticket-holders, and the per-
centage of prize-money that doesn’t get claimed is not
worth grumbling over. Also, there is no reason for sug-
gesting that Victoria loses a larger proportion of its
average investment, taking one year with another, than
any of the other provinces. Perhaps Duffy will kindly ex-
plain the true inwardness of his meaning?
• • * •
Dear Bulletin, — In the interests of Australian
sport let me make a suggestion : Carbine, Trenton, Car-
nage, Emmalea, Newhaven, Merman, Maluma have all
been snapped up by England, and now Aurum is going.
Many of our best cricketers have settled “up over,” and
others are being nibbled at. We must try and get even
to some extent. “Bought for England” is becoming
tiresome. Let our wealthiest sportsmen prove their
patriotism and their love of sport by clubbing together to
purchase this English cricket-team for Australia. If the
thing be gone about in earnest and generously, each
Govt, will doubtless assist. It’s only a matter of money,
and we’d get used to them in time. Something must be
done.— Azure Prospect.
• • • •
Greygown, once favorite for Melb. Cup, is now
the favorite mount of the street urchins of Caulfield. He
was sold recently for a sov.
• • * *
C.M.G. : “During the dreadful hot spell in
Melbourne in Xmas week, when fat citizens were going
down in their tracks all over the place, and even the
trained athletes were turning up their toes and calling for
ice-packs and the benefit of medicine and clergy, I noticed
the men engaged in demolishing the ruins left by the big
fire hard at work in the fiercest glare of the hottest sun
of the season, with pick and hammer, up on those red-hot
walls. I have not heard that any of them had to be laid
oh ice in a back bar, and fanned by an army of sym-
pathetic ladies. Our hardy cricketers do not seem to
score well by contrast. But perhaps, if those laborers had
had the remotest chance of being iced and fanned by nice
girls, they might also have been apoplexed by the heat.
What do you think?"
Britishah: “ Nine wickets for 652, as the
result of two whole days’ batting by the side
that took first innings, reads very like a game
of wearing the other side out. Take the figures for
the three test-matches. In Sydney the Englishmen,
winning the toss, had scored 551 runs at about
4 o'clock on the second day. In Melbourne the Aus-
tralians were all out for 520 shortly after 5 o’clock-
second day. In Adelaide they had scored exactly 475 for
six wickets only at 4 o’clock—second day. The loss of
each wicket represents a loss of scoring time, therefore
unless the Australian bowling and ground fielding is
inferior to the work of Stoddart's team, and it cer-
tainly isn’t, the visitors must be credited with a much
livelier batting entertainment than their opponents give
to the public. Patience is a virtue which comes rough
upon those onlookers who are not barracking for its
exponents. How would an Australian crowd regard an
English performance of what they call “the game?”
Scornfully. Stonewaller Bannerman was wont to get
much jeering in interproviucial matches—outside of
Sydney—though, when he was making English bowlers
sick on any Australian ground, the crowd revelled in his
wearisomeriess and encouraged him with roars of ap-
proval. The first two days’ play in the Adelaide test-
match was a record, anyhow—either of dull Australian
batting, or brilliant Hinglish attack. You pay your
money and take your choice of opinion.”
As The Bulletin goes to press the Australians
look uncommonly like winning the third test-match, and
getting two legs in out of five. Against their lst-innings
total of 573 the Englishmen could only put up 278, and
had to follow on. Howell—whose exclusion from pre-
vious test-matches some people attribute to his inability
to say “ Haw!'’ with dignity and effect—bowled splen-
didly, and got 4of the 10 English wickets; Noble, with 3,
seconding him ably. The Englishmen commenced their
second innings by losing Mason for a duck, and wore
glum faces when left batting.
• * • •
E verybody agrees that Richardson has lost some
of his pace, and more of his “terror” for Australian
batsmen. Then how weak Stoddart’s present team must
be in bowling! What kind of chance would they have if
big Tom were to break down ?
• * • *
“ A still tongue makes a wise head ” is a proverb
which Ranjitsinhji will perhaps learn to take to heart.
As might have been expected, the Adelaide crowd
chivvied him when he went to the wicket on Monday,
which is said “ to have unnerved him, as he is particu-
larly sensitive.” On top of all his other troubles, the
dark gentleman batted with a dislocated finger.
• » * •
No doubt Ranji. thought it “ bad form " for the
Adelaide crowd to go wild over their townies (Darling and
Hill) making “ soft-tommy” of the British bowling. And
this on top of a sore throat, and twice bowled in the
recent match, and having missed a chance off Hill,
although a difficult one. Taking one consideration with
another, the dark gentleman is not having a good time.
• » • »
S.A. cricketers are invariably at sea with
N.S.W. bowling—witness their wretched score of 139 in
Adelaide on a perfect wicket. G. Giffen and Clem. Hill
were the only batsmen who showed anything like first-
class form. Howell’s length bowling was simply too good,
and how that splendid trundler came to be omitted
against Victoria in Melb. is a mystery. The best batting
in the match was shown, with the exception of Giffen and
Hill, by N.S. Welshmen. Noble, Iredale, Trumper, and
M'Kenzie all performed well.
G. Giffen bowled away in the N.S.W. second
innings for no wickets and 118 runs! George is captain
of the S.A. team, which is a sufficient explanation. Mean-
while Reedman was kept off, although his average was
two wickets for 11 runs ! Jonah Jones came out of the
match with 11 wickets for 122 runs—a grand average
which will greatly help to put him above Richardson at
the season's end.
The match, like the one in Melb. between
N.S.W. and Vic., was played in scorching weather. Sin-
gularly enough, the end of the contest, as in Melb.,
brought a cool change for the bigger event between Aus-
tralia and England.
• * • •
Gate, gate, and nothing but gate. The reason
of the test-matches being crowded on t.op of the interpro-
vincials sticks out prominently. By playing whilst the
men are on the ground, the syndicate is saved heavy
travelling expenses. What matters it if interest in art
interprovincial match is ruined by coming immediately in
front of a test-match, and the men are stale and battered
about, so long as the div. comes out all right?
• • * *
The babble of cricket-reporting in “great ’’dailies
doesn't tend to create much respect for enthusiastic follow-
ers of the game. Melb. Argus after the second test-match
dosed its readers with such representative utterances as
these —“ Mr. Jack Blackham said: ‘ Well, our boys have
made a good score, and they deserve it.’ Mr. Harry Boyle
said :1 1 was delighted with Syd. Gregory’s running between
the wickets.’ Mr. J. J. Ferris said : ‘ Trott played awfully
well, especially after lunch.’ Mr. W. O. Whitridge said r
‘ I was much impressed with the slowness of the ground.’
A pillar of Essendon football said of Iredale : ‘ Why, it’s
like eating strawberries and cream to see him bat.’ ” And
so on. Tub Bulletin begs to chip in the observation that
“No feature of the game was more admirable than the
Gross Receipts.”
* * • *
It looks as if there is trouble in store re Sydney
Cricket Ground. The members who find the sinews of
war, in conjunction with the bobites, have in the past
been mere nonentities, but there is much talk amongst
them just now, and a very different state of affairs is
likely to obtain in the near future. Some want to know
why the ground is not cleared of debt before any more
money is expended on it, tinless to make good necessary
wear and tear, and they even hint that a list of names of
the debenture-holders, and a statement of the rate of in-
terest paid, would be very interesting.
* * * •
Re Stoddart’s stipulation that not more than 13
men shall field against the English team in 18 and 22
a-side matches, the reason thereof is that such matches
can only be regarded as exhibitions, and, with the full
team in the field, the batsmen would be unable to show
the full value of their best strokes. Apropos, Stoddarfc
tells with a twinkle how the captain of the Glen Innes 18
interpreted the condition. That general got leave to
change his men as often as he liked, and began with his
11 best fieldsmen and two best bowlers. The other
bowlers were ready, and at each change of bowling a
trundler would leave the field, and his successor come on
to it.
• * • *
George Palmer, the old Australian Eleven man,'
is caretaker of the Launceston Association Ground, Tas.,
and played in the recent North v. South match, in which
he showed about the best form on his side: the North,
however, was badly licked, owing chiefly to Eady’s
14-yard rushes and express deliveries. North and South
are now equal for the first time, each having won 2ff
matches.

The Cavill family were in evidence at the East
Sydney Swimming Club’s carnival on Saturday. The
220yds. professional championship of N.S.W. was won
easily by “ Turns ” Cavill from Gillespie, in 3min. ; and
the mile amateur championship fell to Percy Cavill, in
26win. 52 l-ssec.—a new record for Australia.
• * * •
The Brisbaneites put the defeat of their boats
in the recent carnival down to the superior sails carried
by the Sydney boats. As a result the crack Sydney sail-
builders are inundated with orders from Bananaland.
• * * *
Sueensland Rowing Association has decided to
the interprovincial eight-oar race under the Vic.
amateur definition, which will mean the withdrawal of
N.S.W. from the event. N.S.W. will thus maintain her
unchallengeable Respectability, and when an eight-oar
race does take place between Australia and England, the
Australians will be all, or mostly, Cornstalks.
• * • *
“ Newhaven ” Jackson, the winner, and J.
Robertson (200 yards), who fell in the final of the L.V.W.
Cup, last Saturday, are two of the cognoscentis, “ tips”
for A.N. A. Wheel Race.
• * * •
The second day of Yic. League’s meeting was
chiefly notable for having the first paced tournament held
in Melb. Walne won, of course, Green being knocked out-
by a sea-bath of seven hours’ duration, on one of the hot
days. Walne beat Barden with ease, and a crowd, with
its*patriotic feelings aroused by the test-matches, howled
itself hoarse in consequence. In the final run, Body,
through superior pacing, got right, away from the Banana-
lander, but Walne picked up in the last two laps, and won
with a splendid sprint.
In the evening, Body reversed the order by
whipping off the Cyclone Plate, of three distances.
Winning the three-mile event, Walne ran all out in the
mile final, and scratched himself for the last test. Body
winning the mile and five-mile goes, scored best on
points, and won the £4O. The League Cup fell to “ New-
haven ” Jackson, and the mile handicap to L. Barber.
The L.V.W. will drop about .£2OO over the
meeting, the first loss sustained by it so far, but the
beginning of the end* unless care is taken. The loss
iefly arises from the holding of races at night; for,
though decent crowds have turned up on both afternoons
the evenings have been appalling. There is also talk of
reducing prize-money in future, and with no possibility
of ever seeing Bilmartin again the public will continue
stopping away more regularly than ever.
• * * •
A Sydney sporting paper came out with an
account of the Griffo-Tracey fiasco, copied from a Yankee
paper -thus unwittingly giving away a Melb. contem-
porary, which printed the same article word for word as
from its “ own correspondent.”
* « * •
Bill Martin left on Friday for the U.S., where
he will ride again. Immediately the Adelaide League re-
fused to rescind his disqualification, Bill cabled to Yankee-
land asking if the L.A.VV. would let him ride. An affir-
mative answer came and Bill accordingly left on two days’
notice. Bill threatens never to return to Australia, as
this gawdam country never gave him fair play, but
cruelly hounded him down and vilified him. But, as a
matter of fact, Bill got a lot more than fair play from
Australians, and was favored more than any three'riders
of the soil. Bill was a man of such grit and had such
cheerful, bull-pup-like little ways about him that we all
had a more than sneaking affection for him even to the
very last.
Mr. Sam Hordern’s 22-footer has arrived from
England, and will probably make her first effort at Syd-
ney Anniversary Regatta. To an Australian she would
not appear to have a show with our boats, but her
“breeding-,’' by Linton Hope, must make us treat her
with respect until actually found wanting. Her rigging
is the part which will be least fancied in Port Jackson.
She carries a small jib worked with a fine sheet through a
single deadeye, but she may be the means of showing our
boating men that their tremendous head-sails are a mis-
take. Australian ideas have undergone great changes
during the past few years. Our builders used to consider
a deep forefoot gave the boat gripping power on a wind,
but the modern boats have no forefoot or heel to speak
of, it being found that the centreboard is the only reliable
factor in holding a wind. Good as are our boatbuilders,
they are working more or less in the dark as compared
with a designer such as Linton Hope. The trial between
the English and Australian boats will be most interesting.
But, as The Bulletin has before remarked, it would have
liked to see Mr. H. give (say) Donnelly an order to build
one of his best to meet the Britisher.
* * * ♦
The Tasmanian lakes are now swarming with
the coarse, greeny, John Bull faced Beritish trout. These
grab-all foreigners are gradually exterminating the un-
protected and sweet-fleshed native fish ; and, whilst the
citizens of the North and South are bursting their galls
over the. price of meat, hundreds of tons of solid English
fish, nearly as big as whales, are strangling each other for
want of swimming room not far from their back-yards.
The average Tas. native prays incessantly that in time
something will be imported to extinguish the trout.
Meanwhile, it is charming to note, notwithstanding the
majesty of the law and the squattahs, some thousands
are being done to death by netting, shooting, and (hat
delightfully exciting method called “tickling” (the
writer has done it himself), assisted by that bird-fish-
beast the platypus.

The “ Dunlop Record ” for January, ’9B, is now
ready for gratis distribution. This interesting booklet is
got up in good style, and contains good photos, of Platt-
Betts, Megson and Carpenter, besides accounts of Virgin’s
and Mather and Coleman’s transcontinental rides.
Old newspapers are now being made into
travelling trunks. The papers are pasted together,
pressed, soaked with oil, dried, japanned, and
baked. They are then lighter and stronger than
wood.
Ben Tillett’s arrangement to act as paid-
organiser of Labor in M.L. has fallen through—
because the people who put up the idea wouldn’t
put up the cash. So Ben is going home again—
and (in view of the recent Progressive success)
talking of a fresh seat on London County Council.
But that isn’t so easy.
WHEN RANJI GETS BOWLED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Ranji gets bowled the Australian man yells,
With a yell that’s as wide as the land where he dwells,
For the “Prince” bet our power to bowl him was
nought,
That the wreck of his wicket would never be wrought—
Just as though we were prey to a conjuror’s spells 1
In the City of Harbour, the City of Smells,
The City of Giffen and church-going hells.
E’en the babe at the breast to whoop loudly is taught,
When Ranji gets bowled.
And far away back where dear Jim to Bill tells
Cricket yarns that are tall as a building o/ Prell’s,
They forget all about the stray sheep that they've
sought,
No drought, fire, or flood with importance is fraught,
If only they drop on a paper that tells
When Ranji gets bowled 1
H. Q. Q.
DEPARTIN’ MARTIN.
Good-bye, Billy, sturdy Plugger,
Steadfast in the strife ;
Often winning, seldom beaten,
Either way with “ milk ” or shuggar,
You have done your best to sweeten
William’s lot in life.
Bill, you put your iron heart in
What, you found to do ;
Ne’er a rival don at biking
Billed the track as long as Martin J
No one cut a figure striking
Frequently as you.
Back to Yankeeland you travel,
Brave, undaunted still.
With your legs of divers sizes
Heaven give you health to snavel
Many big and smaller prizes,
Good old Plugger Bill!

The late George Ash’s pet idea for estab-
lishing a training-home for weak-minded children
in S.A. has been so far successful that the house
has been secured, and two experts, a Mrs. and a
Miss, have been imported from England. The
Brookmans have given a further substantial
lrimp of their Westralian bullion to the home.
Sydney police say that since they received
the parliamentary franchise, M’sP. and the local
politieiahs, who afe almost all plotting to be
cariqidates while pretending to support others,
jday them a respect in strong contrast to the treat-
ment meted out to them of old. A member stopped
a policeman’s little boy in the street the other day
and asked anxiously about the health of his father,
adding, “ Be sure to tell dad I shook hands with
you and asked about his health.”
The Jap. evidently aims at nothing short
of domination in the Pacific. To say nothing of
the powerful British-built cruisers he has just
bought from Chili, he is at present building in
British, American, and Japanese dockyards,
battleships, first-class cruisers, torpedo-boat de-
stroyers, torpedo-boats and despatch-vessels, on a
scale nothing short of marvellous. As far as can
be ascertained, he is in this way now adding to his
navy : Four battleships, one of 10,000 tons and
three of 14,800 ; five first-class 9600-ton cruisers,
two of 5000 tons, one of 4300, and three of 3000
tons ; eight torpedo-boat destroyers, 15 torpedo-
boats, and a very rapid despatch - vessel. The
little brown man is further a perfect stickler for
the very best, and some of the vessels being built
to his order in Britain are said by experts to be
better than anything afloat.
Once more the wild-eyed female cries out
in the night. The hysterical yell was heard at
Warrnambool (Vic,) this time, arid a frenzied maid-
servant hurled herself into her mistress’s room in
the darkest hour before dawn, frantically declar-
ing that there Was a man in the house, and the
matt’s name Was Richard Harrison. The dis-
tracted maid did riot see Harrison, and nobody
else had any idea that there was a strange bad
mart anywhere near; but she declared she had
heard a man, and was positive it was Harrison,
because of his breath, or an inward conviction, or
something; and the local Solons hearing the case,
after Harrison had sworn that on the night in
question he had never been out of his bed,
declared that they had not the slightest doubt of
his guilt, and Richard will chop Govt, metal till
14th March.

GREAT EXCITEMENT
IN MELBOURNE.
Merit, like murder, always “ will out. ” Mere
assertion never proves anything. Only practical
demonstration of claims to distinction can con-
vince the public mind. The brain of man is the
harbinger of doubt. It accepts nothing that can-
not and is not supported by proof—proof founded
on truth, walled about, domed and turreted with
facts. Facts are the offsprings of development,
and development is the material and legitimate
progeny of doubt. Doubts lead to investigation,
investigation to discovery, and discovery en-
lightens the mind, expands the intellect, and
brings man into closer affinity with nature.
Things that are commonplace enough to us at the
present age would have, less than a century ago,
subjected the projector or discoverer to imprison-
ment and torture, and perhaps death. The dis-
coverer of the earth’s rotundity and the propa-
gators of the fact groaned and suffered beneath
the rack of ignorance and superstition, and were
put to cruel and ignominious deaths. When
Herschell first swept the horoscope of Heaven
with his wonderful telescope, and proclaimed the
existence of the stellar world, the million of glit-
tering spheres whirling in space, compared with
some of which our earth is but a tiny speck, an
avalanche of malice and manevolence was hurled
against his devoted head. So on down through
the whole realm of natural science, until within a
few deoades, every new discovery, every fresh
revelation of fixed natural law, was met with a
tidal wave of credulity and doubt. That word
“impossible” has stood a colossal barrier on the
highway of “Acceptance and Belief.” It took
ages to beat it down, but thanks to modern educa-
tion, which has ungyved the hand and unfettered
the brain of man, that monarch of nature, and
permits him to go on untrammeled, solving her
great problems and interpreting her wondrous
laws.
To-day there is passing in review before our
eyes such a panorama of recent discoveries in the
world of natural phenomena and other branches
of science that we stand convulsed and amazed,
but it must be patent to all who have given the
subject attention that the advancement made in
the medical field eclipses all others, and towers
like a white shaft to the clouds above and beyond
conception.
The Polypathic system in the treatment of
chronic disease is undoubtedly the most advanced
of all medical science, and if one were to judge by
the the vast throng that may be seen daily at their
offices in their magnificent institute, 84 Collins-
street, Melbourne, it would seem that half the
population of the city were clamoring for their
services.
The success attending this new system is almost
miraculous, but it is in accordance with natural
and applied science, and wins its constant vic-
tories over disease because of the true following
out of that which natural ability and the highest
developments of Medical Science has shown to be
sure and true. All sufferers are welcome to the
fullest investigation of the POLYPATHIC
MEDICAL INSTITUTE, of 84 Collins Street,
MELBOURNE. War. HENRY GAZE, M.D.,
L.S.A. Lond., L.R.C.P. Lond., M.R.C.S. Eng.,
is Physician in charge.
Those who cannot call personally, can
write. All communications are strictly con-
fidential, and an opinion will be given in all
cases by return mail where the consultation
fee of 10s. is enclosed.
More capital invested, more physicians
employed, more cures made by the Poly-
pathic System, than by any other similar
Institution or body of Physicians in the
world.*

An impoverished and senile Chow, oa@
Taw, was brought before Rockhampton Polio©
Court the other daw, charged with vagrancy. Th©
P.M. ordered the Celestial wreck to be despatched
to Dunwich Asylum. Taw pleaded hard for a pass
to China, but the Court was obdurate, although
accused threatened to wait there till he got what
he. wanted. Seriously, wouldn’t it pay better to
ship decrepit Chows homeward than to keep them
in Australia ?
The chairman of a bench of three Q. Jay-
pees explained, in re the bench’s decisions in)
double-breasted cross-summons proceedings, that
he had previously made enquiries as to the char-
acter of the parties, and that he found that the
first complainant (who was, of course, second
defendant) “bore an excellent reputation.” The
parties fined appealed to the Full Court, on the
ground that the Bench had been influenced by
evidence other than that given in open court, i
The Full Court very properly quashed the Jay-
pees’ decision. Another illustration of the verity
that no Judge nor magistrate should ever “give
r '.isons ” for a decisien.
Scene—Cairns (Q.) Police Court;: Japanese
interpreter has been offered a fee of half-a-guinea.
“Excuse me,” responded the little brown gent,
“my fee on such occasions is two guineas and I
cannot accept anything less.” On the police as-
serting that 10s. fid. was the fee of the Chinese
interpreter —“ But I am not. a Chinaman, and I
value my services at 2 guineas. I will not create
a precedent by taking a less sum, but, if the court
cannot obtain another interpreter, I will give my
services for nothing.” And he accordingly did so.
Dear Bulletin, —The local members of
the Land Board up here are a squatter, of the
Central division, and a homestead lessee, of the
Western. One day, some of the squatter’s land
came before the board for appraisement of rental;
the squatter, of course, on the floor of the court.
Chairman, to homestead lessee, “ What do you
say?—2d?” H.L. : “Oh, / think 2id.” Chair-
man : “ Very well; you know the land better than
I do.” Then to the squatter: “The rental is
assessed at 2|d.” Next day, the homestead lessee
is on the floor of the court, and his lease rental is
being appraised. Chairman, to colleague (the
squatter, this time) : “ Shall we say l|d ? ”
Squatter: “ No, by God ! —2d ! ” Then to H.L.:
“ Got home on you that time, old man ! ” —B.
Humility is such a truly Christian virtue
that it is seldom seen in a Christian country. But
one genuine sample turned up recently at Melb.
City Court. Plaintiff had "charged another
man with punching his jaw, but to his description
of the stoushing he added, “Well, your Worships,
I don’t think he had any bad intent.” “ What’s
that?” thundered the inspector. “I think he
didn’t mean no harm ; he done it for my own
good.” The Court sat up astounded. “ Then you
think you deserved it ? ” queried the police, sar-
castically. “ Well, sometimes I do, but not this
here time,” cheerfully admitted the forgiving
plaintiff. And the Bench had only sufficient
strength left to fine the accused Is., and limply
order the Court to be adjourned.

THE
Royal flora Copper R|ining Co.
(No TAabiUty),
{ Lake Dora, Tasmania.
CAPITAL - - - £30,000.
In 60,000 Shares of 10s. each.
40,000 Shares are paid up, and 40,000 are paid up to 2s.
£l5OO net placed to credit.
The Company is formed to purchase from Messrs. Curtin
and others the application for Lease No. 2010-93 M., at
Lake Dora, Tasmania.
The following reports have been received and speak for
themselves.
Brokers : Messrs. CLARKE & CO.
Lake Dora, Gormanston,
Jan. 10th, 1898.
Dear Sirs, —I have been requested to furnish you with a
report on Section 2010-93 M, and beg to submit the follow-
ing particulars. <-
, Section 2010-93 M is a Mineral Lease consisting of 80 acres
charted in the name of M'Kye and Curtin, situated west
and adjoining the Lady Dora Copper Mining Company,
and about eight miles north of Mt. Lyell.
The geological features of the section are identical with
Mount Lyell, being a continuation of the same belt of
copper-bearing schist. Near your western boundary line
a trench has been cut 70ft. long and Bft. deep, exposing
payable copper for the whole distance, and the same metal
showing in the face of the trench. The outcrop of the lode
at ; this point is fully 100 ft. wide with a strike of about 30
degrees west of north, and can be traced through the sec-
tionmaintainingitsfull width and value. Thecopperchiefiy
occurs in the form of copper pyrites, but in some parts
green carbonite is fully disseminated. Melaconite and
Native Copper are sometimes met with, also free gold by
panning. The facilities for working are good, the lode
. being so wide it will enable the Mine to be worked by the
open-cut system.
In conclusion, I have no hesitation in saying that I
recommend this property to intending investors, for in
Die Mine they have a large body of payable ore to operate
on.—Yours respectfully,
THOMAS BESSELL,
Manager Lady Dora C.M.G.
Lake Dora, Jan. 10,1898.
Dear Sirs, —I have been requested to furnish you with a
Report on Section No. 2010-9351., and beg to submit the
following particulars.
The above section is a mineral Lease consisting of 80
acres charted in the name of M‘Kye and Curtin, east and
adjoining Lady Dora, north and adjoining the Lake Dora
Copper Mining Co., and about 8 miles north of Gorman-
ston. The geological features of this section and the
locality generally are identical with Mt. Lyell, being a
continuation of the same belt of country. Within
one chain of your western boundary-line there is
a trench cut varying from 3 to Bft. deep and
70ft. across ; so far they have neither walls. The
lode at this point is fully 100 ft. -wide with a
strike of about 35 degrees west of north, with a dip of 1 in
5. Copper pyrites exposed for full width of trench in
what I consider to be paying quantities, green carbonate,
melaconite and native copper are also met with. Free
gold can also be obtained by panning with dish. The
facilities for working are splendid, the lode being so wide
and trace through the property will enable you to work it
by open cut systems. In conclusion, 1 have no hesitation
in recommending this property to intending investors as
one offering good returns for capital invested. —I remain,
Dear Sirs, Yours respectfully,
S. COOPER,
Manager Dora M.O.

Do burglars hammer at the door, and wake up
everyone before effecting an entry ?
YES.
This is practically what they do when they ap-
proach within COO-EE
of a building protected by M'Dermott’s
BURGLAR ALARM.
It is inexpensive, and can be fitted anywhere.
When fixed, absolute
SECURITY
is guaranteed, even though an entry is made
through some unprotected door or window, the
SECRESY
being so complete as to baffle the greatest
caution in stepping anywhere or simply touch-
ing a protected spot. The noise would
WAKE THE DEAD,
and so paralyse the intruder as to render his cap-
ture merely the matter of knocking him down
WITH A BRICK.
No custodian of valuables should be unprotected.
Banks, Stores, Offices, and Dwellings in town,
suburbs or country, are now fitted with this
Burglars’ BUGBEAR.

One of the revelations of the Queensland
Teachers’ Union Conference was that it is the
ambition of every assistant-teaoher to be “allowed
to spank the kid.”
The bursting of the little Cresswell boom
ought to be the end of all serious endeavour to
establish Roger C. D. Tichborne as a living
person. Even a healthy Roger would probably
have been dead by this time, and the young man
who disappeared some 45 years ago Was, accord-
ing to all accounts, a very feeble and asthmatic
weed. Nevertheless the fact that Roger isn’t
legally alive, and wasn’t legally alive when Orton
claimed the Tichborne estates, cannot shake the
argument that he escaped drowning in the Bella.
Against the watery-grave assumption there are
the scraps of evidence and suspicions of evidence,
and circumstantial legends, to the effect that
a Roger Tichborne, or somebody who passed
for him, came to Australia in the early Fifties.
A quarter of a century back, when Orton appeared
on the scene, the Tichborne family might perhaps
have got satisfactory proof of Roger’s death—-
natural or violent—-by making an honest inquiry
into these Australian traditions; but the family
policy was a defensive one. The very last thing
it would do.was to start an investigation which
might, by any chance, bring the real Tichborne to
the surface.
Melb. detective Macmanamy got an easy
job one day lately. The d. was lounging up
against a post, gazing abstractedly into the middle
distance, when a stranger wandered into his line
of vision, and stayed there. Mac. had still a far-
away look in his eye, and the stranger interested
him not, but presently the latter went in at the
knees, and chattered audibly, and, when the
officer recovered from his day-dream, the other
fellow was embracing his calves, and beseeching
him to let him off this time, and declaring, with
tears and awful sobs, that he really wouldn’t
break into any more houses if allowed to depart in
peace, and become a Sunday-school teacher and
an ornament to society. The officer didn’t know
the man, and had never heard of him, but, after
listening to his protestations, he ran him in on the
off-chance, and found he had secured quite a prize.
Who, after this, will sneer at the magic, magnetic
Eye of the Law?

Dr. Creed, M.L.C., writes to the Sydney
press:—
Few people are aware how narrowly we have, on at
least two occasions within the last 20 years, escaped
attack by hostile forces. At the time of the Russo-
Turkish war —or, rather, at its termination—when the
Indian troops were sent to Malta, a Russian fleet was
lying in Vladivostock fully prepared to start, on receipt
of a telegraphic order, to make a foray on the Australian
capitals, and it was only the agreement arrived at shortly
before the conference, at Berlin, between Russia and Great
Britain, which prevented the attack being made. My in-
f-'rfn'ahts, for there were more than one who gave me identi-
cal information, met in Italy the Russian Admiral who
was in command, and who was very fond of telling Aus-
tralians whom he chanced to meet all about the proposed
expedition. The second and more imminent danger
occurred in 1885 at the time when the attack by the
Russians on the. Afghan troops at Pendjeh nearly led to a
rupture between the two nations. At this time a cabled
cipher dispatch to the Governor arrived in Sydney on
Sunday afternoon, saying : “ Rut the colony in a state for
instant defence ; you may be attacked at any moment.”
Further information arriving later showed that there
were 15,000 men at sea for the purpose of making
a simultaneous assault on Sydney, Melbourne and
Adelaide. It appears that the .Russian Govern-
ment had fitted out vessels in various neutral
European ports, and had sent picked men in plain
clothes to them, they purporting to be merely emigrants.
The whole thing was managed so secretly and cleveily
that the British authorities got no inkling of it until after
the ships had sailed for the secret rendezvous, somewhere
on the ocean, to which they went to await orders. The
dispute between the two nations being settled, Australia
was spared this outrage, and many, if not all, of these
men landed at Vladivostock in uniform as soldiers.
Proverbially, the third time will be —fatal?

A Triumph in Medical Science.
MARVELLOUS CURE OF LOCOMOTOR
ATAXIA.
Some six years ago,” said Mr. Jarvis to a
reporter, “ I was skylarking in the Natatorium
Swimming Baths, in Pitt-street, Sydney, when
m y feot slipped, and I fell over the pipe through
which the steam is pumped to warm the water.
My head struck the concrete floor, and my legs
were hurled across the pipe, my flesh being
shrivelled and singed. I fell insensible. When I
was lifted up the burnt flesh from my legs was
4-5
torn away by the neat, and left adhering horribly
to the pipe.
“For sixteen weeks,” continued he, “I was un-
able to leave my bed, and when at last I rose I
was not the same man. lam a boilermaker, you
know, and the work is excessively hard. Still, I
endeavored to keep pace with demands. One
day, however, whilst engaged in a refrigerating
chamber, I caught cold in my back through
entering; in a heated condition. This pulled me
down still more, and I became weaker than ever.
Ultimately I was forced to abandon my trade of
boilermaking at the Atlas Engineering Works.
Looking round for something easy, suited to my
feeble condition, I commenced collecting accounts
for a butcher at Olebe Island. I was, however,
becoming seriously incapacitated, and would be
sometimes confined to bed two days at a time.
Later on I had to lay up three days running.
Then suddenly my sight became affected, and I
could never see correctly. When money was
being paid to me I’d always make mistakes, and I
continually saw‘double.’ I’d have one shilling
in my hand, and I’d see two there, and so on.
Becoming weaker and weaker, with failing sight,
and faltering step, I took to my bed altogether!
The doctor pronounced it an advanced ease of
locomotor ataxia, and I was refused admission to
the Sydney Hospital, as my trouble was said to
be undoubtedly chronic and incurable. I was,
however, given my tram-fare, and an order on the
Director of Charitable Institutions for admission
to the Liverpool or Rook wood Asylum. But
instead of that I tottered home, to spend twelve
months of agonising existence in bed. I lost all
power of walking, and my body became abso-
lutely dead and immovable from the waist down-
wards. Day after day I lay awake restlessly
counting the weary hours, with my helpless legs
drawn close together. Then my wife would come
and shift them apart for me. Sharp pains shot
continuously across my waist. My sight had
failed me, and then another fearful disaster hap-
pened. I lost all control over my bowels and
bladder. Every bit of feeling in my legs was
gone, and pins stuck in would cause me no
pam. I was frequently dizzy, and my palate
was paralysed, so that I had no taste in my
mouth, and could never tell what I was eat-
ing. Not that I ate much, for my appetite
wholly disappeared, and I had no desire for food.
The whole of my interior organs were also para-
lysed, and the tips of my fingers had no feeling
in them. My m dieal adviser told Mrs. Jarvis
that I would never work again, and that, although
I might last some time, I would possibly never
be able to use my legs. He did all he could
to ease ray excruciating agony with opium and
morphia pills, but as my malady was the dreaded
locomotor ataxia, of which no authenticated
cure had ever occurred, he was forced to regard
my plight as hopeless. I tried every patent
medicine without success, and 1 also bought an
electric battery and applied it with the same re-
sult. I thus gave up all hope, and drifted on in
a sea of darkness and misery for a period of
12 long months. One day, however, I saw the
case of John Marshall, of Hamilton, Canada, re-
ported in an American paper, and I noticed that
he had been marvellously cured of my very com-
plaint by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.
Jumping at a straw, I resolved to try them, and
' j so \ I ~n > an( ? n °t till then, did a magnificent
and astounding miracle take place ! I improved
visibly, -hirst my appetite returned, my sight
was restored, the waist pains faded away, and I
could taste what I ate. Then, gradually, feeling
returned to my limbs and to the points of my
nngers. I could run my hand over my legs and
teel the contact. My lower limbs were no more
like blocks of insentient wood ; my will-power in-
creased ; my veins had warm blood infused into
them.
“I was able to leave bed and walkacross the room
with a stick, and sit on the chair, IVty improve-
ment, though gradual, all the time steadily in-
creased, and a little later I was able to walk the
length of the lane with a stick, whilst now I can
move about the house without that aid. Alto-
gether,” Mr. Jarvis continued, “I was off and on
in bed for two years, regarded as a chronic case by
a Government medical officer, and told I could
never recover. Yet here lam!” he said, with a
laugh, “ as well as I was five years ago, and still
on the improve.
I’ve had hundreds and hundreds of visitors,”
he explained, “ since my cure was published, and
Mr*. Jarvis and I have been kept busy going to
the door.” “Indeed, we have,” responded the
lady, laughing. “During Jubilee time, when all
the country visitors were in town, we held a regu-
lar reception, and Mrs. Jarvis could hardly do

a decline. It was of no use, though ; nothing did

Indigestion a Matter of the
Past Now.
Marriekville has not a happier, brighter resi-
dent than Mrs. Edwards, wife of the Marrick-
ville Town Hall caretaker. Hearing that Mrs.
Edwards had been for years a great sufferer from
indigestion and dyspepsia, a reporter called on
her to get the facts of the case, particularly as he
also heard that she had suddenly been cured.
Mrs. Edwards very courteously received the repre-
sentative, and gave him an account of her ailments
and their cure.
“ First of all,” she proceeded, “ I suffered from
sleeplessness, ringing noise in my head, and an
over-bearing pain at my chest. Furthermore., my
appetite failed, and all that would rest on my
stomach was soft food, such as milk and arrow-
root. But what alarmed me most was the way
my heart would go pit-a-pat on the very slightest
exertion. I can assure you if I walked a quarter
of a mile these distressing symptoms would come
on. This frightened me very much, for I feared
I was suffering from heart disease. As to the
remedies I swallowed, why their name is legion,
but unfortunately no good effect resulted, and I
feared my troubles were neither to be cured nor
alleviated. I consequently began to feel very low
and depressed.
One day I read of some cures effected by Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, and indeed
it proved a joyous day for me. I determined to
try a few boxes of this specific, bought them, and
commenced to take them according to the direc-
tions which I noticed were around each box.
After the first two boxes, I felt a decided improve-
\

ment, and decided to continue with this medicine.
As I persevered with the treatment, I made satis-
factory progress.”
“ You said that your heart gave you considerable

“Yes. I was afraid it was diseased, and lam

Pale and Sallow Girls.
Upon parents rests a great responsibility at
the time their daughters are budding into
womanhood. If your daughter is pale, com-
plains of weakness, is “ tired out ” upon the
slightest exertion ; if she is troubled with head-
ache or backache, pain in the side ; if her temper
is fitful and her appetite poor, she is in a condition
of extreme peril, a fit subject for the development
of the most dreaded of all diseases—Consumption.
If you notice any of the above symptoms, lose no
time in procuring Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. They
will assist the patient to develop properly and
regularly; they will enrich the blood and restore
health’s roses to the cheeks; bright eyes and
lightness of step will surely follow their use, and
all danger of consumption and premature death
will be averted. Wise and prudent mothers will
insist upon their daughters taking Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills upon the approach of the period of
puberty, and thus avoid all chances of disease
or early decay.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have cured more than
seventy-five hundred cases of diseases arising from

sallow complexion, muscular weakness, depres-
sion of spirits, loss of appetite, palpitation of the

all forms of female weakness, hysteria, paralysis,
locomotor ataxy, rheumatism and sciatica,
scrofula, rickets, hip-joint diseases, chronic ery-
sipelas, consumption of the bowels, and lungs.

A law recently passed in New York makes
it a felony for any one, except a registered physi-
cian, to have an anaesthetic in his or her posses-
sion. “Knock-out Drops” are as dangerous as
any other concealed weapons.
In June, 1897, the U.S. treasury was still
paying pensions to 16 widows and daughters—
more or less genuine—of soldiers who fought in
the revolutionary war which ended 116 years ago,
and to seven alleged survivors of the second war
with Britain which ended more than 83 years
since.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

cessful in stopping persistent hiccough. He
reports cure in a case that lasted two days.

Amurkan distiller on his new whisky :
It is the most wonderful whiskv that ever drove the
skeleton from a feast or painted landscapes into the brain
of man. It is the mingled souls of wheat and corn. In
it you will find the sunshine and the shadow that chased
each other over the billowy fields, the breath of June,
the carol of the lark, the dews of night, the wealth of
summer and autumn’s rich content, all golden with im-
prisoned light. Drink it, and you will hear the voices of
men and maidens sing the “ Harvest Home,” mingled
with the laughter of the children. Drink it,' and you
will feel within your blood the star-lit dawns, the,dreamy,
tawny dusks of many perfect days. For long 1 j r ears
this liquid joy has been within the happy staves of oak,
longing to touch the lips of man. '

A Tale of Chivalry.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was in the digging days, years ago. She lived
with her husband at one end of the settlement,
where the clumps of blue-gums were and the long
rank grass of the plain began. She was a pretty,
delicate-looking thing; he a big-faced, coarse-
voiced creature, more devil than man. She never
dared to move or lift an eyelid from the floor when
he was in his swearing, fighting moods. That
was how we knew that they were really married
and quite respectable.
By-and-bye he took to going away, sometimes
for weeks together. That was my time to hang
round and do the heavy villain. When I bothered
Effie to clear out with me she cried because I
didn’t respect her. When I left off bothering she
cried again because I didn’t love her.
You’ve heard of the bushranging in those days?
We had it down our way. Of course, if diggers
will come strolling home in ones and twos instead
of twenties, nobody wonders if they come to grief.
None of us pitied Long Bill when we found him
in the scrub with the side of his head knocked in ;
but, all the same, we were up to start a raid on
our own account. I was a big man thereabouts,
and they made me boss.
I went up to see Effie before we left the settle-
ment. Her husband was away, had been away
two weeks ; and when I came I found the house
deserted. She, too, was away; had left the house
the night before—had gone no one knew whither.
There was no time for wondering. I understood
when we tracked him down at last to a deserted
farm-house. Two of us recognised him as the
leader of the gang. I knew him—as Effie’s hus-
band. And there stood Effie, dusty, travel-worn,
yet not much frightened. She’d ridden all the
way from the settlement to warn him. Queer
creatures, women !
Effie was a townswoman, didn’t understand
bush ways, hadn’t any idea that we might possibly
do the business right away, without the help of
any old woman in a wig. And I’d have got her
away, miles out on the flat, before the affair was
more than begun, if that awful coward hadn’t
yelled after her. He did it, just as her foot was
in the stirrup, and she was babbling that, of
course, she knew justice must be done, and she
hoped they’d treat him as kindly as possible on
their way to Ballarat; for of course they would
take him to Ballarat, wouldn’t they, to be tried ?
Then he cried out, and she understood. She
was off the horse in a moment, and staggered
towards me, pale, gasping.
“Jack, not that! He’s my husband, Jack.
Jack, they’re not going to kill him ? You won’t
let them ”
I tried to deceive her, but the words would not
come.
“ It’s justice,” I muttered.
It’s not justice,” she cried, and her eyes flashed
with a sudden comprehension ; “if he were an-
other man, you wouldn’t do this kind of justice.
You want to get him out of the way. You think
I d marry you then, but I never will. I swear it.
Oh! Jack,’’ she was pleading now, “don’t let
them kill him. Don’t let me feel a murderess.”
I took her two hands and held them in mine.
Her heart was not beating more fiercely than my
o*\n.
“On one condition, Effie.”
“On one ? What is it ? ”
“That you promise to live with me as my wife."
We had ridden all day with the wind stirring
the tall grasses at our saddle-girths. Now the sun
was level with our eyes, and we saw its flashlight
on the windows of the township we were approach-
ing—the township where we were not known.
Twilight had fallen when I entered the parlor re-
served for the exclusive use of Mr. and Mrs.
Robertson. Through the thin partition I could
hear the rough voices in the bar. Outside the
long fences were melting away in the dimness. I
saw and heard vaguely. I was waiting for the
sound of her footfall.
At last she came in. She still wore the riding-
dress she had gone to change. Oust was on it,
and fatigue—and something more—on the* pale
young face she raised to mine.
“ Tired, darling?” I whispered.
She trembled ; then all at once, with a little
sob, she was at my feet.
• "P ear Jack ! ” —she took my hand and fondled
it— you don’t mean to keep me to this. I have
sworn, but don’t hold me to it. Jack, be gener-
ous. Let me—go. Let me—keep—my— ”
She stopped, and her sobs smote my ear.
How can I describe what I felt ? At her broken
words of entreaty a rush of new emotions over-
whelmed me. As I saw her at my feet before me
all thoughts of self vanished ; I thought only of
her, and of all that women needed of protection
and pity and reverence. I remembered how frail
they were. They needed our strength, not to
crush them, but to save them from pain and
misery and desolation.
And so—because I reverenced her as he never
did—-I kept her to her bargain.
M.L.
CONSTANCE CLYDE.
An Old Queensland Digger’s
Letter.
[DISINTERRID FOR THE BULLETIN.]
About 79, ten of us, with 23 horses, left
Cooktown to prospect for gold. We travelled up
as far as the Archer. The niggers nearly had L.
there one night. He was lying asleep on his
back. One spear pinned him, through his scalp,
to the ground ; another pinned one of his feet to
the ground, and a third went through the calf of
one of his legs. 0. was lying near him, in a ham-
mock slung between two trees, with a fly thrown
across a surcingle which was stretched from tree
to tree above the hammock. Four spears were
thrown through the fly lying just above O.’s
stomach, but 0. never woke until he heard L.’s
revolver going off.
• • * *
The niggers there were the worst that I
ever met—worse than the Palmer ones. Some-
times, when travelling through kangaroo or spear
grass from 8 to 10ft. high, smoke would spring up
in different places all round us, at about 50 yards
off. We would have to gallop through before the
circle of fire was complete. Day after day, they
used to try and burn us this way. Without
horses we would not have had a dog’s chance. We
could never see them either, although they were
always near us.
* • • *
One day we did get a fair show. That
morning H. got off his horse to have a shot at
some ducks in a water-hole we were passing K.
and the others were leading, the pack-horses fol-
lowing, and C. and myself tailing them up.
On looking round I saw two niggers pegging at
H. with spears. 0. and I fired, but they got
away.
* • * *
Shortly afterwards, we got through a clump
of timber on to a small open flat, the grass on
which had been burned recently. It looked as
level and as green as the ground at Flemington
racecourse. On the plain was a big mob of niggers
—all bucks. We galloped in a circle round them,
and that mob never again tried to roast a white
man alive. B.’s dog, a cross between a bull and
a kangaroo, jumped at one of them. The nigger
threw up his arm. The dog seized it by the wrist.
The nigger stooped down and gripped his teeth in
the dog’s nose. The dog at once shifted his hold
to the black’s throat, brought him to the ground
lay on him, and killed him in no time.
* * * *
We brought back about 850 oz, of gold-
alluvial ; we did not look for reefs.
* * * *
About ’B9, W. called on me at Norman-
ton. .He had left Oooktown to look for country,
engaging , who had left the native police
through a shooting case. They kept the eastern
side of the Peninsula until they got to Albany
Pass. They camped there with Jardine for a few
weeks, and then came down the Peninsula on the
west side until they reached Normanton. At
their seventh camp on their way from Jardine’s,
W. found three different lines of reef, and broke
off some specimens. They weighed about 351 h,
and contained about 30oz. of gold.
I never saw richer specimens, and I have been
toeveryrush in Queensland.
W. told me that knew nothing about it.
I here were three kinds of quartz—one dark-brown,
one white with bluish veins, and some dark burnt
quartz.
W. wanted me to return with him, and I agreed
to go. He then went on the burst, and died of
d.t.
• * • •
Some months after that, I met G-. at Nor-
manton. He had just come down from Cape
York. He said that he got gold when up there,
and wanted me to make one of a party to go. I
could not go at the time. He went to Croydon to
a P ar ty, offering to pay all expenses, but
could only get three good men to promise to go.
He abandoned his purpose. Bad men would be
no good up there, and four men camping in one
spot would have all the niggers in the district on
to them. So he went on the spree, knocked all
his money down, and is now managing a station
nearß .
; w iU know W.’s seventh camp. lam sure
there is a rich field there, and I am on at any time
to make one to go.—Yours sincerely, &c.
• * * •

“Ever Since Then.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Since we left the town behind us with its
tramp of many feet and rattle of wheels, with the
close air and the turmoil and unrest which we
both bate, and turned in the direction of our own
clear lonely bush home, we have been idly chatter-
ing as is our wont.
A white-flowered magnolia breathed its heavy
odour across the road, and we began to speculate
on the powerful effect of the olfactory nerve upon
the brain, and we agreed that one is affected more
deeply and permanently by the sense of smell
than by any other of the senses. We remarked
how vividly memory can be aroused by a chance
scent, and how sometimes we then see clearly
what has been forgotten for a score of years.
“ Was it carnations you wore in your hair that
first night I saw you, Kittie? There’s some scent
that always brings you to my mind, standing in
your white dress and eating an unromantic ice
while I wrote my name for the third extra. If it
wasn’t carnations it was roses,” and his voice
keeps time with the music of the horses’ feet—
“ Ever since then the scent of the roses.”
“It was a pink dress I wore,” is all I say ; but
does he think I have never taken a sly peep into
his big leather - covered desk, and seen that
withered spray of stephanotis, lying there yet, dry
and brittle, with the little curl of hair I cut for
him years ago ?
Since then, we have been silent, for the track is
rough and the horses are but youngsters, inclined
to be startled in the fast-growing dusk. So we
drive along, and the jolting over the bit of cordu-
roy road, the bump of the wheel over the unseen
stump, and Graham’s shouted “Get-up!” as the
horses start at a white post, all combine to keep
me from the forgetfulness of sleep, and my mind
travels ever farther back on the road of life where
I have journeyed.
* * * *
A familiar whiff comes to us as we pass a dim
white homestead, where the men are slouching in
to their evening meal. It is unmistakeably roast
mutton, and of a sudden there comes before my
mind’s sight a small white-pinafored girl, who
gazes with puzzled eyes at the round, bewildering
face of a clock. It is one o’clock, she knows, for
she can smell the nursery dinner; but how does
Jane ever tell that it is time the cloth was laid ?
This is the constant puzzle that vexes her brain.
« Well, you are a silly to stare at the clock like
that and not be able to read it,” says the big
brother.
“ I can read it,” she says defiantly. “ It’s one
o’clock,” for the smell of mutton grows stronger.
“ It’s just not one o’clock, Miss Clever,” is the
retort, “ for it’s ten minutes to, but you’re such a
duffer that I don’t believe you’ll ever know any
better.” , , ,
A great despair settles on the child, for she
knows she will never be able to read the time, and
what will she do when she grows up ? She thinks
how she will say to cook : “ I want my dinner at
one o’clock, please,” and how she will have to slip
into the passage or the garden, and then call out,
“ Is it one o’clock, Jane? ” for it will never do for
the servants to guess. They might not give her
any dinner at all, and always say, “ It isn’t one
o’clock yet.”
. There is trailing honey-suckle on the posts
of a verandah, breathing its heavy scent upon my
t'.rcd face. I see another verandah and the slim
girlish figure in the cane chair, and the busy hands
that are always stiteli—stitching. The little girl
is there again, still with the puzzled half-
bewildered expression that earns her the nick-
name of “ Alice in Wonderland.” This time her
brain can find no way out of the difficulty.
“Muvver,” she say's at last, “ when 1 am grown
up and a gentleman asks me to marry him, and
I don’t want to, what can Ido ? It would be so
rude to say ‘ I don’t like you. ’ ” And there is no
hidden laugh in the sympathetic mother-face that
knows so well how a smile can hurt as she answers
with such convincing seriousness—“ You need not
say‘l don’t like you.’ You can say, ‘I like you
very much, but not quite enough to marry you.’
Would that do, sweetheart?” and with the re-
lieved response, “ Yes, that would do,” one more
puzzle is dispelled.
* * * •
Jolt ! we go over a rough bridge, and the horses
plunge forward as they hear the crackling of a
bush fire. The smoke is blown across us, with the
fragrance of burning logs, and I am far away in
my dreams again.
There is a glorious bonfire crackling and flaming
in the breeze, and an indignant little girl is called
away from the delights of potato-roasting in the
red ashes—to be hurried into a stiff white frock
that scratches round her neck, and led to be pre-
sented to inopportune visitors. “Hush, dear!”
says a voice as she stumps crossly into the room.
A thin, long-armed girl is hitting one end of the
piano while a bigger one knocks her fingers at the
other.
“Just listen how prettily your little cousin
plays,” says Mother. “ Wouldn’t you like to be
as clever as that?” The small child looks from
one player to the other. “I don’t think much of
it,” she says contemptuously, “ she has to have
someone to help her all the time,” and with an
impatient wriggle she escapes and flies to her
beloved potato-roasting again.
• , * * •
And now there is the indescribable scent
of wild, silent bush around us, with its tall, rung
trees gleaming white above the dark bracken.
There is an awful desolation in it all, for a small
child crouches heart-broken beside a fallen log
and I can hear her sobs across the width of
years. For they have told her that the girl-
mother, who lay so still and was so cold to
kiss, has gone away and will never come
back again. And so, in her utter loneliness, she
sobs herself to sleep, and then wakes to see a real,
live wallaby quite close to her, and leaps to her
feet to chase it with excited cries. Then the
verandah door opens and someone calls her in, and
she sees that they are all still crying, and she
twists her little handkerchief into a ball and tries
to rub a tear into the comer of her eye.
* * * *
“ Woa, then ; steady there !” We pull up
with a jerk, and a boy throws open the white gate
and runs to take the horses. We are home at last,
and I am cramped and dazzled by the sudden light
as Graham lifts me out. “Where have your
thoughts been all this time?” he asks ; “ it’s an
hour since you spoke. Are you very tired?”
“No, I am not very tired,” I tell him ; and I try
to collect my thoughts, that have been s > far away
with a little child who is not now, but who was,
twenty long years ago.
MAMOOSE.
Lost and Given Over.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A mermaid’s not a human thing,
An’ courtin’ sich is folly ;
Of flesh an’ blood I’d ruther sing
What ain’t so mel-an-choly.
’Ere’s Berta, Loo, Juanita, Sue,
A long good-luck to me an’ you—
Sing rally, ri-a-rally !
The seas is deep, the seas is wide.
But this I’ll prove, what else beside,
I’m bully in-the-alley,
I’m bul-lee, in our alley.
The Hoogli gal ’er face is brown,
The Heelo gal is lazy ;
The gal that lives by ’Obart Town
’U’d drive a dead man crazy.
Come wet your lip, an’ let it slip,
The Gretna Green’s a tidy ship,
Sing rally !
The seas is deep, the seas is blue,
But ’ere’s good ’ealth to me an’ you,
Ho, rally!
The Lord may drop us off our pins
To feed ’is bloomin’ fishes ;
But, Lord, forgive us all our sins 1
Our sins is most delicious.
Come drink it up, an fill yer cup,
The World it owes us bite an’ sup—
’Ere’s Mi mi, Ju-Ju, Sally—
The seas is long, the winds is strong,
The best of men they will go wrong,
Hi, rally, ri-a-rally !
The Bowery gal she knows ’er know,
The Sydney gal is silly,
The Hayti gal ain’t white as snow,
They’re whiter down in Chili.
Now, what’s the use to shun the booze,
They’ll flop your bones among the ooze,
Sou’-west by south-the-galley,
The seas is green, the seas is cold,
The best of men they must grow old,
Sing rally, re-a-rally !
All round the world “ where’er I roam ”
This lesson I am learnin’ —
If you’ve got sense you’ll stop at ’ome
An’ save the bit you’re earnin’.
But damn the odds! it’s little odds,
When every ’eathen ’as ’is gods
An’ neither two will tally.
When Black and White drink, woman, fight.
In them three things they’re all all-right,
Sing rally, re-a-rally !.
When double bunks, fo’castle end,
Is all the kind that’s carried,
Our manners they will likely mend—
Most likely we’ll get married.
But till sich time as that is done
We’ll take our fun where we begun—
Sing rally !
The flesh is weak, the world is wide,
The Dead Man ’ee goes over-side—
Sing rally, rally !
It’s Tokio town when the sun goes down,
It’s ’arf-a-pint an’ it’s ’arf-a-crown —
Sing rally !
’Er spars may lift an’ ’or keel may shift,
When a man is done ’e’s got to drift —
Ho, rally !
We’re given an’ lost to the gals that wait
From Trinity to Whitsunday—
From Sunda Strait to the Golden Gate,
An’ back to the Bay o’ Fundy.
Oh, it’s Mabel Loo, an’ it’s Nancy-poo !
So ’ere’s good luck, an’ 1 love you—
Sing rally !
It’s cents an’ dollars, an’ somebody hollers,
The sun comes up an’ the morn in’ follers —
Ho, rally, rally!
The Hoogli gal ’er face is brown,
The Heelo gal’s a daisy ;
The gal that lives by ’Obart Town
She'd drive a dead man crazy.
So pretty an’ plain, it’s Sarah Jane
’Uggin’ an’ kissin’ an’ come again—
Sing rally,ri-a-rally!
The seas is deep, the seas is wide.
But this I’ll prove, what else beside —
I’m bully in the alley,
Ho ! Bul-lee in the alley !
Sydney, 1897.
E. J. BRADY.
Among the celebrities who have died re-
cently was a long-lived ewe belonging to farmer
Hayward, of Oakwood farm, Marshbrook, Eng.
She was 20 years old last lambing-time, had reared
33 lambs successfully, and had never had an
attack of footrot. In America a ewe has been
known to live to a quarter of a century, and back-
country Australian merinos often cross the score.
The latest coir in-the-slot marvel is the
public-telephone invented by Dr. Clendinnen of
Victoria, a contrivance which actually returns the
fee if the exchange does not succeed in placing
the caller in communication with the desired
person, but promptly impounds it as soon as the
hanging-up of the transmitter indicates the com-
pletion of the conversation.
A Melbourne man, charged recently with
“ embezzlement ” of fifty shillings received by
him as agent, had to undergo three adjournments
of his case because, as the Bench was informed by
his solicitor, “counsel was otherwise engaged.”
When he was committed for trial he changed his
solicitor, and the old one refused to hand over
documents until a little bill of illo2 costs was
paid in full. In the higher court the Crown
promptly entered a nolle prosequi, and the accused
sees too late that he could have got the charge
blown up in the police-court for five guineas if he
had gone the right way about it. Also, he hears,
on unreliable authority, that at No. 1 adjourn-
ment his counsel was at the pigeon-shooting
match ; at No. 2 he was at a big cricket-match ;
and at No. 3 he was helping his wife to brtjr a
wedding-present for a friend. He is of opinion
that only a massive gold frame would be a worthy
setting for that nolle.
